The Status of Banu Taghlib: Jizya by Essence, Sadaqa by Name
Correction and clarification of Banu Taghlib's status and the legal nature of their payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the double payment of Banu Taghlib as jizya and not sadaqa, and identify the proof from the practice of 'Umar.

Core Idea
A tax's legal nature is determined by its essence, not its name, as established by 'Umar's pact with Banu Taghlib.

This passage corrects a common error about Banu Taghlib and clarifies a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the legal classification of payments from non-Muslims. While 'Umar ibn al-Khattab allowed Banu Taghlib to call their payment 'sadaqa', he explicitly stated it is their jizya. The Hanafi school relies on this precedent to rule that the double payment is essentially jizya, with all its legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Correct the Identity
Identify that Banu Taghlib were Christian Arabs, not polytheists, as the commentator initially claimed. Understand why this distinction matters for jizya rulings.
Jizya is imposed on People of the Book, not polytheists.
2
Examine the Pact with 'Umar
Study the narrations of Ibn Abi Shaybah, 'Abd al-Razzaq, and Abu 'Ubayd that recount 'Umar's agreement with Banu Taghlib. Note the key elements: they refused jizya, offered double sadaqa, and the Companions consented.
The pact occurred in the presence of the Companions and was effectively a consensus.
3
Determine the Legal Nature
'Umar explicitly said 'This is your jizya; call it jizya or sadaqa.' This indicates the payment is jizya in essence. The Hanafi school uses this to rule that regardless of naming, the tax remains jizya.
The ruling is based on the authority of the Caliph and Companion consensus.
4
Apply the Hanafi Ruling
Conclude that Banu Taghlib's payment is legally jizya, which affects its distribution (to public treasury) and other ahkam. This sets a precedent for similar cases.
If considered sadaqa, it would be distributed as zakat; as jizya, it goes to the general treasury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قوم من مشركي العرب؛ هذا خطأ من الشارح لله، والصحيح أنهم قوم من نصارى العرب، وقد روى قصة صلح له معهم ابن أبي شيبة، وعبد الرزاق، وأبو عبيد القاسم بن سلام في كتاب «الأموال» وغيرهم. وخلاصة رواياتهم: أن عمر لما وضع الجزية على النصارى قصد أن يضعها على بني تغلب وهم قوم من العرب تنصروا وأنفوا من أداء الجزية وقالوا: نعطى الصدقات المقررة على أهل الإسلام ضعف ما يعطونه، فصالحهم على ذلك بمحضر من الصحابة له، وأجمعوا على ذلك، وقال عمر: هذه جزيتكم فسموها جزية أو صدقة.
His statement: 'A people from the polytheist Arabs' — this is an error from the commentator, may Allah forgive him. The correct [view] is that they are a people from the Christian Arabs. The story of the peace treaty between him ('Umar) and them was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah, 'Abd al-Razzaq, Abu 'Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam in the book 'al-Amwal' and others. The summary of their narrations: When 'Umar imposed the jizya on the Christians, he intended to impose it on Banu Taghlib, and they were a people from the Arabs who had become Christians and were too proud to pay jizya. They said: 'We will give the prescribed sadaqat of the people of Islam double what they give.' So he made peace with them on that basis in the presence of the Companions, and they all agreed. 'Umar said: 'This is your jizya; call it jizya or sadaqa.'
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
The passage is a correction and clarification of the legal status of Banu Taghlib regarding zakat and jizya. The commentator initially stated that Banu Taghlib were polytheist Arabs, but the correct view is that they were Christian Arabs. The ruling here is established through the practice of Caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) and the consensus of the Companions. Banu Taghlib refused to pay jizya because they considered it humiliating for their Arab pride. Instead, they proposed to pay double the sadaqa (zakat) that Muslims pay. 'Umar agreed to this, and the Companions concurred. 'Umar explicitly stated that this payment is effectively their jizya, and they could call it either jizya or sadaqa. This incident demonstrates the flexibility and pragmatism of early Islamic law in dealing with non-Muslim communities, and the Hanafi school relies on this precedent for rulings on jizya from Arab Christians.

Key Terms
الجزية (al-jizyah): The tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
الصدقة (al-sadaqah): Here it refers to the obligatory charity (zakat) on Muslims, but used in the pact with Banu Taghlib as a name for the double payment.
بني تغلب (Banu Taghlib): A Christian Arab tribe that made a pact with 'Umar to pay double sadaqa instead of jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what was the nature of the double payment made by Banu Taghlib?
A) It was a voluntary charity (sadaqah)
B) It was essentially jizya, as stated by 'Umar
C) It was a new type of tax
D) It was a penalty for not converting

Key Takeaway
The essence of a tax determines its legal classification, not the name used in a pact, as established by 'Umar's ruling on Banu Taghlib.

Permissibility of Paying Zakat Before the Year
Ruling on paying zakat early before the year completes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which early zakat payment is valid per the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Early zakat is permissible only if you own nisab at the time of payment.

Zakat is normally due after one lunar year of possessing nisab. But can you pay it early? The Hanafi school allows it, but with a crucial condition: you must own a nisab at the moment of payment. This lesson explains the ruling and its conditions from the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Paying zakat before the end of the year. The ruling is ja'iz (permissible).
Ja'iz here means valid and acceptable, not obligatory.
2
List the conditions for validity
According to the text, the key condition is that the payer must own a nisab at the time of payment. What does this mean exactly?
Ownership of nisab at the time of payment substitutes for the condition of having held it for a full year.
3
Determine the consequences of fulfilling the condition
If early payment is made while owning nisab, what happens after the year? No additional zakat is due for that wealth, even if the year completes and nisab is still owned.
The early payment settles the obligation for that period.
4
Identify what invalidates early payment
If the payer does not own a nisab at the time of payment, the early zakat is invalid and must be repeated.
This is the main invalidating factor (mani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجازَ تقديمها لحول، ولأكثر منه ، ولِنُصب لذي نصاب (۳) قوله : وجاز ؛ أي يجوز أن يؤدي زكاة مال قبل حولان الحول، وكذا يجوز تقديم زكاة الحولين فصاعداً، وكذا يجوز أداء زكاة نصب قبل أن يملكها، ويشترط في ذلك أن يكون عند الأداء، مالكاً لنصاب ، فعند ذلك لا يثنى بعد الحول، ولا جميع بعد ملکه نصاب آخر إن أدى ، كأنه قبله.
And it is permissible to pay it (zakat) early before the year, and for more than a year, and for a nisab one does not yet possess? (wording unclear). (3) He said: 'and it is permissible' – meaning it is permissible for a person to pay zakat on wealth before the year has passed, and likewise it is permissible to pay zakat for two years or more in advance, and likewise it is permissible to pay zakat on a nisab before owning it, provided that at the time of payment he owns a nisab. In that case, no (obligation) remains after the year, nor anything after he owns another nisab if he paid, as if it were before.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the permissibility of paying zakat early. The matn states that early payment is allowed for the current year, for multiple years, and even for a nisab one does not yet own (i.e., before the wealth reaches nisab). The commentary explains that one may pay zakat before the year ends, for two or more years in advance, and even before possessing the nisab, but with a crucial condition: the payer must own a nisab at the time of payment. If he does so, then after the year passes, no further zakat is due on that wealth, and if he later acquires another nisab, no duplicate payment is required. This is because the early payment suffices for the future obligation.

Key Terms
تقديم الزكاة (taqdeem al-zakat): Paying zakat before the completion of the lunar year.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum amount of wealth that triggers zakat obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wealth must have reached nisab at the end of the year
B) The payer must own a nisab at the time of payment
C) The payer must be wealthy
D) The early payment must be for at least two years

Key Takeaway
Early zakat payment is valid in the Hanafi school only if the payer owns a nisab at the moment of payment, and it suffices for the future obligation.

Advancing Zakat Payment Before the Year
Commentary on the ruling and evidence for advancing Zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on advancing Zakat payment, identify the condition of owning nisab at the time of payment, and explain the legal distinction between the cause of obligation (sabab) and the condition of payment (shart).

Core Idea
Advancing Zakat is valid because the cause (nisab) exists; the year is only a condition for payment, not for obligation.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains that it is permissible to pay Zakat before the completion of the lunar year. The author clarifies the legal principle: growing wealth (nisab) is the cause of obligation, while the passing of the year is a condition for payment. Therefore, if one owns the nisab at the time of payment, the advance is valid and fulfills the obligation once the year completes. The proof is the hadith where the Prophet allowed his uncle al-Abbas to pay two years' Zakat in advance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is advancing Zakat payment before the end of the lunar year. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) if a condition is met.
The base text says 'وجاز تقديمها' - 'and it is permissible to give it before a year'.
2
Locate the condition
Identify the condition for validity: the payer must own a nisab (minimum threshold of growing wealth) at the time of payment.
The text states: 'ويشترط في ذلك أن يكون عند الأداء، مالكاً لنصاب'.
3
Understand the legal principle
Grasp the distinction between the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) and the condition for payment (shart wujub al-ada'). The nisab is the cause; the year is the condition. Since the cause exists, the obligation in the dhimmah is established, allowing payment before the condition is fulfilled.
The text says: 'المال النامي سبب لوجوب الزكاة، والحلول شرط لوجوب الأداء'.
4
Analyze the proof
The hadith of al-Abbas is the evidence. The Prophet permitted him to advance sadaqah (Zakat) for two years. This shows the permissibility of early payment.
The hadith is narrated by al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, al-Tirmidhi, and Abu Dawud.
5
Apply the ruling to edge cases
Consider cases: if the payer does not own a nisab at payment, the advance is invalid. If he owns one nisab and pays for more than one nisab, it suffices if he later acquires the additional wealth.
The text discusses: 'إذا لم يملك نصاباً أصلاً لم يصح الأداء' and 'إذا كان له نصاب واحد ... فيؤدي لأكثر من نصاب واحد ... أجزأه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة زكاة الأموال
وجازَ تقديمها لحول، ولأكثر منه ، ولِنُصب لذي نصاب
فلمَّا جَرَى الصُّلْحُ على ضعف زكاة المسلمين، لا تؤخذ من صبيانهم، ولكن تؤخذ من نسائهم كالمسلمين مع أن الجزية لا توضع على النساء".
عمر
( وجاز" تقديمها لحول ، ولأكثر منه ، ولنصب الذي نصاب فقال ه : إنا لا نأخذ مشرك صدقة، فلحق بعضهم بالرُّوم، فقال النعمان من ه : يا أمير المؤمنين ; إنَّ القوم لهم بأس شديد فخذ منهم الجزية باسم الصدقة، فبعث عمر له في طلبهم، وضعف عليهم ، وأجمع الصحابة لله على ذلك». انتهى [١] قوله : كالمسلمين ; فإنه لا تؤخذ الزَّكاةُ من صبيانهم، وتؤخذ من نسائهم ورجالهم. [٢]قوله : لا توضع على النِّساء ; قال في خزانة المفتين»: الجزية ضربان : جزية توضع بالتراضي والصلح ، فيقدر بحسب ما يقع عليه الاتفاق، ولا تغير. وجزية يبتدأ وضعها إذا غلب الإمام على الكفار وأقرهم على أملاكهم، فيضعُ على الغني الظاهر الغنى في كل سنة ثمانية وأربعين درهماً، يأخذ منه في كل شهر أربعة دراهم، وعلى وسط الحالِ أربعة وعشرين في كلِّ شهر در همين، والغنى وعدمُهُ يعرفُ بمعتاد كل بلدة، هو الصحيح، وعلى كل فقير يكسب اثني عشر درهماً في كلِّ شهر
درهماً.
وتوضع الجزية على أهل الكتاب والمجوس والوثني من العجم، ولا توضع على عبدة الأوثان من العرب، ولا المرتدين ولا يقبلُ منهما إلا الإسلام أو السيف، ولا جزية على راهب لا يخالط وصبي وامرأة مملوكة وأعمى، وزمن، وفقير لا يكسب، ولا على مكاتب ومدبر، وأم ولد.
(۳) قوله : وجاز ; أي يجوز أن يؤدي زكاة مال قبل حولان الحول، وكذا يجوز تقديم زكاة الحولين فصاعداً، وكذا يجوز أداء زكاة نصب قبل أن يملكها، ويشترط في ذلك أن يكون عند الأداء، مالكاً لنصاب ، فعند ذلك لا يثنى بعد الحول، ولا جميع بعد ملکه نصاب آخر إن أدى ، كأنه قبله.
والأصل في هذا أن المال النامي سبب لوجوب الزكاة، والحلول شرط لوجوب الأداء، فإذا وُجِدَ السَّبب يصحُ الأداء مع أنه لم يجب"، فإذا وجد النصاب يصح الأداء قبل الحول، وكذا إذا كان له نصاب واحد كمئتي درهم مثلاً، فيؤدي لأكثر من نصاب واحد، حتَّى إذا مَلَكَ الأكثر بعد الأداء أجزأه ما ادى من قبل ، أما إذا لم يملك نصاباً أصلاً لم يصح الأداء.
والأصل فيه ما أخرجه البزار والطبراني : أنه تعجل من عمه العباس صدقة سنتين)، وفي رواية الترمذي وأبي داود : «إنَّ العباس له سأله عن تعجيل الصدقة، فرخص له في ذلك»(٢).
[1]قوله : الأصل في هذا ... الخ ; حاصله : إنَّ هاهنا أمرين :
أحدهما نفس الوجوب، وهو كونُ الشَّيء واجباً في الذمة، وكونها غير فارغ عنه إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء.
وثانيهما : وجوب الأداء وسبب نفس الوجوب هو المالُ النَّامي بقيودِهِ المذكورة سابقاً، فإذا وجد ذلك اشتغلت ذمة المالك بالزَّكاة ووجبت عليه. ووجوب الأداء إنما يتحقق بحولان الحول، فصحة الأداء متفرعة على وجوب ذلك الشيء في نفسه، فإذا وُجِدَ سببُ الوجوب صح الأداء، وإن لم يجب بعد، بخلاف ما لم یکن عنده نصاب مطلقاً، فإنها لم تجب حينئذ عليه مطلقاً، فلا يصح أداؤها مقدما.
(٢) قوله : مع أنه لم يجب ; قد يستشكل ظاهره بأنه لما وُجِدَ سبب الوجوب وجبت لا محالة، وإلا لزم الفصل بين سبب الوجوب والوجوب، فكيف يصح قوله : مع أنه لم يجب». ويجاب عنه : بأن الضمير راجع إلى الأداء، والغرض منه نفي وجوب الأداء لا نفي أصل الوجوب، والحاصل إن تحقق سبب الوجوب يجب الشيء في الذمة، فإذا وجد المال النصاب وجبت في الذمة، وتعلقت بالزَّكاة، وأما وجوب الأداء الموقوف على مطالبة الشارع فهو إنّما يتعلّق بعد حولان الحول.
Book of Zakat, Zakat on wealth. And it is permissible to give it before a year, and for more than a year, and for a nisab for one who owns a nisab. When the treaty (sulh) was made for half the zakat of Muslims, it is not taken from their children, but it is taken from their women like Muslims, even though jizya is not placed on women. 'Umar said: We do not take charity from a polytheist. Some of them joined the Romans, so al-Nu'man said to him: O Commander of the Faithful, these people have severe strength, so take jizya from them in the name of sadaqah. So 'Umar sent for them and made it incumbent upon them, and the Companions agreed on that. End. His statement: 'like Muslims' - indeed zakat is not taken from their children, and it is taken from their women and men. His statement: 'not placed on women' - said in Khizanat al-Muftin: Jizya is of two types: jizya placed by mutual consent and treaty, then it is estimated according to what is agreed upon, and is not changed. And jizya initiated when the imam dominates the disbelievers and allows them to keep their property, then he imposes upon the apparently wealthy forty-eight dirhams per year, taking four dirhams each month; upon the middle class twenty-four dirhams, in each month two dirhams; richness and lack thereof is known by the custom of each town, that is the correct view; upon every poor earner twelve dirhams per year, one dirham per month. And jizya is imposed upon the People of the Book, Magians, and polytheists from non-Arabs, and is not imposed upon idol worshippers from Arabs, nor upon apostates; from them only Islam or the sword is accepted. There is no jizya upon a monk who does not mix, a child, a female slave, a blind person, a chronically ill person, a poor person who does not earn, nor upon a mukatib, mudabbar, or umm walad. (3) His statement: 'and it is permissible' - meaning it is permissible to give zakat of wealth before the year's completion, and likewise it is permissible to give zakat of two years or more in advance, and likewise it is permissible to give zakat of a nisab before owning it, with the condition that at the time of giving, one is the owner of a nisab. Then he is not obliged after the year, nor after he owns another nisab if he gave, as if he gave before. The principle in this is that growing wealth is the cause (sabab) for the obligation of zakat, and the completion of the year is a condition for the obligation of payment. So when the cause exists, payment is valid even though it has not become obligatory. So when the nisab exists, payment is valid before the year. Likewise, if one has a single nisab, e.g. 200 dirhams, and gives for more than one nisab, then if he later owns the additional amount after the payment, what he gave earlier suffices. However, if he did not own a nisab at all, the payment is not valid. The basis of this is what al-Bazzar and al-Tabarani narrated: that he (the Prophet) advanced from his uncle al-Abbas the sadaqah of two years. In the narration of al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud: al-Abbas asked him about advancing sadaqah, so he permitted him to do that. (1) His statement: 'The principle in this...' the summary is that there are two matters here: one is the actual obligation, which is that something is obligatory in the dhimmah and not free from it except by payment or release. The second is the obligation of payment. The cause of the actual obligation is the growing wealth with its conditions mentioned previously. So when that exists, the dhimmah of the owner becomes occupied with zakat and it becomes obligatory upon him. The obligation of payment is only realized by the passing of the year. So the validity of payment is derived from the existence of that thing in itself. So when the cause of obligation exists, payment is valid even if it has not yet become obligatory. Contrary to when one does not have a nisab at all, then it has not become obligatory upon him at all, so advancing it is not valid. (2) His statement: 'even though it has not become obligatory' - one might object that when the cause of obligation exists, it necessarily becomes obligatory, otherwise there would be a separation between cause and effect. How can he say 'even though it has not become obligatory'? The answer is that the pronoun refers to payment, and the intent is to negate the obligation of payment, not the actual obligation. The gist is that when the cause of obligation is realized, the thing becomes obligatory in the dhimmah. So when the nisab wealth exists, it becomes obligatory in the dhimmah and relates to zakat. As for the obligation of payment, which depends on the Legislator's demand, it is only after the passage of the year.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that it is permissible to advance Zakat payment before the completion of the Zakat year. The author first states the ruling in the matn (base text), then expands on it with evidence and reasoning. Initially, there is a historical digression about how Umar treated a Christian tribe who refused to pay Zakat, eventually imposing jizya on them. This is used to illustrate that Zakat is not taken from non-Muslims, but jizya is, and that Zakat is not taken from children even among Muslims. The main ruling is explained thereafter: advancing Zakat is valid if the payer owns the nisab at the time of payment. The legal principle is that the growth of wealth (the nisab) is the cause (sabab) for the obligation of Zakat in the dhimmah, while the passing of the year is a condition for the obligation to actually pay. Therefore, if the cause exists (possessing nisab), payment can be made before the condition (year's end) is fulfilled. The proof is the hadith of al-Abbas, where the Prophet permitted him to pay Zakat two years in advance. The commentary also addresses a potential objection: how can payment be valid when the obligation hasn't yet arisen? The distinction is made between the obligation in the dhimmah (which exists as soon as the nisab is possessed, though it is not due yet) and the obligation to pay (which only arises after the year). Thus, advancing Zakat is valid as long as the nisab is owned at the time of payment.

Key Terms
تقديم الزكاة (taqdīm al-zakāh): Paying Zakat before the completion of the lunar year.
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujūb): The cause that brings about the obligation in the dhimmah, which is possession of nisab of growing wealth.
شرط وجوب الأداء (sharṭ wujūb al-adā'): The condition for the obligation to pay, which is the passing of one lunar year.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum amount of wealth that makes Zakat obligatory, e.g., 200 dirhams of silver or 20 mithqals of gold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for advancing Zakat payment before the year according to this passage?
A) The payer must have a surplus of wealth beyond the nisab.
B) The payer must own a nisab at the time of payment.
C) The payer must have intent to pay for multiple years.
D) The payer must obtain permission from the ruler.

Key Takeaway
Advancing Zakat is valid in the Hanafi school if the payer owns the nisab at payment, based on the hadith of al-Abbas and the legal distinction between cause (sabab) and condition (shart).

Paying Zakat Before the Year's Completion (Ta'jil al-Zakah)
Ruling on permissibility of paying zakat before the year ends with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paying zakat before the year's completion and explain the distinction between the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) and the condition for payment (shart al-ada).

Core Idea
Paying zakat before the year is valid because the cause exists, even if the payment condition is unmet.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: zakat becomes due in the dhimmah as soon as productive wealth reaches the nisab, but the obligation to pay only matures after the year. This distinction allows early payment, as the Prophet permitted al-Abbas to advance his zakat. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the cause and condition
Identify sabab al-wujub (cause of obligation: productive wealth reaching nisab) and shart al-ada (condition for payment: completion of the year).
The cause triggers the obligation itself; the condition triggers the duty to pay.
2
Apply to ta'jil
Understand that early payment is valid because the cause exists, even though the condition for payment is not yet met.
The existence of the cause makes the payment count toward the eventual obligation.
3
Analyse the hadith
Examine the Prophet's permission to al-Abbas to pay zakat in advance for two years. This hadith directly supports the permissibility.
The hadith is narrated by al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, al-Tirmidhi, and Abu Dawud.
4
Address the counter-argument
Resolve the apparent contradiction: the phrase 'even though it is not obligatory' refers to the obligation to pay, not the obligation itself. The underlying obligation exists from the moment of nisab.
The commentary clarifies that this is a semantic nuance, not a contradiction in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا أن المال النامي سبب لوجوب الزكاة، والحلول شرط لوجوب الأداء، فإذا وُجِدَ السَّبب يصحُ الأداء مع أنه لم يجب، فإذا وجد النصاب يصح الأداء قبل الحول، وكذا إذا كان له نصاب واحد كمئتي درهم مثلاً، فيؤدي لأكثر من نصاب واحد، حتَّى إذا مَلَكَ الأكثر بعد الأداء أجزأه ما ادى من قبل، أما إذا لم يملك نصاباً أصلاً لم يصح الأداء. والأصل فيه ما أخرجه البزار والطبراني : أنه تعجل من عمه العباس صدقة سنتين، وفي رواية الترمذي وأبي داود : «إنَّ العباس له سأله عن تعجيل الصدقة، فرخص له في ذلك». [1]قوله : الأصل في هذا ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ هاهنا أمرين : أحدهما نفس الوجوب، وهو كونُ الشَّيء واجباً في الذمة، وكونها غير فارغ عنه إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء. وثانيهما : وجوب الأداء وسبب نفس الوجوب هو المالُ النَّامي بقيودِهِ المذكورة سابقاً، فإذا وجد ذلك اشتغلت ذمة المالك بالزَّكاة ووجبت عليه. ووجوب الأداء إنما يتحقق بحولان الحول، فصحة الأداء متفرعة على وجوب ذلك الشيء في نفسه، فإذا وُجِدَ سببُ الوجوب صح الأداء، وإن لم يجب بعد، بخلاف ما لم یکن عنده نصاب مطلقاً، فإنها لم تجب حينئذ عليه مطلقاً، فلا يصح أداؤها مقدما. (٢) قوله : مع أنه لم يجب ; قد يستشكل ظاهره بأنه لما وُجِدَ سبب الوجوب وجبت لا محالة، وإلا لزم الفصل بين سبب الوجوب والوجوب، فكيف يصح قوله : مع أنه لم يجب». ويجاب عنه : بأن الضمير راجع إلى الأداء، والغرض منه نفي وجوب الأداء لا نفي أصل الوجوب، والحاصل إن تحقق سبب الوجوب يجب الشيء في الذمة، فإذا وجد المال النصاب وجبت في الذمة، وتعلقت بالزَّكاة، وأما وجوب الأداء الموقوف على مطالبة الشارع فهو إنّما يتعلّق بعد حولان الحول.
The basis for this is that productive wealth is the cause for the obligation of zakat, while the completion of the year is a condition for the obligation of payment. So when the cause exists, payment is valid even though it is not yet obligatory. Thus, when one possesses the nisab, payment before the year is valid. Similarly, if one has a single nisab, e.g., two hundred dirhams, and pays for more than one nisab, then if he acquires the additional amount after payment, what he paid earlier suffices for him. But if he did not possess any nisab at all initially, payment is not valid. The basis for this is what al-Bazzar and al-Tabarani narrated: that he [the Prophet] collected in advance from his uncle al-Abbas the sadaqah for two years. In the narration of al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud: 'Indeed al-Abbas asked him about advancing the sadaqah, and he permitted him to do so.' [1] His saying: 'The basis for this...' etc.; its summary: There are two matters here: One is the obligation itself, which is that something is owed in the dhimmah and is not discharged except by payment or release. The other is the obligation to pay. The cause of the obligation itself is productive wealth with its previously mentioned conditions. So when that exists, the owner's dhimmah becomes occupied with zakat and it is obligatory upon him. The obligation to pay only materializes with the passing of the year. The validity of payment is derivative upon the obligation of that thing in itself. So when the cause of obligation exists, payment is valid even if it is not yet obligatory, unlike if he has no nisab at all, for then it is not obligatory upon him at all, so payment in advance is not valid. (2) His saying: 'even though it is not obligatory' — its apparent meaning may be problematic because when the cause of obligation exists, it necessarily becomes obligatory, otherwise there would be a separation between the cause of obligation and the obligation. So how can he say 'even though it is not obligatory'? The answer is that the pronoun refers to the obligation to pay, and the intent is to negate the obligation to pay, not the obligation itself. The upshot is that when the cause of obligation is realized, the thing becomes due in the dhimmah. So when one possesses the nisab wealth, it becomes due in the dhimmah and attaches to zakat. As for the obligation to pay, which is contingent upon the demand of the Lawgiver, it only attaches after the passing of the year.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on paying zakat before the completion of the year (ta'jil al-zakah). The author begins by stating the foundational principle: the cause (sabab) for zakat obligation is productive wealth (mal nam) that reaches the nisab and is free of debt, etc. The condition for the obligation to pay (wujub al-ada) is the passing of the year (hawl). However, the validity of payment is linked to the existence of the cause, not the condition. Therefore, if one possesses the nisab, he may pay zakat before the year ends, and it will be valid and count towards his obligation. If he later acquires additional wealth, the earlier payment covers it as well. But if he has no nisab at all, payment is invalid. The author then provides a proof from hadith: the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed al-Abbas to pay zakat in advance for two years. The commentary clarifies a potential confusion: the phrase 'even though it is not obligatory' refers to the obligation to pay (wujub al-ada), not the obligation itself (wujub al-asl). The obligation itself is established as soon as the cause exists, but the obligation to pay is conditional on the year's completion. Thus, payment before the year is valid because the cause exists, even though the payment obligation has not yet matured.

Key Terms
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause that triggers the obligation of zakat, which is productive wealth reaching the nisab and other conditions.
شرط الأداء (shart al-ada): The condition for the obligation to pay, which is the completion of the year.
تعجيل الزكاة (ta'jil al-zakah): Paying zakat before the year's completion after possessing the nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The cause of obligation exists, even though the payment condition is not met.
C) The year is not required for any form of zakat.
D) The Shafi'i school also validates it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits early zakat payment based on the existence of the cause of obligation, supported by the hadith of al-Abbas.

The Nisab of Gold and Silver in Zakat
Definition of nisab and weight units for zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab thresholds for gold and silver, explain the definition of the mithqal, and identify the hadith evidence for these rulings.

Core Idea
Zakat becomes obligatory on gold at 20 mithqals and on silver at 200 dirhams.

This lesson comes from the chapter on zakat on wealth. The passage establishes the minimum thresholds (nisab) for zakat on gold and silver, which are foundational for calculating zakat. It also provides a detailed weight breakdown of the mithqal, showing how classical scholars defined this unit. We will examine the ruling, the evidence, and the technical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab amounts
Read the first line: the nisab for gold is twenty mithqals (also called dinars) and for silver is two hundred dirhams. These are fixed thresholds.
Both amounts are agreed upon across all madhhabs.
2
Decode the definition of the mithqal
The passage gives a detailed hierarchical breakdown of the mithqal in terms of smaller weight units (daniq, taswij, habba, sha'ira, etc.). Note the two conventions: 96 sha'irat (Samarqand) vs 100 sha'irat (Herat / legal convention).
The Hanafi school adopts the 100-sha'ira convention.
3
Locate the hadith evidence
Two hadith are cited: one from 'Ali (ra) explicitly stating both nisabs and zakat rates, and another from the Prophet (saw) regarding silver. Both are found in multiple hadith collections.
Although ahad, the wording is clear and unambiguous.
4
Apply the ruling to modern weights
Using the Hanafi convention (1 mithqal = 4.25g), 20 mithqals = 85g; 1 dirham = 2.975g, so 200 dirhams = 595g. This is the practical nisab today.
Always use the known Hanafi conversion for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة المال (وهو للذهب عشرون مثقالاً) [١]قوله : عشرون ; كون النصاب للذهب عشرين مثقالاً، وللفضة مئتي درهم، ثابت من فعل النبي )، وقوله : ورواياته مخرجة في «سنن ابن ماجه»، و«أبي داوود»، و«سنن الدارقطني»، والصحيحين، و(مسند أحمد»، و«جامع الترمذي»، و«سنن النسائي» وغيرها، في بعضها ذكر نصابها معاً، وفي بعضها اقتصر على أحدهما. [٢] قوله : مثقالاً ، هوا لغة : ما يوزن به، وشرعاً : اسم للمقدار المعين الذي يقدر به الذهب ونحوه، وهو الدينار واحد، إلا أنَّ الدينار اسم للقطعة المضروبة المقدرة بالمثقال. كذا في «فتح القدير». وفي «شرح المختصر للبرجندي»: في «الخزانة»: الدِّينار : وهو المثقال: ستة دوانق، والدائق أربع طسوجات، والطسوج حبتان، والحبة شعيرتان، والشعيرة ست خردلات، والخردلة اثنا عشر فلساً، والفلس ست فتيلات، والفتيل ست نقيرات ، والنّقيرةُ ثمان قطميرات والقطمير اثنا عشر ذرة، فعلى هذا يكون المثقال ستّاً وتسعين شعيرة، وهو المتعارف عند الحساب، وعليه أهل سمرقند. والمعهود عند أهل الشرع أن المثقال مئة شعيرة، وهو المتعارف في وزن أهل هراة في هذا الزمان، وعلى هذا الاصطلاح ذهب من قال : إن المثقال عشرون قيراطاً، وكل قیراط خمس شعيرات. انتهى. (۱) فعن علي له قال : فإذا كانت لك مائتا درهم وحال عليها الحول، ففيها خمسة دراهم، وليس عليك شيء يعني في الذهب حتى يكون لك عشرون ديناراً، فإذا كان لك عشرون دينارا، وحال عليها الحول ففيها نصف دينار في سنن أبي داود (۲): ۱۰۰)، وسكت عنه ، والأحاديث المختارة (۲) : (١٥٤) ، و (سنن البيهقي الكبير (٤ : ١٣٧)، وغيرها. وعن علي الله قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : قد عفوت عن صدقة الخيل والرقيق فهاتوا صدقة الرقة من كل أربعين درهماً درهماً، وليس في تسعين ومئة شيء، فإذا بلغت مئتين، ففيها خمسة الدراهم» في سنن الترمذي (۳): (١٦) ، وصححه، وسنن الدارمي (١ : ٤٦٧) ، و سنن أبي داود (۲) : (۱۰۱) ، و(مسند أحمد» (۱ : ۹۲)، وغيرها.
Chapter of Zakat on Wealth: (And for gold it is twenty mithqals) [1] His saying 'twenty': The fact that the nisab for gold is twenty mithqals and for silver two hundred dirhams is established from the action of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his statement. Its narrations are recorded in Sunan Ibn Majah, Sunan Abi Dawud, Sunan al-Daraqutni, the Two Sahihs, Musnad Ahmad, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan al-Nasa'i, and others. In some of them both nisabs are mentioned together, and in some only one of them is mentioned. [2] His saying 'mithqal': Linguistically, it is that by which one weighs. Legally, it is a name for the specific measure by which gold and the like are estimated, and it is one dinar, except that dinar is the name for the struck piece estimated by the mithqal. This is stated in Fath al-Qadir. In Sharh al-Mukhtasar li al-Barjandi: In al-Khazanah: The dinar is the mithqal: six daniqs, the daniq is four taswij, the taswij is two habbat, the habba is two sha'irat, the sha'ira is six khardalat, the khardala is twelve fulus, the fals is six fatilat, the fatila is six nqirat, the nqira is eight qatmirat, and the qatmir is twelve dharrah. Accordingly, the mithqal becomes ninety-six sha'irat, which is the conventional measure among accountants and the people of Samarqand. The customary measure among the people of sacred law is that the mithqal is one hundred sha'irat, which is the conventional weight of the people of Herat in this time. According to this convention, those who say the mithqal is twenty qirats, each qirat being five sha'irat, hold this view. End. (1) From 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: 'If you have two hundred dirhams and a year passes, then zakat on them is five dirhams. And you owe nothing on gold until you have twenty dinars; if you have twenty dinars and a year passes, then zakat on them is half a dinar' in Sunan Abi Dawud (2:100), and he is silent on it; and in al-Ahadith al-Mukhtarah (2:154), and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kubra (4:137), and others. And from 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'I have exempted you from sadaqah on horses and slaves; so give sadaqah on silver: from every forty dirhams one dirham; and there is nothing on one hundred and ninety (i.e., between 191 and 199), but when it reaches two hundred, then zakat is five dirhams' in Sunan al-Tirmidhi (3:16), and he authenticated it; and Sunan al-Darimi (1:467); and Sunan Abi Dawud (2:101); and Musnad Ahmad (1:92); and others.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is taken from the chapter on zakat on wealth. It begins by stating the nisab thresholds: gold has a nisab of twenty mithqals (which is equivalent to twenty dinars), and silver has a nisab of two hundred dirhams. The author then provides a detailed lexical and legal definition of the mithqal, citing classical works. The mithqal is defined linguistically as a weight, and legally as the standard measure for gold. It is also called a dinar, though technically the dinar is a coined piece. The commentary then lists the hierarchical breakdown of weights: a dinar/mithqal equals six daniqs, each daniq equals four taswij, each taswij equals two habbat, each habba equals two sha'irat, and so on down to the tiny qatmir and dharrah. According to one convention used by accountants in Samarqand, the mithqal is ninety-six sha'irat (barleycorns), while according to the sacred law and the people of Herat, it is one hundred sha'irat, which corresponds to twenty qirats, each qirat being five sha'irat. The author then presents two hadith proofs: one from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) stating the nisab and zakat rates for gold and silver, and another from the Prophet (peace be upon him) stating the nisab for silver and the zakat due. These hadith are recorded in multiple collections, establishing the nisab amounts definitively.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth subject to zakat; for gold it is 20 mithqals, for silver 200 dirhams.
مثقال (mithqal): The standard weight for gold; equivalent to one dinar. Defined in this text as 20 qirats or 100 sha'irat (barleycorns) in the legal convention, or 96 sha'irat in the Samarqand convention.
دينار (dinar): A gold coin weighing one mithqal; the nisab for gold is 20 dinars.

The nisab for zakat on gold is 20 mithqals (approx. 85g) and on silver is 200 dirhams (approx. 595g), established by clear hadith and weight conventions.

Standard Weight of Dirhams for Zakah
Explains the legal weight of dirhams for zakah nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal weight of a dirham in qirats, mithqals, and barley grains, and explain how this standard was established by Caliph Umar (may Allah be pleased with him).

Core Idea
The legal dirham weighs exactly 14 qirats (70 barley grains); ten dirhams equal seven mithqals—this is the authentic Hanafi standard for zakah.

This passage is a technical expansion (sharh) that clarifies the precise weight of the silver dirham used for determining the nisab of zakah. It traces the historical variation of dirham weights in early Islam and explains how Caliph Umar standardized them to resolve disputes. Understanding this weight is essential for correctly calculating whether one's savings exceed the nisab threshold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling statement
The matn states: the nisab of silver is 200 dirhams, with each ten dirhams weighing seven mithqals. This is the core legal definition.
2
Understand the historical context
Dirhams varied in weight during the Prophet's time and the first two caliphs: some were 20 qirats (like the dinar), some 12 qirats, some 10 qirats. This caused disputes in transactions.
3
Learn Umar's standardization
Umar (ra) took one dirham from each type, melted them together, and made three equal dirhams, each of 14 qirats. This became the standard.
4
Convert the ratios
One mithqal = 20 qirats; one dirham = 14 qirats. Therefore 10 dirhams = 140 qirats = 7 × 20 qirats = 7 mithqals.
5
Apply to zakah nisab
For 200 dirhams (nisab), total weight = 200 × 14 = 2800 qirats = 200 × 70 = 14000 barley grains. This is equivalent to 140 mithqals (since 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals → 200 dirhams = 140 mithqals).

And for silver, two hundred dirhams, every ten of them [equal to] seven mithqals. Know that this weight is called 'weight of seven', and it is that the dirham is seven parts out of the parts of which the mithqal is ten. And if you wish to verify the weight of the mithqal and dirham and others according to the custom of the people of our land, then refer to 'Kanz al-Hasanat fi I'ta' al-Zakah' by Mulla Muhammad Mu'in al-Laknawi, and the fatwas of his son Mawlana Muhammad Mu'in, and the place of elaboration on these topics is this place in 'al-Si'ayah', may Allah grant us its completion. [1] His saying: seven mithqals; al-Fakhr al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: 'That is, it is considered that the weight of every ten dirhams is the weight of seven mithqals, and the mithqal, which is the dinar, is twenty qirats, and the dirham is fourteen qirats, and the qirat is five barley grains. The basis of this is that dirhams were different in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and Abu Bakr and Umar. They had three categories: Some were twenty qirats, like the dinar. Some were twelve qirats: three-fifths of the dinar. Some were ten qirats: half the dinar. The first: weight of ten; i.e., ten of them weigh ten of the dinar. The second: weight of six; i.e., every ten of them weigh six of the dinar. The third: weight of five; i.e., every ten of them weigh five dinars. So conflict arose among people regarding payment and receipt, so Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) took one dirham from each type, mixed them, and made three equal dirhams, so each dirham came out as fourteen qirats, and the practice remained upon that until this day in everything.' End. [2] His saying: 'it is that...' etc.; i.e., the single dirham is the measure of seven parts out of the parts of which ten make a mithqal, so the weight of the dirham is half and a fifth of the weight of the mithqal; because seven is the sum of half of ten, i.e., five, and a fifth, i.e., two. So the dirham is half a mithqal and a fifth of a mithqal, so ten dirhams weigh seven mithqals. The mithqal is twenty qirats, the dirham fourteen qirats, and the qirat is five barley grains. If you take ten dirhams, their parts are seventy: each dirham seven parts, by multiplying seven by ten, and from them the mithqals are seven: each mithqal ten parts; therefore this weight is called the 'weight of seven'. [1] His saying: 'five barley grains'; so the weight of the dirham is seventy barley grains, because that is the result of multiplying five (the number of barley grains per qirat) by fourteen (the number of qirats of the dirham). And the number of qirats of ten dirhams is one hundred and forty, the result of multiplying ten by the number of qirats of the dirham, which is fourteen. And the number of barley grains of ten dirhams is seven hundred: the result of multiplying five (the number of barley grains per qirat) by one hundred and forty (the number of qirats). And the number of barley grains of the mithqal is one hundred: the result of multiplying five by twenty. And the number of qirats of seven mithqals is one hundred and forty, the result of multiplying seven by twenty (the number of qirats of a mithqal), and that is the measure of ten dirhams, so ten dirhams equal seven mithqals. And if you wish to know the measure of that according to the weights of our land: know that the well-known weight in our land is 'mahjah' and 'toljah' and 'al-toljah': it is called 'tolah' which is twelve mahjah, and it is called 'mashah'. And the mahjah: it is eight parts, each part is called in Persian 'sharkh', and in Hindi it is called 'rabbi' (with fatha on ra, kasra on ta with tashdid), and its famous name is 'kahankji' (with dhamma on Persian kaf, then ha, then nun, then sukun on Persian kaf, then kasra on Persian jim). And let us call it 'red'. And this part is the measure of four barley grains, so the mithqal, which is twenty barley grains, is twenty-five red parts, and it is three mahjah and one red. So the nisab of gold, which is twenty mithqals, is five toljah and two and a half mahjah, as is known by multiplying three mahjah and one red by twenty. This is for gold.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the legal weight standard for dirhams (silver coins) used in determining the nisab for zakah. The author begins by restating the rule from the matn: the nisab of silver is two hundred dirhams, and the standard is that ten dirhams must weigh seven mithqals (or dinars). This weight is known as 'weight of seven' (wazn sab'a). The author then explains that the dirham is seven-tenths of a mithqal. He directs readers to other works for local equivalents but proceeds to elaborate through a citation from al-Zayla'i's Sharh al-Kanz. Al-Zayla'i provides the historical background: in the time of the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and Umar, dirhams varied in weight—some were heavy (20 qirats, like the dinar), some medium (12 qirats), and some light (10 qirats). Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, resolved disputes by taking one dirham from each type, melting them together, and producing uniform dirhams of fourteen qirats each. This became the established standard. The author then confirms the arithmetic: since the mithqal is 20 qirats and the dirham is 14 qirats, the ratio is 14/20 = 7/10. Thus ten dirhams weigh 140 qirats, while seven mithqals weigh 140 qirats, confirming equality. He further breaks this down into barley grains (sha'irah): one qirat = 5 grains, so a dirham = 70 grains, a mithqal = 100 grains. Finally, he correlates these measures with the local Indian/Persian weights of his region (mahjah, toljah, etc.) to help readers apply the shariah standard practically. This passage is not a new ruling but an explanatory expansion (sharh) of the established rule, ensuring that the zakah obligation is correctly calculated based on the authentic weight standard.

Key Terms
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; the unit of currency used for zakah nisab; legally defined as weighing 14 qirats or 7/10 of a mithqal.
مثقال (mithqal): A gold coin (dinar); the unit of weight for gold; legally defined as 20 qirats or 100 barley grains.
قيراط (qirat): A unit of weight; 1/20 of a mithqal; equivalent to 5 barley grains.
شعيرة (sha'irah): A barley grain; the smallest unit of weight mentioned; used as a base to calculate coin weights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 50 grains
B) 70 grains
C) 100 grains
D) 140 grains

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal dirham weighs exactly 14 qirats (70 barley grains), and ten such dirhams weigh seven mithqals; this standard, established by Caliph Umar (ra), must be used for calculating the silver nisab for zakah.

Nisab of Gold and Silver by Weight
Definition and conversion of nisab weights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the standard nisab for gold and silver in Hanafi fiqh and calculate the gold nisab in terms of local Indian weight units (mahjah, tuljah, red parts).

Core Idea
Ten dirhams weigh seven mithqals; the nisab for gold is 20 mithqal, for silver 200 dirham.

Zakat becomes obligatory when a Muslim possesses wealth above a minimum threshold (nisab). The Hanafi school defines this threshold in terms of standard gold and silver weights: 20 mithqal of gold or 200 dirham of silver. However, actual weights vary by region, so scholars often convert these into local measures. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya does exactly that for the Indian subcontinent, explaining the arithmetic behind the standard 'weight of seven' and translating it into mahjah and tuljah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base units and their subdivisions
The passage defines the dirham, mithqal, qirat, and grain (sha'irah). Note: the mithqal = 20 qirats, the dirham = 14 qirats, and each qirat = 5 grains. This yields the dirham weight: 70 grains, and the mithqal weight: 100 grains.
The dirham is said to be half a mithqal plus a fifth, i.e., 0.5 + 0.2 = 0.7 mithqal, confirming the ratio 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals.
2
Understand the 'weight of seven'
The text explains that 10 dirhams equal 7 mithqals. This ratio is fundamental: it arises because each dirham is 7/10 of a mithqal. The phrase 'weight of seven' refers to this proportional equivalence.
The verse 'weight of seven' does not mean 7 mithqals for 10 dirhams; it means the weight of 10 dirhams is equal to 7 mithqals.
3
Calculate grain numbers for verification
The author multiplies to confirm: a dirham = 14 qirats × 5 grains = 70 grains; a mithqal = 20 qirats × 5 = 100 grains; 10 dirhams = 700 grains; 7 mithqals = 700 grains. Thus equal.
This arithmetic reinforces the reliability of the ratio.
4
Convert to local Indian weight units
The passage introduces the mahjah and tuljah. One tuljah = 12 mahjah. Each mahjah = 8 'red' parts. Each red part = 4 grains. Therefore, a mithqal (100 grains) = 100/4 = 25 red parts = 3 mahjah + 1 red.
The 'red' part is also called shirkh, rabbi, or kahankaji.
5
Determine the gold nisab in local units
Since gold nisab = 20 mithqals, and 1 mithqal = 3 mahjah + 1 red, multiply by 20: 20 × (3 mahjah + 1 red) = 60 mahjah + 20 red. Since 12 mahjah = 1 tuljah, 60 mahjah = 5 tuljah. So the nisab = 5 tuljah + 20 red, but 20 red = 20/8 mahjah = 2.5 mahjah (since 8 red = 1 mahjah). Thus final: 5 tuljah + 2.5 mahjah.
This is the specific value for the Indian subcontinent; other regions use different local conversions.

Source Text

Book of Zakat: Zakat on Wealth. That is, the dirham is half a mithqal and a fifth of a mithqal, so ten dirhams weigh seven mithqals. The mithqal is twenty qirats, the dirham is fourteen qirats, and the qirat is five grains. If you take ten dirhams, their parts become seventy: each dirham seven parts, by multiplying seven by ten; and from them, from the mithqals, seven: each mithqal ten parts; therefore this weight is called the weight of seven. His saying: five grains; so the weight of the dirham equals seventy grains because that is the product of multiplying five — the number of grains in each qirat — by fourteen, the number of qirats of the dirham, and the number of qirats in ten dirhams is one hundred and forty, obtained by multiplying ten by the number of qirats of the dirham, which is fourteen. And the number of grains in ten dirhams is seven hundred: the product of multiplying five, the number of grains per qirat, by one hundred and forty, the number of qirats; and the number of grains in a mithqal is one hundred: the product of multiplying five by twenty; and the number of qirats of seven mithqals, it is said: one hundred and forty, the product of multiplying seven by twenty, the number of qirats of the mithqal, and that is the amount of ten dirhams; thus ten dirhams equal seven mithqals. And if you wish to know the amount according to the weights of our land: know that the known weight in our land is the mahjah and tuljah, and the tuljah is called twelve mahjah, and it is also called mashah. The mahjah consists of eight parts, each part called in Persian: shirkh, and in Hindi: rabbi (with fath on ra and kasr on tashdid ta), and its well-known name is kahankaji (with damm on Persian kaf, then ha, then nun, then sukun on Persian kaf, then kasr on Persian jim), and let us call it 'red'. This part equals four grains; thus the mithqal, which is a certain grain, is twenty-five red parts, which is three mahjah and one red. So the nisab of gold, which is twenty mithqals, amounts to five tuljah and two and a half mahjah, as is known by multiplying three mahjah and one red by twenty. This for gold.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is an expansion on the sharh of al-Wiqaya, explaining the standard weights used to determine the nisab for zakat. The author begins by defining the dirham, mithqal, and their subdivisions: the dirham is half a mithqal plus a fifth, so ten dirhams equal seven mithqals (the known 'weight of seven'). The mithqal has 20 qirats, the dirham 14 qirats, and each qirat equals 5 grains (sha'irah). He then shows the arithmetic: 10 dirhams have 70 parts (each dirham 7 parts), giving 7 mithqals. The number of grains in a dirham is 70 (5*14), in ten dirhams 700, and in a mithqal 100 (5*20). He then translates these into the local weights of the Indian subcontinent: the main units are mahjah and tuljah (also called mashah). One tuljah = 12 mahjah. Each mahjah = 8 parts, each part called 'shirkh' in Persian, 'rabbi' in Hindi, and 'kahankaji' (referred to as 'red'). One red part = 4 grains. Therefore, a mithqal (100 grains) equals 25 red parts (100/4), which is 3 mahjah + 1 red. Multiplying by 20 gives the gold nisab: 20 mithqal = 5 tuljah + 2.5 mahjah. This calculation is crucial for Hanafi practice in the Indian subcontinent where local weights differ from standard Islamic weights.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers the obligation of zakat.
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight for gold; Hanafi: 20 qirats = 100 grains (sha'irah).
درهم (dirham): A unit of weight for silver; Hanafi: 14 qirats = 70 grains.
قيراط (qirat): A unit of weight equal to 5 grains (sha'irah).
شعيرة (sha'irah): A grain; the smallest weight unit used, approximately 0.049 grams.
ماهجة (mahjah): A local Indian weight unit equal to 8 red parts (shirkh) or 32 grains.
تولجة (tuljah): A local Indian weight unit equal to 12 mahjah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many grains (sha'irah) are in a mithqal according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) 70
B) 100
C) 140
D) 200

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi nisab for gold is 20 mithqal (100 grains each) and for silver 200 dirham (70 grains each), with a fixed ratio of 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals.

Zakat on Gold and Silver Jewelry: Obligation and Proofs
Ruling with proof from Sunnah on zakat of manufactured gold/silver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that zakat is wajib on all manufactured gold and silver, including jewelry, and explain the dalil from the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb.

Core Idea
All gold and silver items, even personal jewelry, require zakat when they reach nisab.

In the previous lesson, we calculated the nisab of silver. Now the author defines 'manufactured items' (ma'mul) and clarifies that zakat is due on every object made from gold or silver—coins, jewelry, utensils, even if used. Multiple hadith are cited to prove this obligation, especially the encounter of the Prophet ﷺ with a woman wearing gold bangles. You will see how the Hanafi school interprets these proofs to establish zakat on jewelry, contrary to the Shafi'i school which exempts personal jewelry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of ma'mul
The author defines 'ma'mul' as anything made from gold or silver, including dirhams and dinars (coins), women's jewelry (even if used), sword fittings, belts, vessels, etc. This broad definition sets the scope of zakat obligation.
Note that the definition includes items that are in use, not just stored wealth.
2
State the ruling and its condition
The ruling: zakat is wajib on all such items if they reach the nisab (200 dirhams of silver or 20 mithqal of gold). The author states this explicitly.
3
Locate the primary hadith proof
The key hadith is from Amr ibn Shu'ayb about a woman with a daughter wearing thick gold bangles. The Prophet asked: 'Do you pay zakat on this?' When she said no, he warned: 'Would it please you that Allah encircle you with fire?' This is a strong indication of obligation.
The author also cites hadith from Aisha and Umm Salama to reinforce the point.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (legal indication)
The Prophet's question 'Do you pay zakat?' implies that zakat is due (al-amr al-ijtihadi/bi al-istifham). The threat 'would it please you...' indicates that non-payment is a sin. Together, these establish wujub (obligation).
In Hanafi usul, such a threat is evidence for obligation.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and Hanafi tarjih
The Shafi'i school exempts jewelry intended for personal use. The Hanafi school holds that all gold and silver is zakatable regardless of use. The mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school is wujub, based on the hadith proofs and the principle of 'illah being the substance itself (ghina).
The author does not mention the Shafi'i view explicitly but the ikhtilaf is well-known.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Sunnah. After briefly calculating the silver nisab in local measures (200 dirham = 36.5 tuljah and half mahjah), the author (commentator) explains the phrase 'في معموله' from the base text. He defines 'manufactured items' (ma'mul) as anything made from gold or silver that is used in transactions (coins) or adornment (jewelry) or utensils, even if used. The ruling: zakat is obligatory on such items if they reach the nisab. Then he provides multiple hadith proofs: (1) The Prophet saw a woman with gold bangles and warned her; (2) A woman with a daughter wearing thick bangles; (3) Aisha had gold rings; (4) Umm Salama asked about kanz (treasure). All these hadith establish that zakat is due on gold jewelry, the Prophet rebuked those who did not pay, and he clarified that paying zakat removes the status of 'kanz' (hoarded treasure). The author also cites the sources: Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ahmad, etc., and notes that some hadith are hasan or sahih.

Key Terms
معموله (ma'muluhu): Items manufactured from gold or silver, including coins, jewelry, utensils, and ornaments.
كنز (kanz): Hoarded treasure on which zakat has not been paid.

Nisab of Silver in Local Units
Calculation of silver nisab with local unit conversions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the nisab of silver using standard dirhams and local units like mahjah, and identify the correct amount for zakat.

Core Idea
The nisab of silver is 200 dirhams; each dirham equals 14 qirats, enabling conversion to local measures.

Before discussing zakat on gold and silver items, the commentator establishes the precise nisab of silver. He provides a detailed conversion from the standard dirham to local units (mahjah) so that the reader can accurately determine when zakat becomes due. This lesson focuses on that calculation, which is essential for practical application in the commentator's region and helps us appreciate how fiqh rulings are adapted to local weights and measures.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard nisab of silver
The nisab is 200 dirhams. Each dirham is defined as 14 qirats or 70 barley grains.
This is the foundational figure from which all conversions derive.
2
Understand the conversion to local units
The commentator introduces the 'mahjah' as a local unit. He states that each dirham equals 17.5 ahmar, which is equivalent to 2 mahjah and 1.5 ahmar. Follow the arithmetic carefully.
17.5 ahmar = 2 mahjah + 1.5 ahmar
3
Compute the total nisab in mahjah
200 dirhams × (2 mahjah per dirham) plus the fractions yields 36 tawlah and half a mahjah. This is the local weight equivalent.
The text gives: 'ستا وثلاثين تولجة ونصف ماهجة'.
4
Apply to current coinage
The circulating coin in the commentator's land weighs 11.5 mahjah. Divide the nisab in mahjah by this to find the number of coins that constitute nisab.
Nisab in coins ≈ (400 mahjah) / 11.5 ≈ 34.8 coins, so at least 35 coins.

Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of zakat on silver and minted gold and silver items. It begins by establishing the nisab of silver: 200 dirhams, each dirham being 14 qirats (70 barley grains). The author converts this into local units (mahjah) for practical calculation, noting that in the lands of the Christian government, the standard coin is about 11.5 mahjah, so the nisab can be computed accordingly. Then the commentator explains the term 'مَعْمُولُهُ' (its minted items). He defines it broadly: anything made from gold or silver, including dirhams, dinars, women's jewelry (even if used), sword fittings, belts, bridles, saddles, and vessels (even if used). The ruling is that zakat is obligatory on all such items if they reach the nisab. The commentator then provides hadith evidence to support this. Several hadith are cited: (1) The Prophet asked a woman about zakat on her gold bracelets and warned of punishment in the Fire if she did not pay. (2) 'A'ishah's rings of silver – the Prophet told her they would suffice for her as a source of punishment if she did not pay zakat. (3) Umm Salamah's gold anklets – the Prophet clarified that if the amount reaches nisab and zakat is paid, it is not considered treasure (kanz) that incurs punishment. These hadith establish that zakat is due on gold and silver jewelry and ornamental items, regardless of their use. The Hanafi school follows these proofs and holds that zakat is wajib on all such items when they reach the nisab. This is a clear refutation of the view that jewelry is exempt merely because it is not for trade.

Key Terms
مَعْمُولُهُ (ma'muluhu): Anything made from gold or silver, including coins, jewelry, utensils, and fittings, that is used in transactions or adornment.
ماهجة (mahjah): A local unit of measurement for weight or value used in the commentator's region; approximately 11.5 mahjah = 1 dirham of the current coinage.

Zakat on All Gold and Silver Items: The Hanafi Position and Proofs
Legal ruling and hadith proofs for zakat on all gold/silver items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that zakat is wajib on all gold and silver items (including jewelry and utensils) and cite the hadith proofs used to derive this ruling.

Core Idea
All gold and silver items trigger zakat when they reach nisab; Prophetic warnings against hoarding adornments without paying zakat are explicit.

After establishing the nisab, the commentator turns to the crucial question: does zakat apply only to coins used for trade, or also to jewelry, vessels, and other items made from gold and silver? The Hanafi school takes a broad view: the material itself is the basis for obligation, not the form or use. The commentator defines 'مَعْمُولُهُ' comprehensively and then marshals several hadith to prove that women's jewelry and other ornamental items are subject to zakat. In this lesson, we examine these proofs and the ruling they support.

Guided Path
1
Define 'ma'mul' from the passage
It includes coins, women's jewelry, sword fittings, belts, bridles, saddles, vessels – anything made from gold or silver.
The passage explicitly lists these examples.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Zakat is wajib on all such items if they reach nisab, regardless of use or intention.
This is the central ruling of the passage.
3
Analyze the first hadith (woman with bracelets)
The Prophet asked a woman about zakat on her gold bracelets. When she said no, he warned of punishment in the Fire. This shows non-payment is sinful.
Hadith: «أتؤدي زكاته قالت : لا : قال : أيسرك أن يسوّرك الله من النَّار»
4
Examine the second hadith ('A'ishah's rings)
The Prophet saw silver rings on 'A'ishah and asked about zakat. He said they would suffice for her for the Fire if not paid.
This extends the ruling to silver items.
5
Examine the third hadith (Umm Salamah's anklets)
Umm Salamah asked about gold anklets being 'treasure' (kanz). The Prophet said if zakat is paid, it is not treasure.
This clarifies that zakat payment removes the sin of hoarding.

Source Text

وأما الفضة فقد عرفت أن نصابه مائتا درهم، وكل درهم أربعة عشر قيراطاً، يعني سبعين شعيرة، فتحصل في درهم سبعة عشر ونصف أحمر وهو ماهجتان، وواحد ونصف من ذلك الأحمر، فيكون مقدار مئتي درهم : ستا وثلاثين تولجة ونصف ماهجة.

As for silver, you already know that its nisab is two hundred dirhams, and each dirham is fourteen qirats, meaning seventy barley grains, so in a dirham there are seventeen and a half ahmar, which is two mahjah, and one and a half of that ahmar, so the amount of two hundred dirhams is thirty-six tawlah and half a mahjah. And it is known that the stamped coin circulating in our lands, the lands of the Christian government, is about eleven and a half mahjah, so the amount can be known with minimal reflection by one who is practiced in arithmetic. [1] His statement: 'its minted' means that which is made from gold and silver of dirhams and dinars that people use in transactions, and it is said of such struck coins, and women's ornaments even if used, and sword fittings, belt, bridle, saddle, or any vessels even if used. In short, everything made from them has zakat due on it if it reaches nisab. It has been reported that the Prophet saw on a woman's hand two bracelets of gold and said, 'Do you pay their zakat?' She said, 'No.' He said, 'Would it please you that Allah should encircle you with fire?' (1) From 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: A woman came to the Messenger of Allah with a daughter of hers, and on the daughter's hand were two thick bangles of gold. He said to her, 'Do you pay the zakat of these?' She said, 'No.' He said, 'Would it please you that Allah should encircle you with them on the Day of Resurrection as two bracelets of fire?' He said, 'So she took them off and threw them to the Prophet and said, 'They are for Allah and His Messenger.'' [Reported in Sunan Abi Dawud, Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra, Musnad Ishaq ibn Rahwayh, Musnad Ahmad, al-Mu'jam al-Kabir; authenticated by Ibn al-Qattan, and al-Nawawi said its chain is good.] And from 'A'ishah: 'The Messenger of Allah entered upon me and saw on my hands some rings of silver. He said, 'What is this, O 'A'ishah?' I said, 'I made them to adorn myself for you, O Messenger of Allah.' He said, 'Do you pay their zakat?' I said, 'No, or what Allah wills.' He said, 'They are sufficient for you for the Fire.' [Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Mustadrak; al-Hakim said its chain is authentic on the conditions of the two shaykhs.] And from Umm Salamah: 'I used to wear anklets of gold. I said, 'O Messenger of Allah, is this treasure?' He said, 'If it reaches the amount on which zakat is due and you pay zakat, then it is not treasure.' [Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Mustadrak, al-Mu'jam al-Kabir.]
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of zakat on silver and minted gold and silver items. It begins by establishing the nisab of silver: 200 dirhams, each dirham being 14 qirats (70 barley grains). The author converts this into local units (mahjah) for practical calculation, noting that in the lands of the Christian government, the standard coin is about 11.5 mahjah, so the nisab can be computed accordingly. Then the commentator explains the term 'مَعْمُولُهُ' (its minted items). He defines it broadly: anything made from gold or silver, including dirhams, dinars, women's jewelry (even if used), sword fittings, belts, bridles, saddles, and vessels (even if used). The ruling is that zakat is obligatory on all such items if they reach the nisab. The commentator then provides hadith evidence to support this. Several hadith are cited: (1) The Prophet asked a woman about zakat on her gold bracelets and warned of punishment in the Fire if she did not pay. (2) 'A'ishah's rings of silver – the Prophet told her they would suffice for her as a source of punishment if she did not pay zakat. (3) Umm Salamah's gold anklets – the Prophet clarified that if the amount reaches nisab and zakat is paid, it is not considered treasure (kanz) that incurs punishment. These hadith establish that zakat is due on gold and silver jewelry and ornamental items, regardless of their use. The Hanafi school follows these proofs and holds that zakat is wajib on all such items when they reach the nisab. This is a clear refutation of the view that jewelry is exempt merely because it is not for trade.

Key Terms
مَعْمُولُهُ (ma'muluhu): Anything made from gold or silver, including coins, jewelry, utensils, and fittings, that is used in transactions or adornment.
ماهجة (mahjah): A local unit of measurement for weight or value used in the commentator's region; approximately 11.5 mahjah = 1 dirham of the current coinage.

Key Takeaway
Zakat on gold and silver is not limited to currency; it applies to jewelry, utensils, and all items made from these metals when the nisab is reached, as proven by explicit hadith.

Zakat on Trade Goods: Valuing for the Poor's Benefit
Commentary on zakat valuation rule for bullion and trade goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for valuing trade goods (ard tijarah) for zakat by selecting the currency (gold or silver) that yields the maximum benefit to the poor.

Core Idea
Trade goods for zakat must be valued in the currency that gives the poor the most – not the taxpayer's convenience.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains a key Hanafi ruling: when a merchant has trade goods that are not themselves gold or silver, they must be valued in gold or silver to determine if zakat is due. But which currency? The Hanafi school uniquely requires the valuation that benefits the poor more. This ensures the maximum zakat is paid. Let's walk through the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assets
The passage mentions two types of zakatable assets: bullion (tibr) – raw gold/silver – and trade goods (ard tijarah). First, understand what qualifies as trade goods: movable property intended for sale, not measured by volume/weight, not animals or real estate.
2
Understand the valuation requirement
Trade goods themselves are not cash, so they must be converted into a cash equivalent – either gold (dinars) or silver (dirhams) – to see if their value reaches the nisab threshold.
The nisab for silver is 200 dirhams; for gold, 20 dinars (approx 85g).
3
Apply the 'more beneficial to the poor' criterion
The valuer has a choice between gold and silver, but must pick whichever yields a higher zakat amount for the poor. The text explains the scenarios: if one currency is more current (arwaj), that one is specified; if only one reaches nisab, that one is used; if both reach but one gives more zakat, that one is chosen.
The goal is to maximize the poor's benefit, not ease the taxpayer's calculation.
4
Work through the examples
The commentator gives two concrete examples: (1) Goods worth 240 dirhams or 23 dinars → compare zakat: 240×1/40=6 dirhams vs 23×1/40=0.575 dinar (~5.75 dirhams) → choose dirhams. (2) Goods worth 136 dirhams or 24 dinars → 136×1/40=3.4 dirhams vs 24×1/40=0.6 dinar (~6 dirhams) → choose dinars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وغيره، وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة، صحت أسانيد بعضها كما بسط العيني في «البناية).
[1] قوله : وتبره ؛ هو بكسرِ التَّاء المثناة الفوقية، وسكون الباء الموحدة: وهو عبارة عن الذهب والفضة قبل أن يصاغا.

وثلاثين، قومها بالدنانير.
Book of Zakat, Zakat of Wealth. And its bullion, and trade goods whose value reaches nisab of one of them (gold or silver) valued at what is more beneficial for the poor, one-quarter of a tenth. And its bullion, and trade goods whose value reaches nisab of one of them valued at what is more beneficial for the poor, one-quarter of a tenth. That is: if valuation by dirhams is more beneficial for the poor, the trade goods are valued in dirhams; if valuation in dinars is more beneficial, they are valued accordingly. It was transmitted by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and others; there are many hadith on this topic, some with authentic chains as detailed by al-'Ayni in al-Binayah. [1] His saying: 'and its bullion' — with kasra of the ta and sukuun of the ba; it refers to gold and silver before they are fashioned. [2] His saying: 'and trade goods' — al-'ard (with sukuun of the ra) is goods that are not measured by volume or weight, and are not animals or real estate, as in al-Sihah. With fatha (of the ra), it refers to worldly goods and all property. In the matn, it is used for what is not cash, since cash was mentioned previously, so interpreting it with fatha is more appropriate than with sukuun. [3] His saying: 'its value' — the pronoun refers to 'ard, and the clause is an adjective. [4] His saying: 'of one of them' — it indicates that the valuer has a choice between valuation in gold and valuation in silver. [5] His saying: 'valued at what is more beneficial for the poor' — it is mentioned in al-Siraj and elsewhere: if valuation in gold and silver are equal, he values in either; if one is more current, that one is specified; if it reaches nisab in one but not the other, the one in which it reaches nisab is specified; if it reaches nisab plus one-fifth in one and less in the other, he values in the one more beneficial for the poor. Example: if when valued in dirhams it reaches 240 and in dinars 23, he values it in dirhams because then six dirhams are due, whereas in dinars only half a dinar is due (value five dirhams). And if it reaches 24 dinars and 136 dirhams, he values in dinars.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of zakat for bullion (tibr) and trade goods ('ard tijarah). The matn states: 'And its bullion, and trade goods whose value reaches nisab of one of them (gold or silver) valued at what is more beneficial for the poor, one-quarter of a tenth.' The commentator explains each phrase. First, he defines 'tibr' as gold and silver before being fashioned into coins or jewelry. Then he defines 'trade goods': it is movable property that is not measured by volume or weight, not animals, and not real estate. The word 'ard can be read with sukun (meaning goods) or with fatha (meaning worldly possessions); here, since the previous context mentioned gold and silver (nugud), the reading with fatha is more appropriate to indicate non-cash assets. The valuation rule is crucial: when a person has trade goods that are not themselves gold or silver, they must be valued in gold or silver to determine if their total value reaches the nisab. The valuer has a choice between gold and silver, but he must choose whichever benefits the poor more. The commentator elaborates: if the valuation in gold and silver gives the same result, he may choose either. However, if one currency is more current (arwaj) in the market, that one is specified. If the goods reach nisab in one currency but not in the other, he must value in the currency that shows nisab reached. If the goods reach nisab plus one-fifth (i.e., exceed the nisab by a certain margin) in one currency, but in the other they are less than nisab, he must value in the currency that gives more benefit to the poor. The examples illustrate: Suppose the goods are worth 240 dirhams or 23 dinars (where 1 dinar = ~10 dirhams approx). If valued in dirhams, zakat due is 6 dirhams (1/40 of 240). If valued in dinars, zakat due is half a dinar (5 dirhams value). Since 6 dirhams is more than 5, the valuation in dirhams is more beneficial to the poor, so it is chosen. Conversely, if the goods are worth 24 dinars (zakat = 3/5 dinar ~6 dirhams) or 136 dirhams (zakat = 3.4 dirhams), the valuation in dinars yields more, so that is chosen. This rule ensures that the poor receive the maximum zakat possible. The Hanafi school upholds this method, and the commentator notes that hadith evidence supports it (though not quoted in full in this excerpt).

Key Terms
تبر (tibr): Uncoined gold or silver, i.e., in its raw form before being minted or crafted into coins, jewelry, or utensils.
عرض تجارة (ard tijarah): Trade goods; any movable property intended for sale that is not measured by volume or weight, and is not animals or real estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Either, because they are equal in value
D) Dirhams, because silver is more common

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for trade goods zakat ensures the poor's maximum benefit by requiring valuation in the more beneficial currency.

Valuing Trade Goods for Zakat: The Hanafi Rule of Benefit for the Poor
Valuation method for trade goods zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for valuing trade goods in gold or silver, choosing the currency that yields greater benefit for the poor, and calculate the resulting zakat.

Core Idea
Trade goods are valued in the currency that gives the poor more zakat—not the owner's preference.

Zakat on trade goods is not simply based on the market value in any currency. The Hanafi school has a detailed method to ensure the maximum benefit for the poor. This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah explains the rule: when a merchant has trade goods, they must be valued in gold (dinars) or silver (dirhams)—whichever yields a higher zakat amount for the poor. This lesson will guide you through the logic and the specific scenarios where this rule applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assets subject to zakat
The passage covers three assets: bullion (tibr: uncoined gold/silver) and trade goods (ard tijarah). First, confirm that each qualifies for zakat when it reaches nisab.
Trade goods are merchandise not measured by volume/weight, not animals, not real estate.
2
Understand the nisab threshold for gold and silver
Zakat becomes due when the value in either gold or silver reaches nisab. For silver, nisab is 200 dirhams; for gold, it is 20 dinars (approx. 85g gold). The passage uses these figures in examples.
Remember: 1 dinar is about 4.25g gold; 1 dirham is about 3g silver, but nisab amounts are fixed in classical fiqh.
3
Apply the 'most beneficial for the poor' rule
The valuator (the merchant or the authority) must choose the currency (gold or silver) that results in a larger zakat payment to the poor. This is not the owner's choice but a rule of valuation.
The commentator mentions scenarios: if both values equal, pick either; if one is more prevalent, use that; if only one reaches nisab, that currency is forced.
4
Work through the numerical example in the text
The passage gives an example: trade goods valued in dirhams = 240 dirhams (zakat: 1/40 = 6 dirhams), valued in dinars = 23 dinars (zakat: 0.5 dinar ≈ 5 dirhams). Since 6 > 5, valuation in dirhams is chosen. This illustrates the rule.
240 dirhams exceeds 200 nisab by 40; 23 dinars exceeds 20 nisab by 3 but zakat is half dinar (0.5).
5
Generalize the rule to all trade goods
For any trade goods, at the end of the lunar year, compute the market value in dirhams and dinars. If the zakat amounts differ, choose the higher one. If only one reaches nisab, that one is used.
This ensures the poor receive the maximum due, aligning with the spirit of zakat.

Source Text
