كتاب الزكاة زكاة المال وتبره، وعرض تجارة قيمته نصاب من أحدهما مقوماً بالأنفع للفقراء ربع عشر أي إن كان التقويم بالدراهم أنفع للفقير قوم عروض التجارة بالدراهم، وإن كان بالدنانير أنفع قومت بها. أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وغيره، وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة، صحت أسانيد بعضها كما بسط العيني في «البناية». قوله : وتبره ؛ هو بكسر التاء المثناة الفوقية، وسكون الباء الموحدة: وهو عبارة عن الذهب والفضة قبل أن يصاغا. قوله : وعرض تجارة ؛ العرض : بسكون الراء متاع لا يدخله كيل ولا وزن، ولا يكون حيواناً ولا عقاراً، كذا في «الصحاح»، وأما بفتحها فيطلق على متاع الدنيا وجميع الأموال، وهو في المتن محمول على ما ليس بنقد لذكره قبله فحمله على الفتح أولى من حمله على السكون. قوله : قيمته ؛ الضمير إلى العرض والجملة صفة له. قوله : من أحدهما ؛ أشار إلى أنه بخير بين التقويم بالذهب وبين التقويم بالفضة. قوله : مقوماً بالأنفع للفقراء ؛ ذكر في «السراج» وغيره : أنه إن استوى التقويم بالذهب والفضة يقوم بأحدهما ، فلو أحدهما أروج تعين التقويم، ولو بلغ بأحدهما نصاباً دون الآخر تعين ما يبلغ به، ولو بلغ بأحدهما نصاباً وخمساً، وبالآخر أقل قومه بالأنفع للفقير. وصورته : أنه لو كان بحيث لو قومهما بالدراهم بلغت مئتين وأربعين، وبالدنانير ثلاثاً وعشرين قومها بالدراهم لوجوب ستة فيها ، بخلاف الدنانير، فإنه يجب فيها نصف دينار، وقيمته خمسة، ولو بلغت بالدنانير أربعة وعشرين، وبالدراهم مئة وستة وثلاثين، قومها بالدنانير.
Book of Zakat, Zakat on Wealth. And his bullion, and trade goods whose value reaches nisab in one of them, valued in the way most beneficial for the poor: one quarter of a tenth. i.e., if valuation in dirhams is more beneficial for the poor, the trade goods are valued in dirhams; and if in dinars is more beneficial, they are valued in them. It was reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi and others, and on the topic there are many traditions, some of whose chains are authentic as al-‘Ayni elaborated in al-Binaya. His saying: 'and his bullion' – it is gold and silver before being minted. His saying: 'and trade goods' – 'ard with sukun on the ra' is merchandise that is not measured by volume or weight, and is not an animal or real estate, as in al-Sihah; while with fatha it applies to worldly goods and all wealth, and in the text it is interpreted as what is not currency because it is mentioned after currency, so interpreting it with fatha is more appropriate. His saying: 'its value' – the pronoun refers to the 'ard and the clause is its attribute. His saying: 'in one of them' – indicates that he has a choice between valuation in gold and valuation in silver. His saying: 'valued in the way most beneficial for the poor' – it is mentioned in al-Siraj and others: if valuation in gold and silver are equal, he values in one of them; if one is more prevalent, valuation is determined; if it reaches nisab in one but not the other, what reaches nisab is determined; if it reaches nisab and a fifth in one, and less in the other, he values it in the way most beneficial for the poor. Its example: if when valued in dirhams it amounts to two hundred and forty, and in dinars twenty-three, it is valued in dirhams because six dirhams are due in it, unlike dinars where half a dinar is due worth five; and if it amounts to twenty-four dinars and one hundred and thirty-six dirhams, it is valued in dinars.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Zakat explains the ruling on zakat for gold and silver bullion (tibr) and trade goods (ard al-tijara). The matn states that the nisab is determined by value in either gold or silver, and the valuation must be in the currency that is more beneficial for the poor. The commentator defines key terms: tibr is uncoined gold and silver; 'ard (trade goods) is merchandise that is not measured by volume or weight, not animals or real estate. The valuation method is elaborated: if both currencies give the same value, the owner chooses; if one is more current, that one is used; if one reaches nisab and the other does not, the reaching one is used; if one reaches nisab plus a fifth but the other less, the more beneficial for the poor is chosen. An example is given: if valued in dirhams it is 240 (which exceeds the nisab of 200 by 40, so zakat is 6 dirhams), but in dinars it is 23 (only 20 dinars nisab, so zakat is 0.5 dinar worth 5 dirhams). Since 6 dirhams is more than 5 dirhams, we value in dirhams to benefit the poor more. This is the Hanafi position as found in the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
تِبْر (tibr): Gold and silver before being minted into coins.
عَرْض تِجَارَة ('ard tijārah): Merchandise (not currency, not measured by volume/weight, not animals or real estate) held for trade.
رُبْع الْعُشْر (rub' al-'ushr): One quarter of a tenth, i.e., 2.5% – the standard zakat rate on silver, gold, and trade goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if trade goods, when valued in gold, give a zakat of 0.5 dinar (worth 5 dirhams), and when valued in silver give a zakat of 6 dirhams, which valuation should be used?
A) Gold, because gold is more valuable.
B) Silver, because it gives a higher zakat amount.
C) The owner's choice.
D) The currency prevalent in the market.

Key Takeaway
Trade goods for zakat must be valued in gold or silver, whichever yields a higher zakat payment to the poor, ensuring maximum benefit.

Zakat on Fractional Increases: The Khums Threshold
Zakat on fractional increases above the nisab is only due when the fraction reaches 1/5 of the nisab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for zakat on fractional increases above the nisab and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement with the two companions.

Core Idea
No zakat on fractional increases unless the excess reaches one-fifth (40 dirhams) of the nisab.

Zakat is obligatory on wealth that reaches the nisab, but what about amounts that exceed the nisab? The Hanafi school, following Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that fractional increases (kusur) are exempt from zakat until they cumulatively reach one-fifth of the nisab. This lesson unpacks that rule, its practical application, and the disagreement within the school from the two companions (Sahibayn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the rule: Zakat on excess above nisab is wajib only when the excess reaches or exceeds one-fifth (khums) of the nisab. For silver (200 dirhams), the khums is 40 dirhams.
This is Imam Abu Hanifa's view.
2
Recognize the intra-madhhab variant
The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that any fraction above the nisab is subject to zakat proportionally, without requiring the khums threshold.
Their view is mentioned as 'khilafan lahuma'.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The proof for Abu Hanifa's position is the hadith: 'Do not take anything from fractions' (لا تأخذ من الكسور شيئاً), though its chain is considered weak.
The hadith is cited from al-Jassas and al-Daraqutni.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the rule: If someone has 240 dirhams (200 + 40), zakat is 6 dirhams (5 base + 1 for the excess of 40). If the excess is only 20 dirhams, no extra zakat. Under the companions' view, zakat would be due on the full amount proportionally.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the madhhab is Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated by 'عنده' in the text and the citation of the hadith.
Despite the weak chain, the hadith is used as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في كل خمس زاد على النصاب بحسابه. اعلم أن الزكاة لا تجب في الكسور عندنا إلا إذا بلغ خمس النصاب، فإذا زاد على مئتي درهم أربعون درهما زاد في الزكاة درهم، وإن زاد ثمانون درهما زاد درهمان، ولا شيء في الأقل. قوله : ثم في كل خمس؛ بضم الخاء المعجمة، وضم الميم وبسكونها. قوله : ولا شيء؛ هذا عنده خلافاً لهما، وسنده حديث: «لا تأخذ من الكسور شيئاً» قاله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين وجهه إلى اليمن، أخرجه أبو بكر الجصاص الرازي في «شرح مختصر الطحاوي»، والدارقطني بسند ضعيف.
Then for every five that increases upon the nisab, in its proportion. Know that zakat is not due on fractions according to us unless it reaches one-fifth of the nisab. So if there is an increase of forty dirhams above two hundred dirhams, the zakat increases by one dirham; if it increases by eighty dirhams, it increases by two dirhams; and nothing is due on less. His saying: 'then for every five' – with dammah on the kha' and the mim, and sukun on the mim. His saying: 'and nothing' – this is his (Abu Hanifa's) view, contrary to them (the two companions). Its proof is the hadith: 'Do not take anything from fractions' said by the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he sent him to Yemen. It was reported by Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi and by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi rule regarding fractional increases above the nisab for zakat on silver (dirhams). The matn states that for every five (khums) above the nisab, zakat is due proportionally. The commentator explains that zakat is not due on fractions according to Imam Abu Hanifa unless the fraction amounts to one-fifth of the nisab (i.e., 40 dirhams). So if someone has 240 dirhams (200 nisab + 40), zakat is 6 dirhams (5 dirhams base + 1 for the extra 40). If the excess is 80 dirhams, zakat increases by 2 dirhams. For less than 40 dirhams excess, no extra zakat. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, opposed by the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who say zakat is due on any fraction proportionally. The proof cited is a hadith commanding not to take anything from fractions, though it is considered weak. The commentator clarifies the pronunciation of 'khums'.

Key Terms
خُمْس (khums): One-fifth; here, a fraction equal to one-fifth of the nisab (40 dirhams for silver).
كُسُور (kusūr): Fractions; amounts less than the khums above the nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone has 210 dirhams at year-end (nisab is 200), how much zakat is due?
A) 5 dirhams (on the full 200 plus 10).
B) 5 dirhams (only on the 200 nisab, nothing on the 10).
C) 5.25 dirhams (proportional on excess).
D) 6 dirhams (assuming 210 exceeds nisab by 10).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that fractional increases above the nisab reach one-fifth of the nisab before zakat is due on them, a rule supported by a hadith and upheld by Imam Abu Hanifa.

Zakat on Mixed Silver (Alloy)
Zakat ruling on alloyed silver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how mixed silver is treated for zakat in the Hanafi school and determine the ruling based on the proportion of silver versus alloy.

Core Idea
If silver content predominates, zakat on silver; if alloy predominates, zakat on trade goods value.

This lesson explains how to calculate zakat on silver coins or bullion that are mixed with an alloy (ghishsh). The Hanafi school examines whether the silver or the alloy predominates, and treats the asset accordingly—either as silver or as trade goods. There is also a precautionary ruling when the two are equal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the silver item
Determine whether the item is warq (unminted silver) or a mixed alloy. The passage refers to silver mixed with an alloy from another substance.
2
Assess the predominant component
If the silver content is greater than the alloy, the item is ruled as silver. If the alloy is greater, it is ruled as trade goods (urud). If they are equal, there is ikhtilaf; the chosen view is to pay zakat as a precaution.
A small amount of alloy is excused because silver cannot be minted without it.
3
Apply the corresponding zakat rule
For silver-ruled items: zakat is due at 2.5% if the pure silver reaches nisab (200 dirhams). For trade goods-ruled items: zakat is due on the total value (including alloy) when the value reaches nisab of silver or gold, at the end of the year.
The nisab for silver is approximately 595 grams; for gold it is about 85 grams.
4
Note the precautionary rule for equal mixture
If silver and alloy are exactly equal, the mufta bihi position is to pay zakat as a precaution. This resolves the intra-madhhab disagreement.
This ruling is supported by al-Khaniyya and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وورق غلب فضته فضة، وما غلب غشه يقوم. ونقصان النصاب في الحول هدر. قوله : وورق؛ الورق بفتح الواو، وكسر الراء المهملة: الفضة الغير المضروبة. قوله : فضة؛ يعني إذا كانت فضة مخلوطة بالغش من غيرها، فإن كانت الغلبة للفضة فهو في حكمها، فإن الغش إذا كان قليلاً لا يعتبر إذ الفضة لا تنطبع إلا بقليل الغش، فجعل القليل عفواً دون الكثير، فإن كانت الغلبة للغش فهو في حكم العروض، فتزكي بعد أن تبلغ قيمته نصاباً. قوله : وما غلب؛ وإن ساوى الغش الفضة اختلف فيه، والمختار لزوم الزكاة فيه احتياطاً. كذا في «الخانية»، وفي «الدر المختار» وغيره: «أما الذهب المخلوط بالفضة فإن غلب الذهب فذهب، وإلا فإن بلغ الذهب أو الفضة نصابه وجبت». قوله : ونقصان النصاب... الخ؛ حاصله: أنه يشترط لوجوب الزكاة كمال النصاب في طرفي الحول، ولا يضر نقصانه في الأثناء.
And silver (warq) whose silver content predominates is (treated as) silver, and that whose alloy predominates is valued (as trade goods). And the deficiency of nisab during the year is disregarded. His saying: 'and warq' – warq with fatha on waw and kasra on ra' is unminted silver. His saying: 'silver' – meaning if it is silver mixed with alloy from something else; if the silver predominates, it is in its ruling, for if the alloy is little, it is not considered because silver cannot be minted without a little alloy, so the little is excused while the much is not. If the alloy predominates, it is in the ruling of trade goods, so zakat is due after its value reaches nisab. His saying: 'and what predominates' – if the alloy and silver are equal, there is disagreement, and the chosen view is that zakat is obligatory as a precaution. Thus in al-Khaniyya, and in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others: 'As for gold mixed with silver, if gold predominates it is gold; otherwise, if the gold or silver reaches its nisab, zakat is due.' His saying: 'and the deficiency of nisab...' – its summary: it is a condition for the obligation of zakat that the nisab be complete at both ends of the year, and its deficiency in the middle does not harm.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
The passage covers two rulings. First, zakat on mixed silver (warq): if the silver content is more than the alloy, it is treated as pure silver, and zakat is due when it reaches the silver nisab. A small amount of alloy is overlooked because silver cannot be minted without it. If the alloy predominates, it is treated as trade goods, and zakat is due when its total value (including alloy) reaches the nisab of silver or gold. If the two are equal, there is disagreement; the relied-upon view is to pay zakat as precaution. The second ruling: a deficiency in nisab during the year (e.g., the silver drops below nisab mid-year) does not invalidate the year; only the beginning and end are considered. If at the beginning and end the nisab is complete, zakat is due even if it dipped in between. This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
وَرِق (warq): Unminted silver (coins or bullion).
غِشّ (ghishsh): Alloy or impurity mixed with silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is mixed silver treated as trade goods (urud) for zakat?
A) When the alloy content is less than the silver.
B) When the alloy content is greater than the silver.
C) When the silver and alloy are equal.
D) Always, because it is not pure silver.

Key Takeaway
Mixed silver is ruled by its predominant component: if silver predominates, zakat as silver; if alloy predominates, zakat as trade goods; if equal, precautionary zakat.

Deficiency of Nisab During the Year
Zakat condition on nisab continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a temporary deficiency of nisab during the zakat year does not invalidate the obligation, provided nisab is complete at the beginning and end.

Core Idea
A dip below nisab mid-year is disregarded – only the start and finish matter.

This lesson addresses a common question: what happens if one's zakatable wealth falls below the nisab during the year? The Hanafi school holds that only the beginning and end of the year are considered. A temporary deficiency in between does not break the year or reset the count.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
For zakat to be wajib, the nisab must be present at the beginning of the lunar year and at the end. A deficiency in the middle does not affect obligation.
This is a condition (shart) for zakat, not a pillar.
2
Identify when deficiency matters
If nisab is completely lost at both ends (e.g., wealth drops below nisab on the last day), then no zakat is due for that year. The year resets from the next time nisab is possessed.
Deficiency only matters if it occurs at the beginning or end.
3
Apply the rule to practical scenarios
Example: A person has 600g of silver on Day 1, drops to 400g mid-year, and returns to 600g on the last day. Zakat is due on 600g. If the person had only 400g on both Day 1 and Day 365, no zakat is due.
The actual nisab for silver is ~595g; adjust accordingly.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires nisab to be maintained throughout the entire year. Any dip below nisab resets the year. The Hanafi position is more lenient.
This difference has practical implications for those who follow different schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) The year resets, and zakat is due on the new year from the date of recovery.
B) The deficiency is disregarded, and zakat is due on the end-of-year amount.

Zakat on Fractions: The Fifth Threshold
Ruling on obligatory zakat on fractions reaching a fifth of nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on zakat for fractional increases above nisab and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Zakat is not due on fractional increases until they reach a fifth of the nisab — a threshold established by hadith despite its weak chain.

Zakat is obligatory on wealth that reaches the nisab, but what about amounts exceeding the nisab? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position: only when the excess accumulates to a full fifth of the nisab does additional zakat become due. The Sahibayn disagree, creating an important intra-madhhab difference grounded in different approaches to evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Subject
The passage addresses zakat on fractional increases (kusur) above the nisab. First, extract the general rule: no zakat on fractions until they reach a specific threshold.
The ruling is about additional zakat on the surplus, not the base nisab.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and the Threshold
Abu Hanifa holds that zakat on the increase is due only when the surplus equals or exceeds one-fifth of the nisab (i.e., 40 dirhams for silver). For every fifth above the nisab, one additional dirham of zakat per 40 dirhams is due.
Use the example: if surplus is 40 dirhams, add 1 dirham; if 80 dirhams, add 2 dirhams; less than 40, no extra zakat.
3
Note the Sahibayn's Dissent
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that any increase, no matter how small, should be subject to zakat proportionally. They rely on qiyas (analogy) since the increase is part of taxable wealth.
The text signals this with 'عنده خلافاً لهما' – 'according to him contrary to the two.'
4
Examine the Dalil and Its Strength
Abu Hanifa's proof is a hadith: 'Do not take anything from fractions' (لا تأخذ من الكسور شيئاً). The chain is weak (da'if), but it provides textual support. The Sahibayn either reject the hadith due to weakness or prioritize qiyas over it.
The commentator notes the hadith is from al-Jassas and al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, yet the school adopts it.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The difference means that under Abu Hanifa, small increases (e.g., 10 dirhams) attract no extra zakat; under the Sahibayn, every fractional dirham would incur a proportional zakat. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) follows Abu Hanifa.
This threshold simplifies zakat calculation and reduces burden for those with marginal increases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في كلِّ خُمْس زادَ على النصاب بحسابه. اعلم أن الزَّكَاةَ لا تجب في الكسور عندنا إلا إذا بلغ خُمْسَ النّصاب، فإذا زاد على مئتي درهم أربعونَ درهما زادَ في الزَّكاة درهم، وإن زاد ثمانون درهما زاد درهمان، ولا شيء في الأقل. قوله : ثم في كلّ خمس؛ بضم الخاء المعجمة، وضم الميم وبسكونها. قوله : ولا شيء؛ هذا عنده خلافاً لهما، وسنده حديث : «لا تأخذ من الكسور شيئاً» قاله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين وجهه إلى اليمن، أخرجه أبو بكر الجصَّاصَ الرَّازي في «شرح مختصر الطحاوي»، والدَّارَ قُطْنِي بسند ضعيف.
Then for every five that exceeds the nisab, according to its calculation. Know that zakat is not obligatory on fractions according to us unless it reaches a fifth of the nisab. So if there is an increase of forty dirhams over two hundred dirhams, one dirham is added in zakat; if there is an increase of eighty dirhams, two dirhams are added; and nothing is due on less. He said: 'Then for every five' – with dammah on the khā' and mīm, and sukūn on the mīm. He said: 'And nothing' – this is according to him [Abu Hanifa] contrary to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. Its evidence is the hadith: 'Do not take anything from fractions,' said by the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he sent him to Yemen. It was narrated by Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi and by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on zakat on fractional increases above the nisab. The author first states that zakat is not obligatory on fractions unless they accumulate to a fifth of the nisab. For example, if one has 200 dirhams (the nisab for silver), zakat is 5 dirhams. If an additional 40 dirhams are added (making 240), then an extra 1 dirham is due. If 80 dirhams are added (making 280), an extra 2 dirhams are due. No zakat is due on increases of less than 40 dirhams. This ruling is from Abu Hanifa. The commentary notes that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and hold that zakat is due on any increase, no matter how small. Abu Hanifa's proof is a hadith (though with a weak chain) instructing not to take zakat from fractions. The term 'خمس' is lexically explained.

Key Terms
خُمْس (khums): A fifth; in this context, a fifth of the nisab (i.e., 40 dirhams out of 200).
كسور (kusūr): Fractions; amounts less than a complete nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone has 220 dirhams (nisab is 200), how much zakat is due?
A) 5 dirhams zakat on the 200, and 0.5 dirhams on the extra 20
B) 5 dirhams zakat only, because the extra 20 is less than a fifth of the nisab
C) 5.5 dirhams zakat
D) No zakat because 20 is a fraction

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, zakat on fractional increases above the nisab is only wajib when the surplus reaches a fifth of the nisab.

Zakat on Mixed Silver and Gold
Ruling on zakat for silver mixed with impurity and gold mixed with silver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for zakat on mixed silver and gold, and apply the dominance criterion to determine whether a mixture is treated as the metal, as a trade good, or subject to the obligatory precaution when equal.

Core Idea
Mixed metals: the dominant constituent governs the zakat ruling.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the zakat rules for silver mixed with impurities and gold mixed with silver. The key principle is dominance: if the pure metal outweighs the alloy, it retains its zakat status; if the alloy is greater, it becomes like a trade good. When they are equal, the later authorities prefer to require zakat as a precaution. This lesson breaks down the ruling and its application.

Guided Path
1
Define key terms
Learn that 'waraq' means unminted silver and 'ghish' refers to impurity or alloy. These terms are central to the passage.
Unminted silver is the default, but the same ruling applies to minted coins.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage covers two cases: silver mixed with an impurity (like copper) and gold mixed with silver. For each, the ruling depends on which content dominates by weight.
3
Apply the dominance rule
If silver (or gold) dominates, the mixture is treated as pure silver (or gold) for zakat. If the impurity dominates, it is treated as a trade good (ʻurūḍ), so zakat is based on market value.
A small amount of impurity is overlooked because silver cannot be minted without any alloy.
4
Handle the equal case
When the silver and impurity are equal in weight, there is a difference of opinion. The preferred (mukhtār) view obligates zakat as a precaution.
Sources like al-Khaniyyah and al-Durr al-Mukhtar support this preference.
5
Extend to gold-silver mixtures
For gold mixed with silver, the same principle applies. If gold dominates, it is gold; otherwise, if either the gold or silver content individually reaches its nisab, zakat is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وورق غَلَبَ فضتُهُ فضة، وما غَلَبَ غَشُهُ يُقوّم. قوله : وورق؛ الورق بفتح الواو، وكسر الرَّاءِ المهملة: الفضة الغير المضروبة. قوله : فضة؛ يعني إذا كانت فضَّةً مخلوطة بالغش من غيرها، فإن كانت الغلبة للفضة فهو في حكمها، فإنَّ الغش إذا كان قليلاً لا يعتبر إذ الفضة لا تنطبع إلا بقليل الغش، فجعل القليل عفواً دون الكثير، فإن كانت الغلبة للغش فهو في حكـم العروض، فتزكي بعد أن تبلغ قيمته نصاباً. قوله : وما غلب؛ وإن ساوى الغش الفضَّةَ اختلف فيه، والمختار لزومُ الزَّكَاةِ فيه احتياطا. كذا في «الخانية»، وفي «الدر المختار» وغيره : «أمَّا الذَّهَبُ المخلوط بالفضَّةِ فإن غلب الذهب فذهب، وإلا فإن بلغ الذهب أو الفضة نصابَهُ وجبت.
And silver whose silver content predominates is (treated as) silver; and that whose impurity predominates is valued. He said: 'Waraq' – al-waraq with fathah on waw and kasrah on rā': unminted silver. He said: 'Fiddah' – meaning when it is silver mixed with impurity from something else. If silver predominates, it is in its ruling, because a little impurity is not considered, as silver cannot be minted without a little impurity. So the little is overlooked, unlike the much. If impurity predominates, it is in the ruling of trade goods, so zakat is given after its value reaches a nisab. He said: 'And what dominates' – and if the impurity equals the silver, there is disagreement, and the preferred view is that zakat is obligatory as a precaution. Thus in al-Khaniyyah, and in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others: 'As for gold mixed with silver, if gold dominates, it is gold; otherwise, if either gold or silver reaches a nisab, zakat is obligatory.'
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage addresses zakat on mixed metals. For silver mixed with an impurity (like copper), if the silver content is greater, it is treated as silver for zakat purposes. A small amount of impurity is overlooked because silver cannot be minted without some alloy. If the impurity dominates, it is treated as a trade good (ʻurūḍ), so zakat is due when its market value reaches the nisab. If the impurity and silver are equal, there is a difference of opinion, but the preferred (mukhtār) position requires zakat as a precaution. For gold mixed with silver, the ruling is similar: whichever metal dominates determines the treatment; if they are equal, then if either metal reaches its nisab, zakat is due.

Key Terms
ورق (waraq): Unminted silver; silver in raw or coin form.
غش (ghish): Impurity or alloy mixed with silver or gold.
عروض (ʻurūḍ): Trade goods; goods intended for sale and trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone has 180 dirhams of silver mixed with 20 dirhams worth of copper impurity, which ruling applies?
A) Treated as silver because silver dominates
B) Treated as trade goods because impurity dominates
C) No zakat because mixture is not pure
D) Treated as gold

Key Takeaway
The zakat ruling for mixtures of silver and gold depends on which content dominates: the pure metal or the impurity; when they are equal, the preferred view requires zakat as a precaution.

Adding Gold and Silver by Value or Parts: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on adding gold/silver for zakat nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on mixing gold, silver, and trade goods for zakat, and apply the mufta bihi rule to determine zakat obligation.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are added by value (Abu Hanifa) or by weight (Sahibayn) – the thamaniyyah drives the dispute.

Zakat requires a complete nisab at both start and end of the year. But when you own both gold and silver, can they be combined to reach nisab? The Hanafi school has a famous internal disagreement: Abu Hanifa says add by monetary value; his two disciples say add by weight. This lesson unpacks the proofs and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab condition
The passage first states that for zakat to be wajib, the nisab must exist at the beginning of the year (to start the count) and at the end (for obligation to actualize). A deficiency in between does not harm, but total loss during the year invalidates the year. This is the foundational context for the addition dispute.
This rule applies to all zakatable wealth.
2
Distinguish the two methods of ḍamm
Abu Hanifa holds that gold and silver are added by their market value (qīmah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that they are added by parts (ajzā'), i.e., by weight/quantity. Identify the core difference: value vs. physical quantity.
The term ḍamm refers to combining two distinct types of wealth to reach a single nisab.
3
Trace the illa (cause) for each view
For Abu Hanifa, the illa is thamaniyyah (being a price-standard), which is realized by value, not form. For the Sahibayn, the illa is the quantity (weight) because the Shariah considers the weight of gold and silver (e.g., nisab is defined in weight). Understand how each derives the illa from the same subject matter.
This is a qiyās khafī (subtle analogy) based on the nature of money.
4
Apply to a concrete example
The passage gives the example: 10 dinars (gold) and 90 dirhams (silver) whose value equals 10 dinars. Under Abu Hanifa, zakat is wajib because the gold nisab (20 dinars) is reached in value. Under the Sahibayn, it is not wajib because neither metal reaches its own nisab in weight (gold: 10 < 20 dinars; silver: 90 < 200 dirhams). Work through this calculation.
If the silver were 100 dirhams (half nisab) and gold 10 dinars (half nisab), both agree zakat is due because by parts they form one complete nisab.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical rule
Later Hanafi practice generally follows Abu Hanifa's view of addition by value, as it is more beneficial to the poor and aligns with the thamaniyyah reasoning. Also, trade goods are added to gold and silver by value. If each asset alone reaches its nisab, they are not added but zakat is paid separately; however, one may pay all zakat from one type.
The valuation should be what is more beneficial for the poor in terms of circulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقصان النصاب في الحول هَدْر. قوله : ونقصان النصاب ... الخ؛ حاصله : أنه يشترط لوجوب الزكاة كمال النصاب في طرفي الحول في الابتداء للانعقاد، وفي الانتهاء للوجوب، فلا يضر نقصان فيما بينهما فلو هلك كله في أثناء الحول بطل الحول. ويُضَمَّ الذَّهب إلى الفضة، والعروض إليهما بالقيمة. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما فيضم الذهب إلى الفضَّةِ بالأجزاء حتى لو كان له عشرة دنانير وتسعون درهما قيمتها عشرة دنانير تجب عنده لا عندهما. أما إذا كان له عشرة دنانير ومئة درهم يجوز باتفاقهم، أما عندهما فللضم بالأجزاء، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فمئة درهم إن كان قيمته عشرة دنانير فظاهر، وإن كانت أكثر فكذلك؛ لوجود نصاب الذهب من حيث القيمة فتجب الزكاة، وإن كانت أقل فيكون قيمة عشرة دنانير أكثر من قيمة مئة درهم ضرورة، فتجب باعتبار وجود نصاب الفضة من حيث القيمة. قوله : ويضم الذهب إلى الفضة، وكذا الفضة إلى الذهب، وقيمة العرض للتجارة تضم إلى الثَّمنين، وكذا العكس، وهذا عند الاجتماع إذا لم يكن منهما نصاباً بأن كان أقل، فإذا كان كل منهما نصاباً تاماً بدون زيادة لا يجب الضم بل ينبغي أن تؤدى زكاة كل على حدة، فلو ضم حتى يؤدِّيَ كلَّه من الذهب أو الفضة فلا بأس به عندنا، ولكن أن يكون التقويم بما هو أنفعُ للفقراء رواجاً. كذا في «البدائع»، وغيره. قوله : بالأجزاء؛ لكون المعتبر فيهما القدر لا القيمة، حتى لا تجب الزكاة في مصوغ وزنُهُ أقل من مئتين، وقيمة فوقهما. وله : أنَّ الضمّ للمجانسة في الثَّمنيّة، وهي تتحقق باعتبار القيمة دون الصورة فيضم بها. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : فللضم بالاجزاء؛ فإنَّ مئة درهم نصف نصاب بالفضة وعشرة دنانير نصف نصاب الذهب، فبالضم يوجد نصاب واحد. قوله : فظاهر؛ أي وجوبُ الزَّكاة فيه لتمام نصاب الذهب. قوله : ضرورة؛ إذ لو كانت قيمة عشرة دنانير بقدر مئة درهم لبيعت مئة أيضاً بعشرة دنانير، لو كانت بأكثر من عشرة دينار.
And deficiency of the nisab during the year is disregarded. He said: 'And deficiency of the nisab...' The summary: that for the obligation of zakat, completion of the nisab at both ends of the year is required: at the beginning for its establishment, and at the end for obligation. So deficiency in between does not harm. If all is destroyed during the year, the year is invalidated. And gold is added to silver, and trade goods to both by value. This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for them (the Sahibayn), gold is added to silver by parts. So if he has ten dinars and ninety dirhams whose value is ten dinars, zakat is obligatory according to him, not according to them. But if he has ten dinars and one hundred dirhams, it is permissible by agreement. According to them, it is because of addition by parts; according to Abu Hanifa, if one hundred dirhams is equal in value to ten dinars, it is apparent; if more, likewise because the existence of the gold nisab in terms of value, so zakat is obligatory. If it is less, then the value of ten dinars is necessarily more than the value of one hundred dirhams, so zakat is obligatory based on the existence of the silver nisab in terms of value. He said: 'And gold is added to silver, and likewise silver to gold, and the value of trade goods for business is added to the two price-standard metals, and vice versa. This is when they are combined and neither alone reaches a nisab but is less. If each reaches a full nisab without addition, addition is not obligatory; rather, zakat of each should be paid separately. If he adds so that he pays all from gold or silver, there is no harm according to us, but the valuation should be what is more beneficial for the poor in terms of circulation. Thus in al-Bada'i' and others.' He said: 'By parts' because what is considered is quantity, not value, such that zakat is not obligatory on jewelry whose weight is less than two hundred (dirhams) but whose value is above them. And for him (Abu Hanifa), addition is due to homogeneity in being a price standard, which is realized by value, not form, so he adds by value. Thus in al-Hidayah. He said: 'So addition is by parts because one hundred dirhams is half the silver nisab and ten dinars is half the gold nisab, so by addition one nisab exists.' He said: 'Apparent' means zakat is obligatory because the gold nisab is complete. He said: 'Necessarily' because if the value of ten dinars were equal to one hundred dirhams, one hundred would also be sold for ten dinars; if it were more than ten dinars.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage consolidates two key rulings: the condition of nisab at both ends of the year, and the method of adding gold, silver, and trade goods together for zakat. For the nisab condition, the text explains that for zakat to be wajib, the nisab must be present at the beginning of the year (to start the year count) and at the end (for obligation to actualize). A decrease in between does not invalidate, but total loss during the year breaks the year. For mixing: according to Abu Hanifa, gold and silver are added by their value (qīmah), not by weight. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that they are added by parts (ajzā'), meaning by weight (e.g., half a nisab of each qualifies). The practical difference: if someone has 10 dinars (gold) and 90 dirhams (silver), and the 90 dirhams are worth 10 dinars, Abu Hanifa says zakat is due because the value of the gold nisab exists; the Sahibayn say no because neither reaches its nisab in weight. However, if the silver is 100 dirhams (half nisab of silver) and gold is 10 dinars (half nisab of gold), both agree that zakat is due (by adding by parts). The commentary also addresses mixing trade goods: their value is added to gold and silver. If each asset alone reaches the nisab, they should be zakat separately; but one may pay all from one type.

Key Terms
ضم (ḍamm): Adding different types of wealth (gold, silver, trade goods) together to reach the nisab for zakat.
ثمنية (thamaniyyah): The quality of being a price-standard (used as money). Both gold and silver share this property.
أجزاء (ajzā'): Parts; here meaning by weight or quantity, not by value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, a person has 8 dinars (gold) and 160 dirhams (silver). The value of 8 dinars is 80 dirhams. Is zakat wajib?
A) Yes, because the combined value (8 dinars + 160 dirhams = 240 dirhams) exceeds silver nisab
B) No, because neither alone reaches its nisab.
C) Yes, because 8 dinars is close to gold nisab.
D) No, because gold and silver are separate.

Key Takeaway
Gold and silver are added by value in the mufta bihi view, making zakat due when the combined market value reaches the nisab of either metal.

Combining Gold and Silver for Zakat: Value vs Weight
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining assets by value vs weight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on combining gold and silver for zakat, and apply the rule to determine zakat obligation in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are combined by value (Abu Hanifa) or by weight (Sahibayn); the difference turns on whether the operative cause is monetary function or physical quantity.

Zakat on monetary assets like gold and silver requires a minimum threshold (nisab). But what if you own less than nisab of each, but their combined value reaches the threshold? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This lesson examines the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the core question: How are different asset types (gold, silver, trade goods) combined for zakat—by their actual weight (ajza') or by their market value (qimah)?
This only applies when each type alone is less than nisab.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that combining is by value due to the commonality of being a medium of exchange (thamaniyya). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that combining is by weight, giving primacy to the actual quantity.
Both positions are within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the proofs
For Abu Hanifa, the illa (effective cause) is thamaniyya, which is realized through value, not form. For the Sahibayn, the nisab is defined by weight, so the same consideration must apply when combining.
The passage cites al-Hidaya for Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Work through the examples
Apply both views to the examples: 10 dinars + 90 dirhams (value = 10 dinars): zakat due according to Abu Hanifa only. 10 dinars + 100 dirhams: agreed both sides.
The text also discusses the condition that nisab must be present at beginning and end of the year.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if combined value reaches nisab but neither type individually reaches half nisab by weight, zakat is due according to Abu Hanifa but not the Sahibayn.
Trade goods are also combined by value according to Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويضم الذهب إلى الفضة والعروض إليهما بالقيمة
هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما فيضم الذهب إلى الفضة بالأجزاء حتى لو كان له عشرة دنانير وتسعون درهما قيمتها عشرة دنانير تجب عنده لا عندهما. أما إذا كان له عشرة دنانير ومئة درهم يجوز باتفاقهم، أما عندهما فللضم بالأجزاء، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فمئة درهم إن كان قيمته عشرة دنانير فظاهر، وإن كانت أكثر فكذلك؛ لوجود نصاب الذهب من حيث القيمة فتجب الزكاة، وإن كانت أقل فيكون قيمة عشرة دنانير أكثر من قيمة مئة درهم ضرورة، فتجب باعتبار وجود نصاب الفضة من حيث القيمة. النصاب في طرفي الحول في الابتداء للانعقاد، وفي الانتهاء للوجوب، فلا يضر نقصان فيما بينهما فلو هلك كله في أثناء الحول بطل الحول.
قوله: ويضم الذهب إلى الفضة، وكذا الفضة إلى الذهب، وقيمة العرض للتجارة تضم إلى الثمنين، وكذا العكس، وهذا عند الاجتماع إذا لم يكن منهما نصاباً بأن كان أقل، فإذا كان كل منهما نصاباً تاماً بدون زيادة لا يجب الضم بل ينبغي أن تؤدى زكاة كل على حدة، فلو ضم حتى يؤدي كله من الذهب أو الفضة فلا بأس به عندنا، ولكن أن يكون التقويم بما هو أنفع للفقراء رواجاً. كذا في البدائع وغيره.
قوله: بالأجزاء؛ لكون المعتبر فيهما القدر لا القيمة، حتى لا تجب الزكاة في مصوغ وزنه أقل من مئتين، وقيمة فوقهما. وله: أن الضم للمجانسة في الثمنية، وهي تتحقق باعتبار القيمة دون الصورة فيضم بها. كذا في الهداية.
قوله: فللضم بالأجزاء؛ فإن مئة درهم نصف نصاب بالفضة وعشرة دنانير نصف نصاب الذهب، فبالضم يوجد نصاب واحد.
قوله: فظاهر؛ أي وجوب الزكاة فيه لتمام نصاب الذهب.
قوله: ضرورة؛ إذ لو كانت قيمة عشرة دنانير بقدر مئة درهم لبيعت مئة أيضاً بعشرة دنانير، لو كانت بأكثر من عشرة دينار.
ولا تجب الزكاة عندنا في نصاب مشترك من سائمة ومال تجارة وإن صحت الخلطة. ينظر: الدر المختار (ص 34). (بدائع الصنائع 2:20). (الهداية 2:223).
Gold is combined with silver, and trade goods with both, by value. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), gold is combined with silver by parts (weight), such that if he has ten dinars and ninety dirhams whose value is ten dinars, zakat is due according to him (Abu Hanifa) but not according to them. If he has ten dinars and one hundred dirhams, it is agreed upon. According to them, due to combining by parts; and according to Abu Hanifa, one hundred dirhams if its value is ten dinars, it is apparent; and if more, likewise due to the existence of the nisab of gold in terms of value, so zakat is obligatory. If it is less, then the value of ten dinars is more than the value of one hundred dirhams necessarily, so zakat is due considering the existence of the nisab of silver in terms of value. The nisab is considered at the two ends of the year: at the beginning for commencement, and at the end for obligation. So a decrease in between does not harm. If it all perishes during the year, the year is void. His statement: 'And gold is combined with silver...' etc. His statement: 'by parts' because consideration is of quantity not value, such that no zakat is due on jewelry weighing less than two hundred (dirhams) but of value above. And to him (Abu Hanifa): combining is for homogeneity in being a medium of exchange (thamaniyya), which is realized by consideration of value not form, so combining is by value. Thus in al-Hidaya. His statement: 'So combining by parts' because one hundred dirhams is half nisab of silver and ten dinars is half nisab of gold, so by combining there is one complete nisab. His statement: 'apparent' meaning obligation of zakat due to completion of nisab of gold. His statement: 'necessarily' because if the value of ten dinars equaled one hundred dirhams, one hundred would also be sold for ten dinars; if it were more than ten dinars. And zakat is not obligatory according to us in a combined nisab of grazing livestock and trade goods, even if the mixing is valid. Refer to al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Badai' al-Sanai', al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the ruling of combining gold and silver for zakat calculation. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that different types of assets (gold, silver, trade goods) are combined by value (qimah) to complete the nisab threshold, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that gold and silver are combined by weight (ajza'), not value. The passage then provides examples to illustrate the practical difference. It explains that according to Abu Hanifa, if one has ten dinars (gold) and ninety dirhams (silver) whose value equals ten dinars, zakat is due because the value of the gold and silver together reaches nisab. But according to the two, since ninety dirhams is less than the silver nisab (200 dirhams) and ten dinars is less than the gold nisab (20 dinars), they are not combined by value, so no zakat is due. However, if one has ten dinars and one hundred dirhams, both schools agree that zakat is due because one hundred dirhams is half the silver nisab, and ten dinars is half the gold nisab, so when combined by weight they complete one nisab (according to the two) and by value (according to Abu Hanifa). The passage also discusses the condition that the nisab must be present at both the beginning and end of the year; a decrease in the middle does not affect the obligation. The commentary then clarifies the reasoning: the two consider quantity, so they require the actual weight to reach nisab. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is based on the commonality of being a medium of exchange (thamaniyya), which is realized through value, not physical form. Therefore, if the value of gold and silver together equals the nisab, they are combined. The passage ends with a note that zakat is not obligatory on a mixed nisab of grazing livestock and trade goods according to the Hanafi school, even if the mixing is valid.

Key Terms
الضم (al-damm): Combining different asset types (gold, silver, trade goods) to complete the nisab for zakat.
الأجزاء (al-ajza'): Combining by weight (actual quantity) rather than value.
الثمنية (al-thamaniyya): The quality of being a medium of exchange (monetary value).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how are gold and silver combined for zakat?
A) By weight (ajza')
B) By value (qimah)
C) Only if each individually reaches nisab
D) They are not combined at all

Key Takeaway
The method of combining gold and silver for zakat—by value or by weight—depends on whether the operative cause is seen as monetary function or physical quantity, leading to different obligations in cases where combined value reaches nisab but not weight.

The Collector of Commercial Tithe (al-Ashara): Conditions and Authority
Defines the collector, conditions, and oath ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for the appointment of the collector (al-ashara) and explain how his authority relates to the merchant's denial of the haw with an oath.

Core Idea
A collector must be free, Muslim, and capable; a merchant's denial of haw requires an oath.

This lesson defines the role of the collector of commercial tithe (al-ashara) as appointed by the imam. It specifies the legal conditions for his appointment—freedom, Islam, and capability—and clarifies his authority over passing goods. A key ruling is that if a merchant denies the completion of the year, the collector accepts his statement with an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Read the first sentence and commentary to understand who al-ashara is and why he is called by that name.
Note the derivation from 'ushr (tenth) even though he may take less.
2
Extract the three conditions
Locate the conditions explicitly stated: free (not slave), Muslim, and capable of protection against thieves.
These are conditions for valid appointment; if any is missing, his action is invalid.
3
Distinguish al-ashara from al-sa'i
Understand that al-sa'i is sent to tribes for livestock zakat, while al-ashara is on roads for commercial goods.
The term 'on the road' (على الطريق) excludes the sa'i.
4
Understand the scope of taking
The collector takes from all wealth that passes by him, both apparent and hidden, but not from hidden wealth that does not pass.
If informed about hidden wealth that didn't pass, he cannot take it.
5
Apply the oath ruling
If a merchant denies that the haw (year) has completed, the collector accepts his statement with an oath, because the merchant is a denier.
This follows the principle: the denier is believed with an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العاشر
هو من نصب على الطريق لأخذ صدقة التجار. وصدق مع اليمين من أنكر منهم تمام الحول
قوله: باب العاشر؛ هو اسم لمن يأخذ العشر ونصفه وربعه، سمي به مع أنه لا يأخذ العشر لدوران العشر في متعلق أخذه، كذا في فتح القدير، وغيره، وهذا أولى مما يقال: إنه تسمية ما قيل أن العشر اسم لما يأخذه العاشر وإن كان أقل منه.
قوله: من نصب؛ بصيغة المجهول، أي من جانب الإمام، ويشترط فيه أن يكون حراً لا عبداً؛ لأنه من باب الولاية، ولا ولاية للعبد، وأن يكون مسلماً لا كافراً؛ لأنه لا يلي على المسلم، وأن يكون قادراً على الحماية من اللصوص؛ لأن الأخذ مبني عليها، كذا البحر وغيره.
قوله: على الطريق؛ خرج بهذا القيد الساعي، وهو الذي يبعثه الإمام في القبائل؛ لتحصيل الصدقات من المواشي في إمكانها، واسم المصدق والعامل يشملهما.
قوله: صدقة؛ في هذا اللفظ تغليب؛ فإن العاشر يأخذ من الكافر أيضاً وهو ليس بصدقة، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنه يأخذ من كل مال مر به عليه، سواء كان من الأموال الظاهرة كالمواشي أو الباطنة كالذهب والفضة، أما الباطنة التي لم يمر بها عليه لو أخبر بها العاشر لا يأخذ منها، ولا يشترط للأخذ المرور بها عليه في الظاهر؛ فإن له أن يأخذ منه وإن لم يمر بها عليه كذا في البناية.
قوله: من أنكر؛ يعني إذا مر تاجر على العاشر بمال فأراد العاشر أخذ العشر منه، فقال: لم يتم الحول على هذا المال، فلا يجب عليّ فيه شيء، صدقه العاشر مع يمينه؛ لأنه منكر، والمنكر يصدق باليمين.
Chapter of al-Ashara. He is one appointed on the road to take the sadaqah of merchants. And he is believed with an oath the one who denies among them the completion of the year. His statement: 'Chapter of al-Ashara': it is a name for one who takes a tenth, half a tenth, or a quarter of a tenth. He is called by this even though he does not take a tenth, because the tenth revolves around the object of his taking. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and others. This is better than what is said that it is a naming from what is said that 'al-ashr' is a name for what the collector takes even if it is less than that. His statement: 'who is appointed' in passive voice, i.e. by the imam. Conditions for him: he must be free, not a slave, because it is a form of authority (wilaya), and a slave has no authority; he must be Muslim, not a disbeliever, because a disbeliever cannot have authority over a Muslim; and he must be capable of protection against thieves, because taking is built upon that. Thus in al-Bahr and others. His statement: 'on the road' excludes by this restriction the sa'i, who is the one sent by the imam to the tribes to collect zakat on livestock in their places; the terms musaddiq and 'amil include both. His statement: 'sadaqah': in this expression there is predominance (taghlib); for the collector takes also from the disbeliever, which is not sadaqah. And in the absolute term there is indication that he takes from every wealth that passes by him, whether apparent wealth like livestock or hidden like gold and silver. As for hidden wealth that did not pass by him, if the collector is informed of it, he does not take from it. And it is not required for taking that the wealth passes by him in apparent form; for he may take from him even if it did not pass by him. Thus in al-Binaya. His statement: 'the one who denies': meaning if a merchant passes by the collector with wealth and the collector wants to take the tenth from him, and he says: 'The year has not completed for this wealth, so nothing is due from me,' the collector believes him with his oath, because he is a denier, and the denier is believed with an oath.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on al-Ashara, the collector of commercial taxes. It defines him as a person appointed by the imam (ruler) to collect sadaqah (zakat/tithe) from merchants at a road checkpoint. The commentary explains the term 'ashara' as derived from the tithe ('ushr) he takes, even though he may take less than a tenth. The conditions for his appointment are: he must be a free person (not a slave), because authority cannot be delegated to slaves; he must be Muslim, as a non-Muslim cannot have authority over Muslims; and he must be physically capable of protecting against thieves, as collection relies on security. The term 'on the road' distinguishes him from the sa'i, who is sent to tribes to collect zakat from livestock. The word 'sadaqah' is used generically, since the collector also takes from non-Muslims (jizya? Actually from non-Muslim merchants, the tax is not sadaqah but rather a custom duty). The passage clarifies that the collector can take from all types of wealth that pass him, whether apparent (livestock) or hidden (gold/silver), but with a nuance: if hidden wealth does not physically pass by him and he is informed of it, he cannot take it; however, if it does pass by him, he can take it even if it is hidden. The ruling on oath: if a merchant denies that the wealth has completed a year (hawl), the collector accepts his statement with an oath, because the merchant is a denier (munkir) and the default is that the denier is believed with an oath. This is in accordance with the principle that the burden of proof is on the claimant, and an oath is on the denier.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-ashara): The person appointed by the imam to collect commercial tithes from merchants passing on roads.
الساعي (al-sa'i): The person sent by the imam to collect zakat from livestock in tribes, as opposed to the road collector.
التغليب (al-taghlib): Using a term in a predominant sense; here 'sadaqah' is used even when taking from non-Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He must be a merchant himself

Key Takeaway
The collector's authority is conditional on his freedom, Islam, and capability; a merchant's denial of haw is accepted with an oath.

Why the Tax Collector is Called 'Ashir
Defines the term 'ashir and explains its naming rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'ashir in Hanafi fiqh and explain the rationale for its naming.

Core Idea
The 'ashir is named for the tenth even when collecting less, because the tenth is the basis of his office.

This brief passage defines the chapter title 'Bab al-Ashir' and clarifies why the tax collector is called 'ashir. It resolves a potential confusion: the collector may take less than a tenth, so why call him 'ashir? The Hanafi scholars provide a precise linguistic and legal rationale grounded in the revolving concept of the tenth ('ushr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'ashir in the passage. Note that it is the subject of the chapter title and the definition.
The term appears in the phrase 'Bab al-Ashir'.
2
Understand the literal vs technical meaning
The literal meaning is 'one who takes ten'. The technical meaning in Hanafi fiqh is broader: a collector appointed to take a portion (tenth, half, quarter) from trade goods.
The passage explicitly mentions 'half of it' and 'quarter of it'.
3
Grasp the rationale for the naming
The passage explains that the name 'ashir persists even when less than a tenth is taken because the concept of the tenth (al-ushr) 'revolves' around his work — his office is fundamentally tied to the idea of the tenth.
The term 'revolving' (دوران) indicates the underlying basis, not the exact amount.
4
Compare with the alternative explanation
The passage mentions an alternative: some say the name 'ashir is used because what he takes is called 'ushr, even if it is less than a tenth. The author prefers the first explanation as more sound.
The preferred view is from Fath al-Qadir and others.
5
State the preferred view
The Hanafi position is that the term 'ashir derives from the revolving of the tenth in his office, not from the precise amount collected.
This definition is important for understanding the scope of the 'ashir's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العاشر [1] قوله : باب العاشر ؛ هو اسمٌ لِمَن يأخذ العشر ونصفه وربعه، سُمِّي به مع أنه لا يأخذ العشر لدوران العشرِ في متعلق أخذه ، كذا في «فتح القدير»، وغيره، وهذا أولى مما يقال : إنه تسمية ما قيل أن العشر اسم لما يأخذه العاشر وإن كان أقل منه.
The chapter of the 'ashir. He said: The chapter of the 'ashir; it is a name for one who takes the tenth, half of it, and a quarter of it. He is named thus even though he does not take the tenth due to the revolving of the tenth in what he takes, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. And this is better than what is said: that it is a name such that it is said that the tenth is a name for what the 'ashir takes even if it is less than that.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the chapter title 'Bab al-Ashir'. The commentator explains that the 'ashir (tax collector) is so named because he is appointed to collect a tenth (or less) from merchants' goods. Even though he may collect half a tenth or a quarter, the term 'ashir is used because his office revolves around the concept of the tenth (ushr). This is a definition ruling: the name is based on the typical object of collection, not the exact amount. The author prefers this explanation over the alternative that the word 'ushr is used for whatever the 'ashir takes, even if less.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-ashir): The tax collector appointed by the imam to take a portion (tenth, half tenth, etc.) from merchants' goods.
العشر (al-ushr): The tenth; the standard rate of collection from trade goods passed through the 'ashir, though the actual rate may vary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The term 'ashir in Hanafi fiqh refers to a tax collector whose office is defined by the concept of the tenth, not by the exact amount collected.

Conditions for Appointment of the 'Ashir
States conditions for the 'ashir: free, Muslim, capable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for appointing a zakat collector ('ashir) and distinguish him from the sa'i.

Core Idea
The 'ashir must be free, Muslim, and capable — because collection involves wilayah and risk.

The 'ashir is a critical official in the zakat administration, responsible for collecting dues from trade goods at road checkpoints. The Hanafi school stipulates specific conditions for his appointment to ensure the integrity and effectiveness of the collection. This lesson dissects those conditions and clarifies how the 'ashir differs from other collectors like the sa'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of the 'ashir
Understand that the 'ashir is appointed by the imam to collect zakat on trade goods from passing merchants on the road. This is a position of governance (wilayah).
The word 'ashir comes from 'ushr (tithe), but here it refers to the collector of zakat on trade.
2
List the three conditions
From the text, extract the three requirements: freedom, Islam, and capability (to protect against thieves). Write them down in order.
Each condition has a specific rationale given in the passage.
3
Understand the rationale for each condition
For each condition, explain why it is necessary: (1) Freedom because wilayah requires full legal capacity; (2) Islam because a non-Muslim cannot have authority over a Muslim; (3) Capability because the collector must safeguard the collected goods.
The rationale is derived from the phrase 'because it is from the category of wilayah' and similar phrases.
4
Differentiate between 'ashir and sa'i
Note that the sa'i is a different type of collector sent to tribes to collect zakat on livestock at the pastures. The 'ashir is fixed on roads for trade goods.
The text explicitly excludes the sa'i by the condition 'on the road'.

Both are types of collectors, but the 'ashir is stationed on roads.

Scope of the 'Ashir's Collection: Taghleeb and Types of Wealth
Explains taghleeb and scope of 'ashir collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of taghleeb in the term 'sadaqah' as it applies to the 'ashir's collection, and distinguish between apparent and hidden wealth for taxation.

Core Idea
The 'ashir uses taghleeb to collect from all wealth passing by, including non-Muslims and both visible and hidden assets.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the term 'sadaqah' in the base text is used loosely (taghleeb) because the 'ashir also collects from non-Muslims, which is a tax, not charity. This broad application extends to all wealth that physically passes by the collector, whether livestock or gold, but with an important exception for hidden wealth that never passes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and commentator's focus
The base text uses the word 'sadaqah'. The commentator points out that this is an instance of taghleeb (generalization). Your task is to locate the word and understand why taghleeb is needed here.
2
Understand taghleeb as a legal principle
Taghleeb means using a term in a broader sense than its literal meaning. Here, 'sadaqah' literally means charity, but it is used to include the tax taken from non-Muslims. Identify the specific example given.
3
Distinguish between apparent and hidden wealth
The passage states that the 'ashir takes from both apparent wealth (e.g., livestock) and hidden wealth (e.g., gold and silver). But note the condition that hidden wealth must pass by the 'ashir. If it does not pass, it is not collected even if the 'ashir knows about it.
For apparent wealth, passing is sufficient even if the goods themselves are not seen.
4
Identify the exception for hidden wealth that did not pass
The exception is crucial: if the hidden wealth never passed by the 'ashir, he cannot take it. This protects the owner's privacy and prevents abuse. Confirm this by reading the sentence on hidden wealth that 'did not pass by him'.
This is a unique Hanafi nuance.
5
Apply the condition to a hypothetical
Imagine a merchant passes by with gold hidden in his saddlebag that the 'ashir does not see. Apply the rule: does the gold 'pass by'? The text says passing by the 'ashir is not limited to physical sight; the merchant passing is sufficient. So the gold does pass. But if the gold never passes at all, then no collection.
The nuance: 'passing by' is satisfied if the person carrying the wealth passes, even if the wealth is not seen. But if the wealth is not carried past, it does not pass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] قوله : صدقة ؛ في هذا اللفظ تغليب ؛ فإنَّ العاشر يأخذ من الكافر أيضاً وهو ليس بصدقة، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنه يأخذ من كلّ مال مرَّ به عليه، سواء كان من الأموال الظاهرة كالمواشي أو الباطنة كالذهب والفضة ، أما الباطنة التي لم يمر بها عليه لو أخبر بها العاشر لا يأخذ منها ، ولا يشترط للأخذ المرور بها عليه في الظاهر ؛ فإنَّ له أن يأخذ منه وإن لم يمر بها عليه كذا في «البناية».
[4] His saying: 'sadaqah'; in this word there is taghleeb (predominance/generalization), for the 'ashir takes also from the disbeliever, which is not sadaqah. And in the absolute expression there is an indication that he takes from every wealth that passes by him, whether it be of apparent wealth like livestock or hidden wealth like gold and silver. As for hidden wealth that did not pass by him, if the 'ashir is informed of it, he does not take from it. And it is not a condition for taking that the wealth passes by him in appearance; for he may take from him even if it did not pass by him. Such is in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the word 'sadaqah' in the matn. The commentator notes that the term is used by way of taghleeb (predominance/generalization), because the 'ashir also collects from non-Muslims, and that payment is not technically sadaqah (charity) but a tax. So the term is used loosely. The absolute phrasing indicates that the 'ashir collects from all types of wealth that pass by him on the road, whether apparent (like livestock) or hidden (like gold and silver). However, if hidden wealth does not pass by him and he later learns of it, he cannot take it. Interestingly, it is not a condition that the goods physically pass by; if the merchant himself passes by with the wealth, the 'ashir can take even if the goods are not physically present—meaning the obligation attaches upon the merchant's passing. This is based on the principle that the 'ashir is stationed on the road to collect from merchants who pass.

Key Terms
تغليب (taghleeb): Predominance or generalization; using a term in a broader sense than its literal meaning.
الأموال الباطنة (al-amwal al-batinah): Hidden wealth, such as gold and silver that are not openly displayed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because sadaqah is only for Muslims.
B) Yes, because the term sadaqah is used loosely and includes the tax.
C) No, non-Muslims pay jizya separately.
D) Yes, but only if they are dhimmis.

Key Takeaway
The term 'sadaqah' in the context of the 'ashir is broadened via taghleeb to include taxation of non-Muslims, and the 'ashir may only collect from wealth that passes by him, with an exception for hidden wealth that never passes.

Believing the Merchant's Denial of the Full Year
Ruling on accepting merchant's denial of the year with oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a merchant denies the completion of the year for zakat on trade goods and explain why the denier is believed with an oath.

Core Idea
A merchant's denial of the year for zakat is accepted with an oath — the denier is believed.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a dispute arises about whether a full lunar year (hawl) has passed for trade goods, the merchant's denial is given weight. The 'ashir (tax collector) must accept the merchant's statement that the year is incomplete, provided the merchant swears an oath. This ruling is based on the general principle that a denier (munkir) is believed with an oath, as the burden of proof lies on the claimant. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'ayah clarifies this specific application in zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
Determine who is involved: a merchant and an 'ashir. The 'ashir wants to take 'ushr (zakat) on trade goods; the merchant denies the year has passed.
2
State the ruling
The 'ashir must believe the merchant with an oath. The ruling is clear: 'the 'ashir believes him with an oath.'
3
Extract the legal basis
The merchant is a denier (munkir). The general principle is that a denier is believed with an oath because the burden of proof is on the claimant.
This principle applies across all claims in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
If a debtor denies a loan, the same principle applies: his denial is accepted with an oath, placing the burden on the creditor to prove the debt.
This shows the universality of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[0] قوله : من أنكر ؛ يعني إذا مرَّ تاجر على العاشر بمال فأراد العاشر أخذ العشر منه ، فقال : لم يتم الحول على هذا المال، فلا يجب عليّ فيه شيء، صدقه العاشر مع يمينه ؛ لأنه منكر، والمنكر يصدق باليمين.
[0] His saying: 'who denies'; that is, when a merchant passes by the 'ashir with wealth, and the 'ashir wants to take the tenth from him, and he says: The year has not completed for this wealth, so nothing is obligatory on me regarding it; the 'ashir believes him with his oath, because he is a denier, and the denier is believed with an oath.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a merchant denies that a full year (hawl) has passed since he obtained the goods, thus claiming that zakat is not yet due. The rule is that the 'ashir must accept this claim if the merchant takes an oath. The reason given is that the merchant is a denier (munkir), and the general principle of fiqh is that a denier is believed with an oath, as the burden of proof is on the claimant. This applies specifically to the 'ashir-merchant relationship. The commentator uses the term 'munkir' (denier) to indicate that the merchant is not admitting the debt but denying its existence.

Key Terms
منكر (munkir): A person who denies a claim against him. In this context, the merchant denies the obligation of zakat.
الحول (al-hawl): The lunar year that must pass for zakat to be obligatory on trade goods.
العاشر (al-'ashir): The tax collector responsible for collecting 'ushr (tenth) on trade goods.
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by the denier to support his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a merchant says the year has not completed, what must the 'ashir do?
A) Take the tenth anyway.
B) Believe him without oath.
C) Believe him with an oath.
D) Confirm with witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a merchant's denial of the completion of the year for zakat is accepted when accompanied by an oath, because the denier is believed with an oath, shifting the burden to the claimant.

Claim of Zakat Payment to a Poor Person in the City
Ruling on claim of zakat payment to a poor person in the city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a merchant's claim of paying zakat directly to a poor person in the city, distinguish between sa'imah and non-sa'imah, and identify the condition of payment being within the city.

Core Idea
For sa'imah, only the sultan can collect zakat; direct payment to the poor is void.

Zakat on grazing livestock (sa'imah) follows a unique rule: the owner cannot distribute it himself. The sultan (or his agent) must collect and distribute it. If a merchant claims to have paid sa'imah zakat directly to a poor person in the city, his claim is rejected and he must pay again to the sultan. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and the crucial condition that the payment must be made within the city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its context
The passage begins with a scenario where a merchant claims to have paid his zakat obligation to a poor person in the city. We need to determine whether this claim is accepted.
This is a common scenario in trade: merchants often handle their own zakat distribution.
2
Distinguish sa'imah from non-sa'imah
The ruling differs based on the type of wealth. For non-sa'imah (e.g., trade goods, cash), the merchant is believed with an oath if he claims payment to a poor person in the city. For sa'imah (grazing livestock), the claim is not accepted.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Understand the condition 'fi misr' (in the city)
The passage emphasizes that the claim is only considered if the payment was made within the city. If the wealth was taken out of the city, it is treated like apparent wealth (zahir), over which the imam has primary collection rights. Thus, any previous payment is void.
This prevents evasion of zakat by moving wealth outside the city's jurisdiction.
4
Analyze the internal dispute
There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: some say the merchant's direct payment is the actual zakat and the sultan's taking is merely policy; others say the merchant's payment is void and only the sultan's taking is zakat. The author of al-Hidayah affirms the latter.
This dispute affects whether the merchant has fulfilled his obligation or must pay again.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position is that for sa'imah, the sultan's taking constitutes the zakat. The merchant's direct payment is void, and he must pay again if the sultan demands it.
This ruling is specific to sa'imah; for non-sa'imah, direct payment is acceptable within the city.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الفراغ عن الدين، أو ادعى أداءه الى فقير في مصر في غير السوائم) حتّى إذا ادعى الأداء إلى فقير في مصر في السوائم لا يُصَدَّقُ إذ ليس له في السوائمِ الأداء إلى الفقير ، بل يأخذ منه السلطان، ويصرفُهُ إلى مصرفه [1] قوله : أو ادعى ؛ يعني قال صاحب المال : قد أديت ما وجب عليَّ في هذا المال إلى الفقراء في المصر، يُصدق مع اليمين. [٢] قوله : في مصر ؛ قيد به لأنه لو ادعى أداءه بعد الإخراج من المصر لا يعتبر بقوله ؛ لأنَّ الأموال الباطنة بالإخراج تلتحق بالظاهرة، فكان الأخذ فيها للإمام فيأخذ منها العشر ثانياً، وإن كان أداه أولاً ؛ لكونه لغو. كذا في «البحر» وغيره. [٣] قوله : بل يأخذ منه السلطان ؛ أي أو نائبه كالعاشر وغيره، فلما كان حق الأخذ فيه له صارَ أداؤه سابقاً باطلاً ، ثم اختلف في أنَّ الزَّكَاةَ هل تكون ما أدّى إلى فقير، أو ما يأخذ الساعي، فقيل : الزَّكاةُ هو الأول، والثاني: سياسة، وقيل: الأوَّلُ لغو، والثاني : هو الزكاة، وصححه في «الهداية».
Or being free from debt, or he claims to have paid it to a poor person in the city regarding non-sa'imah (non-grazing livestock). So if he claims payment to a poor person in the city regarding sa'imah, he is not believed, because he has no right to pay to the poor for sa'imah; rather, the sultan takes it from him and spends it in its channels. [1] His saying: 'or he claims' means the owner of the wealth said: I have paid what was obligatory on me of this wealth to the poor in the city; he is believed with an oath. [2] His saying: 'in the city' is a condition because if he claims payment after taking (the wealth) out of the city, his statement is not considered, because hidden wealth upon being taken out becomes like apparent wealth, and the taking of it is for the imam. So he takes the tenth from it a second time, even if he had paid it previously, because it was void. Such is in al-Bahr and others. [3] His saying: 'rather, the sultan takes it from him' means his deputy like the 'ashir and others. Since the right of taking it belonged to him, his prior payment became void. Then there is a difference of opinion: is the zakat what is paid to a poor person, or what the collector takes? It is said: the first is the zakat and the second is policy; and it is said: the first is void and the second is the zakat, and this is corrected in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage deals with the claim that the merchant has already fulfilled his zakat obligation by paying directly to a poor person in the city. The ruling distinguishes between non-sa'imah (non-grazing livestock, such as trade goods) and sa'imah (grazing livestock). For non-sa'imah, the merchant is believed with an oath that he paid. However, for sa'imah, he is not believed because the right of collection belongs to the sultan (imam) alone; the merchant cannot directly distribute that zakat. Thus, if he claims to have paid sa'imah zakat to a poor person in the city, it is void, and the sultan (or his agent, the 'ashir) takes it again. The condition 'fi misr' (in the city) is crucial: if the merchant claims payment after having left the city with the goods, his claim is rejected because hidden wealth taken out of the city becomes like apparent wealth, over which the imam has the primary right of collection. There is also a scholarly dispute: some say the zakat is what the merchant pays himself, and the sultan's taking is merely policy; others say the merchant's payment is void and only the sultan's taking constitutes zakat. The author of al-Hidayah affirms the latter view.

Key Terms
السوائم (as-sawa'im): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep/goats) that are pasture-fed; their zakat must be collected by the imam's agent.
مصر (misr): A city or town; used to distinguish payment inside vs. outside the town.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is believed with an oath.
B) He is not believed, and the sultan takes it again.
C) The payment is valid if witnessed.
D) He must pay again only if he leaves the city.

Key Takeaway
For sa'imah, direct payment to the poor is invalid; the sultan's collection alone fulfills the zakat obligation.
