Claim of Payment to Another 'Ashir: Conditions for Acceptance
Ruling on claiming payment to another 'ashir in the same year.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which a merchant's claim of paying zakat to another 'ashir is accepted, and apply them to determine whether the claim is valid.

Core Idea
A claim of prior payment to an 'ashir is believed with oath only if the 'ashir was just and verified to exist that year.

In Hanafi zakat law, when a tax collector ('ashir) demands payment and the merchant claims he already paid to another collector earlier in the same year, the law provides a specific procedure. This lesson explains the two critical conditions for such a claim to be accepted: the justice of the previous collector and the verified existence of that collector in the same year. Understanding these conditions prevents unjust double payment while preserving the integrity of zakat collection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being made
The merchant asserts that he already paid zakat to another 'ashir in the same year. The current 'ashir must evaluate this claim.
The claim is about a past payment to a different collector, not to the demanding one.
2
Apply the condition of justice ('adl)
The 'ashir who allegedly received the payment must be a just collector ('adl). If he was from the Khawarij (rebels), the payment is not recognized.
Khawarij collectors are considered unjust; payment to them does not discharge the obligation.
3
Apply the condition of existence (wujud)
It must be verified that the other 'ashir actually existed as a collector in that year before the current demand.
If existence is unknown, the default (al-asl al-adam) is that there was no such 'ashir, so the claim is rejected.
4
Determine the outcome
If both conditions are met, the merchant is believed with an oath; if not, the current 'ashir collects the zakat again.
The merchant's oath is required; belief is not automatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عاشر آخر إن وُجِدَ في السنة) : أي إذا ادعى أداءه إلى عاشر آخر ، والحال أن عاشراً آخر موجود في هذه السنة [4] قوله : أو عاشر آخر؛ أي يصدقُ مع اليمين إذا ادعى أداءه إلى عاشر آخر في هذه السنة، لكن بشرط أن يكون عاشر أهل العدل، فإن كان عاشر الخوارج يؤخذ ثانياً. [0] قوله : موجود؛ أي تحقق أنَّ الآخر كان عاشراً في هذه السنة قبل هذا العاشر الطالب، فلو لم يدر هل كان هناك عاشر أم لا ، لم يُصدّق ؛ لأنَّ الأصلَ عدمه.
Or another 'ashir, if he exists in the year; that is, if he claims to have paid to another 'ashir, while another 'ashir exists in this year. [4] His saying: 'or another 'ashir'; that is, he is believed with an oath if he claims to have paid to another 'ashir in this year, but on condition that the 'ashir is one of the people of justice (just collectors). If he was an 'ashir of the Khawarij, it is taken a second time. [0] His saying: 'exists'; that is, it is verified that the other was an 'ashir in this year before this demanding 'ashir. So if he does not know whether there was another 'ashir or not, he is not believed, because the default is absence.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage covers the scenario where the merchant claims he has already paid the zakat to another 'ashir earlier in the same year. The ruling is that he is believed with an oath, provided that the other 'ashir was a just collector ('adl) from Ahl al-'Adl (people of justice). If the other was a Kharijite 'ashir (a rebel or unjust collector), the payment is not accepted, and the current 'ashir takes it again. Additionally, it must be verified that the other 'ashir actually existed in that year before the current 'ashir demanded payment. If it is unknown whether another 'ashir existed, the merchant is not believed, because the default (al-asl) is that no such 'ashir existed. The merchant's claim is then a claim against the default, which is not accepted without evidence.

Key Terms
عاشر الخوارج (ashir al-khawarij): A tax collector appointed by the Khawarij (rebel group); his collection is not valid.
الأصل العدم (al-asl al-adam): The legal principle that the default (original) state is non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Believe the merchant with oath.
B) Reject the claim and take the zakat again.
C) Accept if the Kharijite was popularly recognized.
D) Require two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A merchant's claim of prior payment to another 'ashir is accepted only if the previous 'ashir was just and his existence in that year is verified.

Claims Accepted Without Proof in Zakat Collection
Lists claims accepted under oath in zakat collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the seven claims accepted under oath that exempt a merchant from zakat.

Core Idea
The merchant's oath suffices for claims negating zakat; the collector accepts without proof.

In the Hanafi school, when a merchant's goods appear to be trade capital, the collector ('ashir) may assess zakat. However, the merchant can avoid this by swearing to certain circumstances that negate zakat liability. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya enumerates these claims, ranging from lack of trade intent to the goods being owned by someone else. The underlying principle is that the merchant knows his own situation, and the default is truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The merchant's word is accepted under oath because he is aware of his own intentions and obligations; the collector is not.
2
Categorize the claims into groups
Group them into claims about intention (no trade intent), ownership (deposit, consignment, mudarabah), debt (surrounds/reduces nisab), and status (hired worker, mukatab, ma'dhun slave).
3
Analyze each claim's legal effect
For each, understand why it negates zakat: e.g., 'no intent' means goods are not trade capital; 'debt' means nisab is not met; 'deposit' means goods are not owned by the merchant.
4
Note the authority
Al-Zayla'i (in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq) is the source of this list; it is an authoritative enumeration in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the principle
If a merchant makes any of these claims under oath, the collector must accept and not demand proof.
The oath is a condition; without it, the claim is not accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إذا قال: إنّي لم أنو التجارة، أو علي دين محيط، أو منقص للنصاب، أو ليس هذا المال لي، بل هو وديعة أو بضاعة أو مضاربة، أو أنا أجير فيه أو مكاتب أو عبد مأذون. كذا ذكره الزيلعي).
And likewise if he says: I did not intend trade, or I have a debt that surrounds (all assets) or reduces the nisab, or this wealth is not mine, rather it is a deposit (wadi'ah) or a commodity (bida'ah) or a mudarabah, or I am a hired labourer or a mukatab (slave with contract of manumission) or a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade). Such is mentioned by al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage enumerates additional claims by the merchant that, if made under oath, exempt him from zakat. These include: he did not intend to trade the goods (so they are not trade capital); he has debt that covers or reduces the nisab (minimum threshold); the goods belong to someone else as deposit (wadi'ah), consignment (bida'ah), or mudarabah (silent partnership); or he is a hired worker (ajir: using goods for work), a mukatab (slave seeking freedom paying installments, who cannot own trade capital freely), or a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade but claiming the goods are for his master). In all these cases, the merchant is believed with an oath, since the 'ashir has no evidence to the contrary. The authority for this list is al-Zayla'i (likely in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq). The underlying principle is that the merchant knows his own circumstances, and the default is that he is truthful.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to pay for his freedom in installments; he is not fully free to own trade goods for his own account.
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; his earnings belong to the master.
بضاعة (bida'ah): A consignment or commodity sent to another for sale; the goods remain the property of the sender.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A silent partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor; profits are shared.
أجير (ajir): A hired worker who uses goods in the course of employment; the goods belong to the employer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He is believed with an oath.
C) The 'ashir takes anyway because possession is evidence.
D) He must pay zakat as trade goods.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi zakat law, a merchant's oath suffices for specific claims negating zakat, reflecting the principle that the default is truthfulness.

Acceptance of Claims of Zakat Payment
Explains when a Muslim's claim of paying zakat is accepted with oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Muslim's claim of paying zakat is accepted with an oath, and distinguish between livestock and non-livestock, and between hidden and apparent wealth.

Core Idea
For non-livestock zakat within the city, the owner's claim of payment is accepted with oath; for livestock, the ruler collects so the claim is void.

In this passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah, we examine the Hanafi position on when a Muslim's claim of having paid zakat is legally accepted. The key distinction lies between livestock (sawa'im) and other wealth, and between wealth still within the city and wealth that has been removed. The commentator also notes an intra-school dispute about the nature of the zakat payment itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The text discusses claims of payment to a poor person and claims of payment to another collector. Note which scenarios are accepted and which are not.
Both involve the owner swearing an oath.
2
Apply the livestock exception
For livestock (sawa'im), the claim of payment to a poor person is not accepted because the ruler takes the zakat directly. Understand why the owner has no authority to distribute livestock zakat.
This is a strict condition: only the ruler or deputy collects.
3
Consider the location condition
The claim is only accepted if the property was still within the city (Egypt) at the time of payment. If removed from the city, it becomes apparent wealth and the claim is not accepted.
Hidden wealth becomes apparent upon removal.
4
Analyze the payment to another collector
If the owner claims he paid to another just collector in that year, he is believed with an oath. But if the collector was from the Khawarij, the payment is void.
Condition: the other collector must be just and known to exist.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is disagreement: some say the zakat is the owner's payment to the poor, others say it is the collector's taking. The relied-upon position is that the collector's taking is the actual zakat.
Al-Hidayah corrects the view that the owner's payment is void.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from the Book of Zakat. It addresses the scenario where a Muslim wealth owner claims to have already paid the zakat due on his property. The commentator explains the legal position of the Hanafi school regarding such claims. The key ruling is that if a person claims to have paid the zakat on his non-livestock property (ghayr al-sawa'im) to a poor person within the city (Egypt), his statement is accepted with an oath. However, for livestock (sawa'im), such a claim is not accepted because the owner is not authorized to distribute zakat on livestock; rather, the ruler or his deputy collects it. Therefore, if he claims to have paid it himself, it is ignored and the ruler takes the zakat again. Similarly, if he claims to have paid to another collector, his statement is accepted with an oath, provided that the other collector existed in that year and was a just collector. If the collector was from the Khawarij (rebels), the payment is not considered valid. The passage also lists other statements that are accepted from the owner, such as denying intent for trade, claiming a debt that reduces the nisab, or that the property is a deposit, merchandise, etc. There is a noted intra-Hanafi disagreement: some say the actual zakat is the owner's payment to the poor, and the collector's taking is merely administrative; others say the owner's payment is void and the collector's taking is the true zakat. The relied-upon position, as corrected in al-Hidayah, is that the collector's taking constitutes the zakat.

Key Terms
السوائم (al-sawa'im): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep, goats) that are kept for milk, breeding, or meat and are not used for work.
عاشر (عاشر): A tax collector or zakat collector appointed by the ruler.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah that one is telling the truth.
باطنة (batina): Hidden wealth, such as wealth kept within the city or not revealed to the ruler.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when does a Muslim's claim of paying zakat to a poor person in the city require an oath?
A) For all types of wealth, including livestock
B) Only for non-livestock property within the city
C) Only after removal from the city
D) It is never accepted without written proof

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, claims of paying zakat on livestock are not accepted; the ruler's collection is the valid zakat.

Claims of Zakat Payment: Muslims, Dhimmis, and Harbis
Rules on accepting zakat payment claims for Muslims, dhimmis, and harbis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting zakat payment claims from Muslims, dhimmis, and harbis, and identify the exception for umm walad.

Core Idea
A harbi's claims about zakat are rejected, except for acknowledgment of lineage; a Muslim's oath suffices without written discharge.

This passage builds on earlier rules about zakat claims, extending them to non-Muslims. It clarifies that a Muslim's claim is accepted without written proof, and then distinguishes between dhimmis (who are treated like Muslims in these matters) and harbis (whose claims are generally not accepted). The only exception is a harbi's claim that a slave woman is the mother of his child, because lineage is fundamental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for Muslim claimants
The text states that a Muslim claiming zakat payment does not need to produce a written discharge (bara'ah); his oath is sufficient. This is the correct (sahih) view.
There is a conflicting narration requiring written discharge, but the relied-upon position is that oath alone suffices.
2
Extend the rule to dhimmis
Whatever a Muslim is believed in, a dhimmi is also believed. The reason is that the zakat taken from a dhimmi is double (5% vs 2.5%), so the same considerations apply.
Dhimmi means a non-Muslim citizen under Muslim protection.
3
Recognize the default rejection of harbi claims
A harbi (non-Muslim at war with the state) is not believed in any claim related to zakat. Specific examples: claiming the year hasn't completed, having a debt, that the goods are merchandise or not for trade, or that he has paid.
The reason is that there is no benefit in believing him; he is not under Muslim authority.
4
Identify the exception for umm walad
A harbi's claim that a slave woman with him is the mother of his child (umm walad) is accepted. This is because acknowledgment of lineage is valid even from a harbi, as lineage is a fundamental right.
The claim is only accepted if the child could be his biologically; otherwise it is treated as emancipation and he must pay a tithe.
5
Note the rate of zakat for Muslims
The text mentions that one-quarter tenth (2.5%) is taken from Muslims. This is based on the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him).
This rate is for trade goods; for dhimmis it is double (5%).

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on claims of zakat payment. It begins by stating that a Muslim claimant is not required to produce a written discharge from the collector; his oath is sufficient. However, there is a conflicting narration requiring written proof. The relied-upon correct view is the first. The claim regarding livestock is not accepted, as established earlier. The passage then extends these rules to non-Muslims: a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim protection) is believed in the same situations as a Muslim merchant, because the amount taken from him is double, so the same considerations apply. A harbi (non-Muslim at war) is not believed in any of these claims, because there is no benefit in believing him—if he says the year hasn't passed, it is ignored; if he has a debt, it is not considered; if he says the wealth is merchandise, the owner has no protection; if he says it's not for trade, the apparent contradicts him; if he says he paid, his inner belief is rejected. The only exception is the harbi's claim that a slave woman in his possession is the mother of his child (umm walad); this is accepted because acknowledgment of lineage is valid from a harbi. Then the text mentions that from a Muslim, one-quarter tenth is taken (i.e., 2.5% zakat). This rate is based on the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab. The passage concludes with detailed conditions for the harbi's claim of umm walad: if the child could be his biologically, the claim is accepted; if not, it is considered an acknowledgment of emancipation and he must give a tithe.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'ah): A written document of discharge confirming that the zakat has been paid and received.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen living under the protection of an Islamic state, paying jizyah.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Muslim protection and with whom war may be waged.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining certain rights including freedom upon the master's death.
ربع عشر (rub' 'ushr): One-quarter of one-tenth, i.e., 2.5%, which is the standard zakat rate for Muslim merchants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a Muslim's claim of zakat payment is accepted by oath alone; dhimmis are treated similarly, but harbis are not believed except for lineage claims.

Rates of 'Ushr for Muslim, Dhimmi, and Harbi Traders with Reciprocation Condition
Commentary on rates of 'ushr for different trader categories with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rates of 'ushr for Muslim, dhimmi, and harbi traders, apply the reciprocation condition when the enemy's taking is known, and distinguish the disposition of the collected funds.

Core Idea
The 'ushr rate varies by trader status: Muslims pay 2.5%, dhimmis 5%, harbis 10% unless reciprocation applies.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the duties imposed on traders crossing Muslim territory. The rates are not arbitrary; they derive from a hadith of Anas ibn Malik and are adjusted based on the trader's relationship with the Islamic state. Additionally, when the enemy's taking from Muslim traders is known, the principle of reciprocity may modify the levy from harbi traders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the levy of 'ushr (tenth) on traders passing through Muslim territory. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) as a protective duty, not as zakah.
Distinguish 'ushr from zakah: 'ushr is a customs-like duty, while zakah is a religious obligation on wealth.
2
State the rates for each category
From the hadith: Muslims pay 1/40 (2.5%), dhimmis pay 1/20 (5%), and harbis (those without covenant) pay 1/10 (10%) if their goods reach nisab.
The hadith is ahad, but the Hanafi school uses it as proof due to its clarity.
3
Apply the condition of reciprocation
If the amount the enemy takes from Muslim traders is known, our tithe-collector takes from the harbi the same portion (e.g., if enemy takes 10%, we take 10%). However, if the enemy takes all, we leave enough for safe passage.
This rule reflects fairness: we do not exceed what is taken from us.
4
Understand the disposition of funds
What is taken from Muslims is zakah, spent on its eight categories. What is taken from dhimmis and harbis is not zakah but a protective duty, spent on jizya and kharaj (treasury for public welfare).
The Hanafi school emphasizes that the duty is for protection (himaya), not as a religious obligation.
5
Evaluate the evidence
The hadith of Anas ibn Malik is the primary dalil. It directly specifies the rates. The commentary explains the reciprocation condition via qiyas on the principle of retaliation (mumathala).
The thubut is zanni (ahad hadith), but the dalala is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة باب العاشر
ومن الذمي ضعفه، ومن الحربي العشر إن بلغ ماله نصاباً، ولم يُعلم قدر ما أُخذ منا، وإن علم أخذ مثله إن كان بعضاً لا كلاً إن أخذوه منا.
أي لم يعلم قدر ما أخذ منا أهل الحرب إذا مر تاجرنا عليهم.
(وإن علم أخذ مثله إن كان بعضاً لا كلاً إن أخذوه منا) أي إن علم قدر ما أخذ منا أهل الحرب، فعاشرنا يأخذ من الحربي مثل ذلك إن كان بعضاً، حتى أنهم لو أخذوا كل أموالنا، فعاشرنا لا يأخذ كل أموال الحربي المار.
فعن أنس بن مالك قال: فرض محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم في أموال المسلمين في كل أربعين درهماً درهم، وفي أموال أهل الذمة في كل عشرين درهماً درهم، وفي أموال من لا ذمة له في كل عشرة دراهم درهم. أخرجه عبد الرزاق وغيره.
ثم ما يؤخذ من المسلم زكاة تصرف في مصارفها، وما يؤخذ من الذمة ليس بزكاة، بل تصرف في مصرف الجزية والخراج، وكذا ما أخذ من الحربي بل الأخذ منهما لحمايته. كذا في البناية.
قوله: وإن علم ... الخ؛ الحاصل أن دخول الحربي في الحماية أوجب الأخذ، فإن عرف كمية ما أخذ أهل الحرب من تجار أهل الإسلام عند دخولهم في بلادهم أخذنا منهم مثله مجازاة إلا إذا عرف أنهم يأخذون كل المال منا، فنحن لا نأخذ منهم الكل بل نترك لهم ما يبلغ به إلى مأمنه. وإن لم يعرف ذلك أخذ العشر ضعف ما يؤخذ من الذمي؛ لأنه أحوج منه إلى الحماية، ولو لم يعلم أصل أخذ شيء منا يؤخذ العشر أيضاً؛ لتحقق سببه.
Book of Zakah, Chapter on the Tenth. And from the dhimmi is double it, and from the harbi is the tenth if his wealth reaches nisab, and it is not known the amount taken from us. And if he knows, he takes the like of it if it was part, not all, if they took it from us. Meaning: he does not know the amount taken from us by the people of war when our trader passes by them. (And if he knows, he takes the like of it if it was part, not all, if they took it from us) meaning: if he knows the amount taken from us by the people of war, then our tithe-collector takes from the harbi the like of that if it was part, such that if they took all our wealth, our tithe-collector does not take all the wealth of the passing harbi. For from Anas ibn Malik said: Muhammad (peace be upon him) ordained on the wealth of Muslims one dirham for every forty dirhams, and on the wealth of the dhimmis one dirham for every twenty dirhams, and on the wealth of those who have no covenant one dirham for every ten dirhams. Reported by Abd al-Razzaq and others. Then what is taken from the Muslim is zakah, spent in its categories, and what is taken from the dhimmi is not zakah, but is spent in the category of jizya and kharaj, and likewise what is taken from the harbi, rather the taking from both is for his protection. Thus in al-Binayah. His saying: 'And if he knows...' the summary is that the entry of the harbi into protection necessitates the taking. If he knows the quantity of what the people of war took from the traders of the people of Islam upon their entry into their lands, we take from them the like of it in retaliation, except if it is known that they take all the wealth from us, then we do not take from them the whole but leave them enough to reach their place of safety. And if he does not know that, he takes the tenth, double of what is taken from the dhimmi because he is more in need of protection, and if he does not know at all that anything was taken from us, he also takes the tenth because its cause is established.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on 'ushr (tithe) in the Book of Zakah. It addresses the rates of duty imposed on traders passing through Muslim territory, specifically from Muslims, dhimmis (protected non-Muslims), and harbis (non-Muslims from enemy territory). The matn states: from a dhimmi, double the zakah rate (i.e., 5%), and from a harbi, one-tenth (10%) if his wealth reaches the nisab (minimum threshold) and the amount the enemy took from Muslim traders is unknown. If the amount is known, then we take from the harbi the same amount they took from us, provided it was only part of the goods—if they took all, we do not take all but leave enough for safe return. The commentary elaborates on these conditions and cites a hadith from Anas ibn Malik (ra) establishing the rates: from Muslims 1/40 (2.5%), from dhimmis 1/20 (5%), and from those without covenant (harbis) 1/10 (10%). It then clarifies the disposition of the collected funds: from Muslims is zakah spent on its eight categories; from dhimmis and harbis is not zakah but goes to the treasury for jizya and kharaj and is taken for their protection. The summary reiterates that the harbi's entry into protection obligates the levy, and if the enemy's taking is known, we reciprocate in kind, but if unknown, we take the fixed rate.

Key Terms
عشر ('ushr): One-tenth; the duty taken from harbi traders passing through Muslim territory.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of the Islamic state with a covenant.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from enemy territory with no covenant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 2.5%
B) 5%
C) 10%
D) 20%

Key Takeaway
The 'ushr duty on passing traders varies according to their status and the known taking by the enemy, with the funds from non-Muslims directed to the treasury for protective purposes.

The 'Ushr on Harbi Traders: Less Than Nisab and Timing
Explanation of exceptions and conditions for 'ushr on harbi traders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi disagreement on taking 'ushr from harbi traders with goods below nisab, and state the ruling on when 'ushr is collected (before the year, not annually).

Core Idea
The 'ushr from a harbi trader is not taken from goods below nisab unless in retaliation, and it is taken upon entry, not after a full year.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains two special rules for the 'ushr (tenth) collected from harbi traders entering Dar al-Islam. First, the duty is generally not taken from goods below the nisab threshold, but there is an exception when the enemy has taken from Muslim traders—leading to an internal Hanafi dispute. Second, the 'ushr is collected immediately upon entry, not after a year, because the harbi cannot stay a full year without jizya. Understanding these exceptions clarifies that 'ushr on harbis is not identical to zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
The base rule states that no 'ushr is taken from a harbi trader with goods less than nisab (200 dirhams or equivalent). This mirrors the zakah nisab requirement.
The 'ushr is double the zakah rate, so the conditions of the original (zakah) are considered.
2
Recognize the exceptional circumstance
An exception arises when the enemy (harbis) have taken anything from Muslim traders. In that case, retaliation (mujazat) becomes a factor.
The pronoun 'they' in the matn refers to the people of war, even though not explicitly mentioned.
3
Learn the two Hanafi positions
There is a dispute within the Hanafi school: In Jami' al-Saghir, the 'ushr is taken from the little in retaliation. In al-Mabsut, it is not taken because taking from a small amount is oppression (zulm).
Both positions are transmitted; the text does not decisively prefer one.
4
Understand the timing rule
The 'ushr is taken from the harbi trader immediately upon entry, before the completion of a year. He cannot stay a full year without being subject to jizya.
If he returns after a year without having paid jizya, the 'ushr is taken again as a deterrent.
5
Apply the practical consequence
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in the first position, 'ushr is collected even from small amounts if the enemy took from Muslims; in the second, it is waived.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly settled, but the mention of 'noble morals' leans toward not taking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا من قليله، وإن أقر بباقي النصاب في بيته ولا يأخذ شيئاً منه، إن لم يأخذوا شيئاً منا، ولو عُشْرَ ثم من قبل الحول.
(ولا من قليله، وإن أقر بباقي النصاب في بيته) القليل ما لا يبلغ النصاب.
(ولا يأخذ شيئاً منه، إن لم يأخذوا شيئاً منا) الضمير في لم يأخذوا راجع إلى أهل الحرب، وإن لم يذكر هذا اللفظ.
(ولو عُشْرَ ثم من قبل الحول) أنهم لا يأخذون من تجارنا شيئاً لا نأخذ شيئاً أيضاً؛ لأنا أحق بمكارم الأخلاق منهم. كذا في الهداية وفتح القدير وغيرهما.
قوله: ولا من قليله؛ يعني: إذا مر التاجر الحربي بما هو أقل من النصاب لا يأخذ عاشرنا منه شيئاً؛ لأن المأخوذ ضعف الزكاة، فيعتبر فيه ما يعتبر في الأصل كذا في الهداية.
وهل يؤخذ منه إذا علم أخذهم منا من القليل فيه اختلاف: ففي جامع الصغير: يؤخذ مجازاة، وفي المبسوط: لا؛ بناءً على أن الأخذ من القليل ظلم فلا نرتكبه.
قوله: لم يذكر، أي صراحة، لكنه مذكور حكماً، وهو يكفي لإرجاع الضمير.
قوله: ولو عشر؛ أي التاجر الحربي، وهو مجهول من التعشير بمعنى: أخذ العشر.
قوله: قبل الحول؛ قيد به؛ لأنه لا يمكن له المقام في دارنا حولاً كاملاً، بل يقول له الإمام عند دخوله: إن أقمت حولاً ضربت عليك الجزية، فإن أقام ضربها، ثم لا يمكن من العود غير أنه إن مر ثانياً بعد الحول ولم يكن له علم بمقامه حولاً عشره ثانياً؛ زجراً له، ويرده إلى دارنا. كذا في فتح القدير.
And not from its little, and if he admits to the rest of the nisab in his house, he does not take anything from it, if they did not take anything from us, and even if tithed then before the year. (And not from its little, and if he admits to the rest of the nisab in his house) the little is what does not reach the nisab. (And he does not take anything from it, if they did not take anything from us) the pronoun in 'they did not take' refers to the people of war, even if this wording is not mentioned. (And even if tithed then before the year) that they do not take anything from our traders, we do not take anything either, because we are more deserving of noble morals than them. Thus in al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir, and others. His saying: 'And not from its little' means: when the harbi trader passes with what is less than the nisab, our tithe-collector does not take anything from him, because what is taken is double the zakah, so what is considered in the original is considered here, thus in al-Hidayah. And whether it is taken from him if their taking from us is known from the little, there is disagreement: in Jami' al-Saghir it is taken in retaliation, and in al-Mabsut: no, based on the fact that taking from the little is oppression, so we do not commit it. His saying: 'not mentioned' explicitly, but it is mentioned implicitly, and that is sufficient to refer the pronoun. His saying: 'and even if tithed' meaning the harbi trader, and it is unknown from the tithe meaning taking the tenth. His saying: 'before the year' he restricted it because he cannot stay in our land a complete year, rather the Imam says to him upon his entry: if you stay a year, I will impose jizya on you, then if he stays, he imposes it, then he is not allowed to return, except that if he passes a second time after the year and he did not know about his stay for a year, he is tithed a second time as a deterrent, and he is returned to our land. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the rulings for harbi traders with goods less than nisab and the timing of 'ushr. The matn says: 'And not from its little' meaning no 'ushr is taken from a harbi whose goods are below nisab, and if he admits to having the rest of nisab at home, nothing is taken from him, provided the enemy did not take from us. If they did not take from our traders, we do not take from theirs either, out of noble conduct. The commentary explains that 'little' means less than nisab, and the pronoun in 'they did not take' refers to the enemy. It then notes a key dispute within the Hanafi school: when it is known that the enemy took from us, should we take 'ushr from the harbi's goods even if they are less than nisab? In Jami' al-Saghir (by Imam Muhammad), it is taken in retaliation. In al-Mabsut (by al-Sarakhsi), it is not taken because taking from a small amount is oppressive. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly given here. The passage also addresses timing: the 'ushr is taken from the harbi upon entry, before the completion of a year, because he cannot stay a full year in Dar al-Islam without imposing jizya. If he returns after a year without having paid jizya, the 'ushr is taken again as a deterrent.

Key Terms
قليل (qalil): Goods below the nisab threshold; less than 200 dirhams or equivalent.
مجازاة (mujazat): Retaliation; taking the same amount from the harbi as they took from Muslim traders.
حول (hawl): A full lunar year; the time after which 'ushr or jizya may be imposed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the nisab condition is not met
B) Because taking a small amount is oppressive
C) Because the trader is poor
D) Because it is not wajib

Key Takeaway
The 'ushr on harbi traders has two exceptions: goods below nisab are generally exempt, except in disputed retaliation; and the duty is collected on entry, not annually.

Ruling on the 'Ushr Levy from Harbi Traders: Nisab and Reciprocity
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on nisab and reciprocity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab condition for the 'ushr levy and explain the Hanafi dispute on retaliation for small amounts.

Core Idea
The 'ushr levy mirrors zakat conditions; reciprocity limits taking but retaliation from small amounts is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains two key aspects of the tithe ('ushr) imposed on harbi merchants. First, the levy is not taken on goods below the nisab threshold, because it is double the zakat and inherits its conditions. Second, a principle of reciprocity governs: we only take from them if they take from our merchants, reflecting superior Islamic ethics. A further nuance arises when the enemy takes small amounts—should we retaliate in kind? The Hanafi school has two views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking the 'ushr levy from a harbi trader. The primary ruling is that it is wajib to take from those whose goods reach nisab, and it is not permissible to take from below nisab. The text states 'the tithe collector does not take anything from him' for goods below nisab.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to zakat: 'because what is taken is double the zakat, so what is considered in the original is considered here.' Thus the nisab condition from zakat applies to the 'ushr levy.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Extract the reciprocity rule
The text states 'if they do not take anything from us, we do not take anything from them' (إن لم يأخذوا شيئا منا). The reason given is 'we are more entitled to noble morals than them,' so the rule is based on ethical conduct, not strict legal obligation.
This rule is conditional: if the enemy takes from our merchants, we may take from theirs.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on retaliation
When the enemy takes from our merchants from small amounts (below nisab), there is a disagreement: al-Jami' al-Saghir allows taking in retaliation (mujāzāh), while al-Mabsut forbids it, calling it oppression (ẓulm). The relied-upon view is not explicit here but later authorities tend to avoid oppression.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Determine the operative view
The passage does not give a tarjih. However, later Hanafi preference, based on the maxim 'avoiding oppression is preferred,' likely adopts the position of al-Mabsut. The mufta bihi is probably not to take from small amounts even in retaliation.

Source Text

(ولا من قليله ، وإن أقر بباقي النصاب في بيته) : القليلُ ما لا يبلغ النصاب.

أنهم لا يأخذون من تجارنا شيئاً لا نأخذ شيئاً أيضاً ؛ لأنا أحق بمكارم الأخلاق منهم. كذا في «الهداية»، و«فتح القدير»، وغيرهما.
١ قوله : ولا من قليله ؛ يعنى : إذا مر التاجر الحربي بما هو أقل من النصاب لا يأخذ عاشرنا منه شيئاً ؛ لأنَّ المأخوذ ضعف الزكاة، فيعتبر فيه ما يعتبر في الأصل كذا في «الهداية»(۳).

(Not from his small amount, and if he admits the remaining nisab is in his house): the small is what does not reach nisab. (And he does not take anything from him, if they do not take anything from us): the pronoun in 'they did not take' refers to the people of war, even if this word is not mentioned. That they do not take anything from our merchants, we also do not take anything; because we are more entitled to noble morals than them. As in al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir, and others. 1 His saying: and not from his small amount; meaning: when the harbi merchant passes with what is less than nisab, our tithe collector does not take anything from him; because what is taken is double the zakat, so what is considered in the original is considered here. Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof and a minor intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses two sub-rulings: first, the tithe collector does not take any portion from a harbi trader whose goods are below the nisab threshold, because the levy is analogous to double zakat, which itself has a nisab. Second, the principle of reciprocity: we only take from them if they take from our merchants; if they do not, we refrain, citing superior ethics. The commentary then explains the pronoun reference and notes a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, we do take from small amounts in retaliation if they do so, but according to al-Mabsut, we do not, as it would be oppressive. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated here.

Key Terms
القليل (al-qalīl): Goods below the nisab threshold for the 'ushr levy.
مجازاة (mujāzāh): Taking in retaliation or reciprocation when the enemy takes from our merchants.
النصاب (al-niṣāb): The minimum threshold of wealth that triggers the obligation of zakat; here applied analogically to 'ushr.

Key Takeaway
The 'ushr levy on harbi traders is analogous to zakat and requires nisab; reciprocity governs whether the levy is taken, but retaliation from small amounts is disputed within the Hanafi school.

Taking 'Ushr from a Harbi Trader Before the Full Year
Explaining the condition of a full year for the tithe and the impossibility for a harbi to complete it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking 'ushr from a harbi trader and explain why the condition of a full year is inapplicable.

Core Idea
For a harbi trader, the tithe is taken per entry, not per year.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding 'ushr (tithe) levied on harbi traders. The passage clarifies that the usual condition of a full year (hawl) for zakat does not apply here because the harbi cannot reside in Muslim lands for an entire year without becoming subject to jizyah. Understanding this rational basis helps distinguish the tithe on harbis from the zakat on Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the subject
The passage discusses 'ushr (tithe) taken from a 'harbi merchant' (tājir ḥarbī). Determine who is a harbi and what ta'shīr means: taking one-tenth of his trade goods.
Harbi: a non-Muslim from a non-Muslim territory at war with Islam (or not under a peace treaty).
2
Locate the condition mentioned and its restriction
The text says 'before the year' (qabl al-ḥawl) and then restricts it: 'because it is not possible for him to stay in our land a complete year.' Understand why this condition is impossible.
The harbi cannot remain a full year without being offered Islam or made a dhimmi with jizyah.
3
Analyze the rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī)
The argument is rational: the harbi cannot complete the year without changing his status; therefore, the condition of a full year is waived. The imam warns him upon entry, and if he stays, jizyah is imposed.
This is not based on text but on practical impossibility (ta‘dhur al-ḥawl).
4
Consider the consequence of a second crossing
If the harbi passes again after a year (and did not know about the jizyah obligation), the tithe is taken a second time as a deterrent (zajran). This shows the tithe is taken per crossing, not per year.
The text mentions 'if he passes a second time after the year' (in marra thāniyan ba‘da al-ḥawl).
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Some schools (e.g., Shāfi‘ī) may require a full year for the harbi, analogizing to zakat. The Hanafi response is that the harbi cannot complete the year, so the condition is inapplicable.
This ikhtilāf is legitimate and stems from different views on the ta‘līl (effective cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولو عشر ؛ أي التاجر الحربي، وهو مجهول من التعشير بمعنى: أخذ
[٤] قوله : قبل الحول ؛ قيد به ؛ لأنه لا يمكن له المقام في دارنا حولاً كاملاً ، بل يقول له الإمام عند دخوله : إن أقمت حولاً ضربت عليك الجزية، فإن أقام ضربها، ثم لا يمكن من العود غير أنه إن مر ثانياً بعد الحول ولم يكن له علم بمقامه حولاً عشره ثانياً ؛ زجراً له ، ويرده إلى دارنا. كذا في «فتح القدير»(4).
His saying: and if a tenth is taken; meaning the harbi merchant, and it is derived from 'ta'shir' meaning taking. [4] His saying: before the year; he restricted it because it is not possible for him to stay in our land a complete year. Rather, the imam says to him upon his entry: if you stay a year, I will impose jizyah on you. If he stays, he imposes it. Then he cannot return unless he passes a second time after the year and he did not know about his stay for a year, he takes a tenth from him a second time as a deterrent, and he returns him to our land. As in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on taking the 'ushr from a harbi before the completion of a full year. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'before the year' is a condition: because the harbi cannot reside in Muslim territory for an entire year without becoming a dhimmi subject to jizyah. The imam warns him upon entry. If the harbi stays a full year, jizyah is imposed and he cannot return as a harbi. If he later passes again after the year without knowing about the jizyah obligation, the tithe is taken a second time as a deterrent. This ruling is based on the principle that the 'ushr is a levy akin to kharaj, taken per crossing rather than per annum for harbis.

Key Terms
التعشير (al-ta'shīr): The act of taking one-tenth ('ushr) from a harbi trader's goods.
الحربي (al-ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is not under Muslim protection and is from a territory at war or not at peace with Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that 'ushr from a harbi trader is taken immediately upon each entry, not conditioned on a full year, because the harbi cannot complete a year without becoming dhimmi.

Second Tithe and Tithing Dhimmi's Wine and Pig
Explaining when a second tithe is taken and the differing rulings on dhimmi's wine and pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for taking a second tithe from a harbi trader and explain the Hanafi positions on tithing a dhimmi's wine and pig, comparing them with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Wine is tithed, pig is not; a harbi's second tithe depends on his point of origin.

This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah addresses two related tax issues: when a second tithe is levied on a harbi trader, and whether the wine and pig of a dhimmi are subject to the half-tithe ('ushr). The rulings hinge on the concept of legal value (muttaqawwim) and the status of the trader's journey. We will unpack the condition for the second tithe and the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disagreement over a dhimmi's restricted goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second tithe condition
Read the first part of the passage: 'If he comes from his land and passes, tithe is taken a second time, otherwise no.' Focus on the condition 'from his land' (i.e., from dar al-harb) and the exception 'otherwise no' (i.e., if returning from Muslim lands). Understand that the first amān remains effective until the year passes or he returns to dar al-harb.
The commentary clarifies that a second tithe is depletion of wealth, so it is only justified by a new entry.
2
Locate the ruling on dhimmi's wine and pig
Find the phrase 'And the tithe of wine of a dhimmi, not his pig.' Note that Abu Hanifa's position is stated first. Then observe the three Hanafi variants: Abu Hanifa (only wine tithed), Abu Yusuf (both if together, wine only if alone, pig alone not), and Zufar (both tithed individually).
The text treats this as an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, with Abu Hanifa's view being the mufta bihi.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Read the commentary on 'according to al-Shafi'i'. He argues that wine and pig, though valuable to disbelievers, have no legal value (muttaqawwim) for Muslims. Therefore, the Islamic state cannot take them or their value. Understand the epistemological basis: the ruling follows from the perspective of the tax collector (Muslim) not the taxpayer.
This inter-madhhab difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the dhimmi trader is charged half-tithe on wine (5% of value) but nothing on pig. Under Shafi'i, nothing is charged on either. Under Zufar, both are charged. Under Abu Yusuf, the pig follows the wine when together. Identify the practical consequence for customs officials.
The text does not give a definitive tarjih, but Abu Hanifa's view is presented as the default.
5
Synthesise the underlying principle
The ruling on the second tithe relies on the concept of 'continuity of amān' and 'new entry'. The ruling on dhimmi's goods relies on the distinction between what is valuable to the dhimmi (their own perspective) vs. what is legally valuable (muttaqawwim) to Muslims. Consider how this principle might apply to other items like alcohol-based medicines or pork derivatives today.
Think of the illa: the state assesses taxability based on the owner's ability to use the goods, not the state's ability to own them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he comes from his land and passes, tithe is taken a second time, otherwise no. And the tithe of wine of a dhimmi, not his pig, whether he passes with both or one of them. 'If he comes from his land and passes, tithe is taken a second time, otherwise no': meaning, when the 'ushr has been taken from the harbi, then he passes before the year: if in the second time he came from his land, the tithe is taken a second time; but if he was returning from our land to his land, nothing is taken from him. 'The tithe of wine of a dhimmi, not his pig, whether he passes with both or one of them': this is according to Abu Hanifa. But according to al-Shafi'i, he does not tithe them. According to Zufar, he tithes each one. According to Abu Yusuf: if he passes with both, he tithes both, making the pig a follower of wine; if he passes with wine alone, he tithes it; if he passes with pig alone, he does not. 11 His saying: tithe a second time; meaning the tenth is taken from him a second time. [2] His saying: otherwise no; because taking each time is depletion of property, and the right to take is for its preservation; also the ruling of the first amān remains as long as the year has not passed or he returns to dar al-harb, and it is not permissible to take a second time without the passage of a year or renewal of covenant. [3] His saying: returning from our land to his land; likewise if he was traveling from one town to another of our towns. [4] His saying: tithe; it is intended to take half of the tenth, for that is what is taken from the dhimmi. As for the harbi, the full tenth is taken from his wine, and as for the Muslim, nothing is taken from his wine, and so on, and it has no value legally in his regard. [0] His saying: according to al-Shafi'i, etc.; the summary is that when the disbeliever passes with wine only or with pig only or both, according to al-Shafi'i our tithe collector does not take anything from him because, although they are valuable property in the right of the disbelievers (for wine is like vinegar to them, and pig is like sheep to them, and they are not prevented from selling and buying them), yet they have no value legally in the right of the people of Islam, and they are not valuable property in their regard. So it is not possible for us to take anything from their physical substance or from their value, because it is not permissible for a Muslim to own them or transfer ownership of them, and the value of a thing is like that thing in ruling.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute along with a ruling on the second crossing. It addresses two issues: (1) If a harbi trader passes a second time before the year, the tithe is taken again only if he comes from dar al-harb; if he is returning from our lands, nothing is taken because the first amān is still valid. (2) Regarding the wine and pig of a dhimmi, Abu Hanifa rules that wine is tithed (since it is valuable for dhimmis) but pig is not (because it is not considered valuable property in Islamic law). Al-Shafi'i entirely refrains from taking anything because these items are not legally valuable for Muslims. Zufar takes from both, while Abu Yusuf takes from both when together (treating pig as a follower of wine), from wine alone, but not from pig alone. The commentary elaborates: the second tithe would deplete the wealth, and the original amān's protection lasts until the year ends or he returns home. The tithe from a dhimmi is half of the usual tenth (i.e., 5%), while from a harbi it is a full tenth. Muslims pay nothing on wine or pig. The Shafi'i position rests on the principle that items not valuable for Muslims cannot be subject to levy.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizyah and is protected.
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable in Islamic law, permissible to own and trade.
عشر ('ushr): A tithe or customs duty levied on trade goods, usually one-tenth of the value for harbi and half-tenth for dhimmi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in this passage, what is the ruling on taking 'ushr from a dhimmi's wine and pig?
A) 'Ushr is taken from both.
B) 'Ushr is taken from wine but not from pig.
C) 'Ushr is taken from pig but not from wine.
D) No 'ushr is taken from either.

Key Takeaway
The condition for a second tithe is a new entry from dar al-harb; for a dhimmi's goods, wine is taxable because it has value for him, but pig is not taxable because it lacks legal value (muttaqawwim) altogether.

Tithing a Dhimmi's Wine and Pig: The Mithli/Qimi Distinction
Explains rulings on tithing dhimmi's wine and pig with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tithing a dhimmi's wine and pig, explain the intra-Hanafi differences, and apply the mithli/qimi distinction to evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's wine is tithed because it is fungible (mithli); his pig is not because it is non-fungible (qimi) and taking its value equals taking the pig.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a nuanced zakah question: when a dhimmi trader passes through Muslim lands with wine or pigs—both prohibited for Muslims but permissible for him—should the Muslim tithe-collector take a tithe? The answer involves not only inter-madhhab disagreement with the Shafi'is but also intra-Hanafi differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar. At the heart of the Hanafi position is a sophisticated distinction between fungible goods (dhawat al-amthal) and non-fungible goods (dhawat al-qiyam), which determines whether taking the value is equivalent to taking the prohibited substance itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the tithe-collector taking a zakah toll from a dhimmi who passes with wine and/or pig. The Hanafi ruling: wine is tithed (by value), pig is not.
2
Map the madhhab positions
List the views: Abu Hanifa (wine yes, pig no), Abu Yusuf (wine yes, pig yes only if with wine), Zufar (both yes separately), Shafi'i (neither).
3
Understand the opposing Shafi'i proof
Shafi'i argues that wine and pig have no value in Islamic law, so they cannot be subject to zakah.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using the mithli/qimi distinction
Wine is mithli: taking its value does not equal taking wine. Pig is qimi: taking its value equals taking the pig, which is haram for Muslims.
5
Apply the distinction to Abu Yusuf's follower rule
When pig accompanies wine, it is considered a follower (taba') and is also tithed; alone it is not.

Source Text

قال: إن جاء من داره ومرّ، عشّر ثانياً، وإلا فلا. وعشر خمر ذمي لا خنزيره، مر بهما أو بأحدهما.
ثم قال الشارح: هذا عند أبي حنيفة. وأما عند الشافعي لا يعشرها. وعند زفر يعشر كل واحد. وعند أبي يوسف إن مر بهما يعشرهما، فجعل الخنزير تبعاً للخمر، وإن مر بالخمر منفرداً يعشرها، وإن مر بالخنزير منفرداً لا.
ثم شرح: قوله: عشر ثانياً؛ أي أخذ منه العشر مرة ثانية. قوله: وإلا فلا؛ لأن الأخذ في كل مرة استئصال للمال، وحق الأخذ لحفظه، وأيضاً حكم الأمان الأول باق ما لم يحل الحول، أو يرجع إلى دار الحرب، ولا يجوز الأخذ ثانياً بدون حولان الحول أو تجدد العهد. قوله: راجعاً من دارنا إلى داره؛ وكذا لو كان سائراً من بلدة إلى بلدة من بلادنا. قوله: عشر؛ المراد به أخذ نصف العشر، فإنه الذي يؤخذ من الذمي، وأما الحربي فيؤخذ من خمره العشر، وأما المسلم فلا يؤخذ من خمره، ونحو ذلك، ولا يتقوم شرعاً في حقه شيء. قوله: عند الشافعي... الخ؛ الحاصل أنه إذا مر الكافر بالخمر فقط أو بالخنزير فقط أو كليهما، فعند الشافعي لا يأخذ عاشرنا منه شيئاً؛ لأنهما وإن كانا مالين متقومين في حق الكفار، فإن الخمر لهم كالخل لنا، والخنزير لهم كالشاة لنا، ولا يمنعون من بيعهما وشرائهما، لكن لا قيمة لهما شرعاً في حق أهل الإسلام، وليسا بمتقومين في حقهم، فلا يمكن لنا أن نأخذ منهما شيئاً من عينهما، ولا من قيمتها، فإنه لا يجوز للمسلم تملكهما وتمليكهما، وقيمة الشيء في حكم ذلك الشيء.
والفرق عندنا: أن الخنزير من ذوات القيم، فأخذ قيمته كأخذه، والخمر من ذوات الأمثال، فأخذ القيمة لا يكون كأخذ العين.
وقوله: ولا بضاعة، ومضاربة؛ أي إن مر المضارب بمال المضاربة لا يؤخذ منه. وعند زفر: يعشر كل واحد منهما؛ لأنه وإن لم يكن كل منهما متقوماً لكن أخذ القيمة ليس كأخذ العين، وسبب الأخذ موجود: وهو الحماية، فإنه لما كان كل منهما متقوماً في حقهم، وجبت علينا حفاظته.
وعند أبي يوسف: يعشر الخمر دون الخنزير إلا إذا مر بهما، فحينئذ يعشر الخنزير أيضاً تبعاً للخمر.
وعند أبي حنيفة: يعشر الخمر، ولا يعشر الخنزير سواء مر بهما أو بأحدهما.
قوله: الفرق؛ أي بين الخمر والخنزير، حيث لا يعشر الثاني مطلقاً، ويعشر الأول مطلقاً.
قوله: أن الخنزير... الخ؛ حاصله: أن الأشياء على قسمين: الأول: ما يوجد له مثل متقارب به، ويقال لمثل هذه الأشياء: ذوات الأمثال، وعند هلاكها يجب أداء المثل. والثاني: ما ليس كذلك، ويقال لها: ذوات القيم، وعند هلاكها تجب القيمة. ومن الأول: المكيل والموزون ونحوهما. ومن الثاني: الحيوانات والنياب ونحوها.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: الخمر من ذوات الأمثال، حتى لو أهلك خمر ذمي يجب عليه مثله، فأخذ القيمة في العشر فيه لا يكون كأخذ الخمر المحرم على المسلم شرعاً، بخلاف الخنزير، فإنه من ذوات القيم، فأخذ القيمة فيه كأخذ عينه، وهو ممنوع للمسلم؛ فلذا حكم بأن يعشر الخمر ولا يعشر الخنزير، وبهذا علم وجه قول أبي يوسف أيضاً.

The Book of Zakah. Chapter of the Tenth. He said: If he comes from his land and passes, he is tithed a second time, otherwise not. And the tithe of the wine of a dhimmi, not his pig, whether he passes with both or one of them. Then the commentator said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for al-Shafi'i, he does not tithe it. According to Zufar, he tithes each one. According to Abu Yusuf, if he passes with both, he tithes them both, making the pig a follower of the wine; if he passes with wine alone, he tithes it; if he passes with pig alone, he does not. Then he explained: His saying: 'tithe a second time' means he takes the tithe from him a second time. His saying: 'otherwise not' because taking each time is extirpation of wealth, and the right of taking is to preserve it; also the ruling of the first protection remains as long as the year has not passed or he returns to dar al-harb, and it is not permissible to take a second time without the passing of the year or renewal of the covenant. His saying: 'returning from our land to his land' and likewise if he is traveling from one town to another of our towns. His saying: 'tithe' means taking half of the tithe, for that is what is taken from the dhimmi; as for the harbi, a full tithe is taken from his wine; as for the Muslim, nothing is taken from his wine, and so on, and it is not considered property legally for him. His saying: 'according to al-Shafi'i...' The gist is that if the disbeliever passes with wine only, or with pig only, or with both, according to al-Shafi'i our collector does not take anything from him, because although both are valuable property in the right of the disbelievers—for wine is to them like vinegar to us, and pig to them like sheep to us, and they are not prevented from selling and buying them—yet they have no value legally in the right of the people of Islam, and they are not valuable in their right, so it is not possible for us to take anything from their essence nor from their value, because it is not permissible for a Muslim to possess them or give them in possession, and the value of a thing has the same ruling as that thing. And the distinction in our view is that the pig is among items of value, so taking its value is like taking it, while wine is among fungible goods, so taking the value is not like taking the essence. And His saying: 'And no merchandise, and mudarabah' meaning that if the mudarib passes with the capital of mudarabah, nothing is taken from him. According to Zufar, he tithes each one of them because although neither is valuable (in our right), yet taking the value is not like taking the essence, and the cause for taking exists: that is protection, for since each is valuable in their right, their protection becomes obligatory upon us. According to Abu Yusuf: he tithes wine but not the pig, except if he passes with both, then he tithes the pig as well as a follower of the wine. According to Abu Hanifa: he tithes wine and does not tithe the pig, whether he passes with both or with one of them. His saying: 'the distinction' i.e., between wine and pig, because the second is not tithed absolutely, while the first is tithed absolutely. His saying: 'that the pig...' The gist is that things are of two types: first, what has a close equivalent, and such things are called fungible goods (dhawat al-amthal), and upon destruction the equivalent must be given. Second, what is not so, and they are called items of value (dhawat al-qiyam), and upon destruction the value is obligatory. Among the first: measured and weighed items and the like. Among the second: animals, tusks, and the like. If you know this, we say: wine is among fungible goods, so if one destroys the wine of a dhimmi, the equivalent is obligatory upon him, so taking the value in the tithe of it is not like taking wine which is prohibited for a Muslim; contrary to the pig, for it is among items of value, so taking its value is like taking its essence, and that is prohibited for a Muslim; hence the ruling that wine is tithed and pig is not tithed, and with this the face of Abu Yusuf's statement is also known. His saying: 'and no merchandise' with raf' as a conjunct to his saying 'his pig' and similarly its qarin. And 'bida'ah' linguistically means a piece of property.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on a matn from al-Wiqaya dealing with the zakah (tithe) collected from a dhimmi when he passes through Muslim lands with goods subject to tithe. The core issue is whether wine and pigs—both prohibited for Muslims but permissible for dhimmis—are subject to tithe. The commentator first clarifies the condition for taking a second tithe within the same year: it is only allowed if the dhimmi returns from his home country again after the first passage; if he is merely returning to his country, no tithe is taken. Then he presents the madhhab positions. Abu Hanifa rules that wine is tithed (by taking its value in money) but pig is not, regardless of whether they pass together or separately. Abu Yusuf agrees that wine is always tithed, but if pig accompanies wine, pig is also tithed as a follower; alone it is not. Zufar tithes both each individually. Al-Shafi'i tithes neither because these items have no legal value for Muslims. The commentator then provides the Hanafi reasoning: the distinction lies in the nature of the goods. Wine is a fungible (mithli) good—it has an equivalent—so taking its value is not equivalent to taking the prohibited wine itself. Pig is a non-fungible (qimi) good—it has no standard equivalent—so taking its value would be like taking the pig itself, which is prohibited. Therefore, only wine is tithed. Abu Yusuf's view that pig is tithed when accompanying wine is explained by the principle of following (taba'iyyah). The passage also mentions that mudarib (agent in a mudarabah partnership) is not tithed on the capital. The commentator ends with a linguistic note on 'bida'ah' (merchandise).

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state, obliged to pay jizyah
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Fungible goods that have standard equivalents; upon destruction, the equivalent must be given
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qiyam): Non-fungible goods without standard equivalents; upon destruction, market value is owed
عشر ('ushr): Tithe; a 10% tax on trade goods from dhimmis and harbis passing through Muslim lands

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa rule that a dhimmi's pig is not tithed even though his wine is?
A) Because pig has no value for anyone
B) Because pig is a non-fungible good (qimi), so taking its value is like taking the pig itself, which is prohibited for Muslims
C) Because the dhimmi does not own the pig
D) Because pig is not considered wealth in Islam

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's wine is tithed because it is fungible (mithli); his pig is not because it is non-fungible (qimi) and taking its value would be equivalent to taking the prohibited pig itself.

The Pig and Wine Tithe: A Hanafi Property Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'ushr from prohibited goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on taking 'ushr from a dhimmi's pig and wine, and explain the legal distinction between dhawat al-amthal and dhawat al-qeem that justifies Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
Taking the value of a pig as tithe is like taking the pig itself—forbidden; taking the value of wine is not like taking wine, so tithe is valid.

The 'ushr (tithe) on a dhimmi's trade goods is generally wajib, but what about prohibited items like pig and wine? The Hanafi school debates this, with the key turning on whether taking the value is equivalent to taking the actual prohibited object. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah unpacks the reasoning using the fundamental property categories of fungible and non-fungible goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and three Hanafi positions
State the legal question: Is 'ushr taken from a dhimmi's pig and wine? List the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar.
Abu Hanifa: only wine. Abu Yusuf: wine always; pig only if brought with wine. Zufar: both.
2
Distinguish fungible and non-fungible property
Understand the two categories: dhawat al-amthal (fungibles with standard equivalents) and dhawat al-qeem (non-fungibles, value assessed individually). This distinction determines the legal effect of taking value.
Wine is dhawat al-amthal; pig is dhawat al-qeem.
3
Apply the distinction to the pig
For the pig (dhawat al-qeem), taking its value is legally equivalent to taking the actual pig. Since the actual pig is prohibited for a Muslim, taking its value is also prohibited. Hence, no 'ushr.
Analogy: like destroying a pig, you pay its value (qimah).
4
Apply the distinction to the wine
For wine (dhawat al-amthal), taking its value is NOT equivalent to taking the actual wine. The value is a fungible substitute, not the prohibited substance. Thus, taking the value as 'ushr is allowed.
If a dhimmi destroys another dhimmi's wine, he pays its equivalent (mithl), not the actual wine.
5
Analyze why the other scholars differed
Abu Yusuf and Zufar used the same or different reasoning. Abu Yusuf allowed pig tithe only when brought with wine (subordination). Zufar focused on the cause (protection) rather than the nature of the item. Understand their logic yet see why Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi.
The commentator supports Abu Hanifa, showing his reasoning is most consistent with usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بضاعة، ومضاربة والفرق عندنا : أنَّ الخِنزير من ذواتِ القِيم ، فأخذ قيمته كأخذه، والخمرُ من ذواتِ الأمثال، فأخذ القيمة لا يكون كأخذ العين. ولا بضاعة ، ومضاربة) : أي إن مرَّ المضارب بمال المضاربة لا يؤخذ منه وعند زفر : يُعشَّر كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ لأنه وإن لم يكن كل منهما متقوماً لكن أخذ القيمة ليس كأخذ العين، وسبب الأخذ موجود : وهو الحماية، فإنه لَمَّا كان كل منهما متقوماً في حقهم، وجبت علينا حفاظته. وعند أبي يوسف الله : يعشر الخمر دون الخنزير إلا إذا مر بهما، فحينئذ يعشر الخنزير أيضاً تبعاً للخمر. وعند أبي حنيفة له : يعشر الخمر، ولا يعشر الخنزير سواء مرَّ بهما أو بأحدهما. [١] قوله : الفرق ؛ أي بين الخمر والخنزير، حيث لا يعشرُ الثَّاني مطلقاً، ويعشَّرُ الأول مطلقاً. [٢]قوله : أنَّ الخنزير ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الأشياء على قسمين : الأول : ما يوجد له مثل متقارب به، ويقال لمثل هذه الأشياء : ذوات الأمثال، وعند هلاكها يجب أداء المثل. والثاني : ما ليس كذلك ، ويقال لها : ذواتُ القيم، وعند هلاكها تجب القيمة. ومن الأول : المكيل والموزون ونحوهما. ومن الثاني : الحيوانات والنياب ونحوها. ذمي إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الخمرُ من ذواتِ الأمثال ، حتى لو أهلك خمرَ ذمي يجب عليه مثله ، فأخذ القيمة في العشر فيه لا يكون كأخذ الخمر المحرَّمِ على المسلم شرعاً، بخلاف الخنزير، فإنّه من ذواتِ القيم، فأخذ القيمة فيه كأخذ عينه، وهو ممنوع للمسلم ؛ فلذا حكم بأن يُعسّر الخمر ولا يُعشر الخنزير، وبهذا علـم وجـه قـول أبـي يوسف أيضاً.
And no bida'ah, and mudarabah. The difference according to us: That the pig is from items of value (dhawat al-qeem), so taking its value is like taking it, and wine is from fungible items (dhawat al-amthal), so taking the value is not like taking the actual. And no bida'ah, and mudarabah: meaning if the mudarib passes with the mudarabah capital, nothing is taken from him. According to Zufar: Tithe is taken from each of them, because although neither of them is a commodity (mutqawwam) for us, yet taking the value is not like taking the actual, and the reason for taking exists: which is protection. For when each of them was a commodity in their view, its protection became obligatory upon us. According to Abu Yusuf: He gives tithe on wine without the pig, except if he passes by both, then he gives tithe on the pig as well, following the wine. According to Abu Hanifa: He gives tithe on wine and does not give tithe on the pig, whether he passes by both or one of them. [1] His statement: 'The difference' means between wine and pig, where the second is not tithed at all and the first is tithed at all. [2] His statement: 'That the pig...' its summary: Things are of two types: First: that for which there exists a roughly similar equivalent, and such things are called dhawat al-amthal, and upon their destruction, the equivalent must be given. Second: that which is not so, and they are called dhawat al-qeem, and upon their destruction, the value is due. From the first: measured and weighed items and the like. From the second: animals and garments and the like. Dhimmi. If you understand this, we say: Wine is from dhawat al-amthal, such that if a dhimmi destroys someone's wine, he must pay its equivalent. So taking the value in tithe for it is not like taking the wine prohibited for a Muslim legally. Unlike the pig, for it is from dhawat al-qeem, so taking its value is like taking its actual, which is forbidden for a Muslim. Therefore it is ruled that tithe is taken on wine and not on the pig, and by this the basis of Abu Yusuf's statement is known as well.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of 'ushr (tithe) on goods brought by a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) across a Muslim checkpoint. The base text states that no 'ushr is taken from bida'ah or mudarabah capital. The commentary explains that when a mudarib passes with the capital, no tithe is taken. Then it delves into the difference between pig and wine. The key legal distinction is that pig belongs to dhawat al-qeem (items not fungible, whose value is assessed individually), while wine belongs to dhawat al-amthal (fungible items with standard equivalents). For the pig, taking its value is equivalent to taking the actual pig, which is prohibited for a Muslim to own. For wine, taking its value is not the same as taking the actual wine, which is also prohibited. Therefore, according to Abu Hanifa, tithe is taken on wine (since value is not like the actual prohibited item) but not on the pig (since value is like the actual prohibited item). Abu Yusuf holds that if both pig and wine are brought together, the pig is tithed as a follow-up to the wine. Zufar holds that both are tithed because protection is the cause, and both are commodities in the dhimmi's view. The commentator elaborates with the categories of property to justify Abu Hanifa's position and also shows the basis for Abu Yusuf's view.

Key Terms
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Items that have a standard, roughly similar equivalent in the market, such as measured grains or weighed goods; upon destruction, the equivalent (mithl) must be paid.
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qeem): Items that do not have a standard equivalent and must be valued individually, such as animals or garments; upon destruction, market value (qimah) must be paid.
عشر (ʿushr): A tithe of 10% levied on merchandise of a dhimmi trader entering Muslim territory, as a form of zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, from which of the following items brought by a dhimmi trader is 'ushr taken?
A) Pig only
B) Wine only
C) Both pig and wine
D) Neither pig nor wine

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position on 'ushr from a dhimmi's pig and wine hinges on the property category: pig (dhawat al-qeem) cannot be tithed because its value equals the prohibited item, while wine (dhawat al-amthal) can.

Zakat on Earnings of a Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling on 'ushr from ma'dhun earnings with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which 'ushr is taken from the earnings of a ma'dhun slave according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and explain the point of disagreement with Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
'Ushr on a ma'dhun's earnings depends on the master's ownership — a point of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines the ruling on 'ushr (zakat) from the earnings of a ma'dhun slave — a slave permitted to trade. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether the master owns those earnings, which directly affects the obligation to pay 'ushr. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, who hold that the master owns the earnings, making 'ushr obligatory when conditions are met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key variables
The ruling depends on: (a) whether the slave is in debt, (b) whether the debt encircles his wealth, and (c) whether the master is present. These determine if 'ushr is taken.
2
State the ruling according to the two (Sahibayn)
When the master is present and after debt a nisab remains, 'ushr is taken. If the master is absent, nothing is taken. If debt encircles, nothing is taken.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
He holds that the master does not own the ma'dhun's earnings, so no 'ushr is taken from the master on that basis. The details are in Kitab al-Ma'dhun.
4
Compare the basis of disagreement
The dispute hinges on ownership of the slave's earnings. The two base their view on the general rule that a slave's property belongs to the master; Abu Hanifa makes an exception for the ma'dhun.
This is a rational (aqlī) disagreement about legal capacity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The position of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is the operative ruling for 'ushr, as it is the apparent meaning of the matn and relied upon by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the earnings of a ma'dhun (permitted slave) except if not in debt while his master is with him. (And the earnings of a ma'dhun except if not in debt while his master is with him): meaning if a ma'dhun slave passes by, if he is in debt, nothing is taken from him. If he is not in debt, his earnings belong to his master. So if the master is with him, zakat is taken from him; if the master is not with him, it is not taken. For the owner. And terminologically: it is what the owner gives to a person to sell and trade, with the entire profit for the owner. And mudarabah: it is giving capital for trading with the condition of partnership in profit. In these two forms, the capital is a trust in the hands of the trader, not owned by him, so 'ushr is not taken from it. (1) His statement: 'ma'dhun slave' meaning one whose master has permitted him to trade. (2) His statement: 'if he is...' the summary: The ma'dhun is either indebted with a debt that encircles his wealth, or with a debt that does not encircle, or not indebted at all. In each case, either his master is with him or not. In the first case (debt encircling), nothing is taken from him absolutely. Similarly, in the latter two cases if his master is not with him. And if his master is with him, tithe is taken if after paying the debt a nisab remains. Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on taking 'ushr from the earnings of a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted by his master to trade) when he passes a Muslim tax collector. The matn states 'Earnings of a ma'dhun except if not in debt and his master is with him.' The commentary expands: If the ma'dhun is in debt (especially debt that covers his entire wealth), nothing is taken because the earnings belong to the creditors or to the master subject to debt. If he is not in debt, his earnings belong to his master, so 'ushr is taken only if the master is present. The commentator also explains definitions of bida'ah (investment with all profit for owner) and mudarabah (partnership in profit), noting that in both, the capital is a trust (amanah) so no 'ushr is taken from it. The passage then contains an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the 'two'), the slave's earnings are owned by the master, so if the master is present and the earnings reach nisab after debt, 'ushr is taken. According to Abu Hanifa, the master does not own the earnings of the ma'dhun slave; this refers to the basic ownership issue detailed elsewhere. The relied-upon position for the 'ushr ruling is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade.
بضاعة (biḍāʿah): Capital given by an owner to a trader to trade with, with all profit belonging to the owner, the trader gets no share.
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profit shared according to a pre-agreed ratio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, regardless of master's presence
B) Only if the slave is in debt
C) If the master is present and the earnings after debt reach nisab
D) Only if the slave is not in debt and the master is absent

Key Takeaway
The obligation to pay 'ushr on a ma'dhun's earnings depends on the master's ownership, which is affirmed by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and adopted as the mufta bihi.
