Zakat on Earnings of a Ma'dhun Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on zakat from earnings of an authorized slave, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which zakat is taken from the earnings of an authorized slave (ma'dhun) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding ownership of those earnings.

Core Idea
The earnings of a ma'dhun slave: owned by the master for zakat, but not according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses the conditions under which zakat is taken from the earnings of a ma'dhun slave. It presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether these earnings are owned by the master or the slave. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi position on the relationship between slave ownership and zakat obligations, and shows how legal reasoning (talil) leads to different rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditions
Read the passage and list the conditions under which zakat is taken from the earnings of a ma'dhun. The passage states: if the slave is in debt, nothing is taken; if not in debt, his earning is owned by the master; if the master is with him, zakat is taken; if not, it is not taken. Note the additional condition that after debt repayment a nisab remains.
The conditions are: 1) the slave is a ma'dhun, 2) he is not in debt (or debt is fulfilled leaving nisab), 3) the master is present (i.e., not traveling independently).
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The passage says 'this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to him (Abu Hanifa), the master does not own what is in the hand of the authorized slave.' Identify that the base ruling (taking zakat when conditions are met) is based on the Sahibayn's view, while Abu Hanifa holds that the master does not own the earnings at all.
The disagreement is over the fundamental question of ownership of the ma'dhun's earnings.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The Sahibayn's reasoning: 'the slave's property is owned by the master just as his person is owned by him, even if authorized.' Abu Hanifa's reasoning: authorization gives the slave capacity to possess, so the earnings are owned by the slave, not the master. Reflect on how each side justifies its position.
This is a classic case of disagreement over the legal effect of authorization (idhn).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Sahibayn's view, the master must pay zakat on the ma'dhun's earnings (if conditions are met). Under Abu Hanifa's view, no zakat is due on those earnings. This shows how a difference in ownership leads to different zakat obligations.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The passage does not explicitly state which view is preferred, but later Hanafi authorities (like Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah) adopt the Sahibayn's view. The mufta bihi is that the master owns the earnings and zakat is wajib when conditions are met.
The mufta bihi is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكسب مأذون إلا غير مديون معه مولاه
أي إن مر عبد مأذون فإن كان مديوناً لا يؤخذ منه شيء، وإن لم يكن مديوناً فكسبه ملك لمولاه، فإن كان المولى معه تؤخذ منه الزكاة، وإن لم يكن المولى معه لا تؤخَذ.
(۱) قوله : عبد مأذون؛ أي الذي أذن له مولاه في التجارة. (٢) قوله : فإن كان... الخ؛ الحاصل أن المأذون إما أن يكون مديوناً بدين محيط، أو بغير محيط، أو غير مديون أصلا، أو في كلّ إما أن يكون معه مولاه أو لا، ففي الأول لا شيء عليه مطلقاً، وكذا في الأخيرين إن لم يكن معه مولاه، وإن كان عُشر حيث بقي بعد وفاء الدين نصاب. (۳) قوله : لا يؤخذ منه شيء؛ للشغل بالدين، ولان مال العبد مملوك للمولى، كما أن رقبته مملوكة له، وإن كان مأذوناً، هذا عندهما. وعنده : لا يملك مولاه ما في يد المأذون من كسبه، وتفصيله في «كتاب المأذون».
والمضاربة : هو دفع المال للتجارة مع اشتراط الشركة في الربح. ففي هاتين الصورتين المالُ أمانة في يدِ التَّاجرِ لا مملوك له، فلا يؤخذ منه العشر.
And the earning of an authorized slave except that which is not indebted while his master is with him. Meaning: If a slave is authorized [to trade], if he is in debt, nothing is taken from him; if he is not in debt, his earning is owned by his master. If the master is with him, zakat is taken from it; if the master is not with him, it is not taken. Note: 'Authorized slave' means the one whose master permitted him to trade. Summary: The authorized slave is either in debt with a debt encompassing [his value], or with a non-encompassing debt, or not in debt at all; and in each case either the master is with him or not. In the first case, nothing is taken at all; similarly in the latter two if the master is not with him; but if he is, a tenth [zakat] is taken where after fulfilling the debt a nisab remains. 'Nothing is taken from him' due to preoccupation with debt, and because the slave's property is owned by the master just as his person is owned by him, even if authorized—this is according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. According to him [Abu Hanifa], the master does not own what is in the hand of the authorized slave from his earnings; its detail is in the Book of the Authorized Slave. And mudarabah: giving capital for trade with the condition of sharing in profit. In these two forms, the capital is a trust in the hand of the trader, not owned by him, so no tenth is taken from it.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of zakat on the earnings of a ma'dhun (authorized) slave. It begins by stating the general rule: the earnings of an authorized slave are not subject to zakat except when the slave is not in debt and the master is present (i.e., the slave is with the master). The commentary then elaborates: if the authorized slave is in debt (with an encompassing debt), nothing is taken; if not in debt, his earnings belong to his master. If the master is present (i.e., the slave is not traveling independently), zakat is taken; if not, no zakat. This is based on the principle that the slave's property is owned by the master, similar to his person. However, there is a disagreement within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the master owns the earnings of an authorized slave, while Abu Hanifa holds that the master does not own them. The practical effect is that if the master owns the earnings, zakat is due on them when conditions are met; if not, the earnings are not owned by the master, so no zakat. The passage also briefly mentions mudarabah, comparing it to the case of the authorized slave: in both, the capital is a trust, so no zakat is taken. This is a clear presentation of an intra-Hanafi dispute with practical consequences for zakat.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
مولاه (mawlah): The master (owner) of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only when the slave is in debt and the master is absent
D) Never, because the earnings belong to the slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that the master owns the earnings of a ma'dhun slave, making zakat wajib when the slave is debt-free, the master is present, and nisab is reached.

Definitions of Rikaz, Ma'din, and Kanz and the Ruling of Khums
Definition and ruling of Rikaz, Ma'din, Kanz with khums conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Rikaz, Ma'din, and Kanz, and apply the Hanafi ruling on khums to specific types of minerals and buried treasure.

Core Idea
Hanafi khums on rikaz applies only to solid meltable metals and pre-Islamic buried treasure.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya opens the chapter on Rikaz, a key subject in Zakat. It establishes precise definitions that determine the obligation of khums. Understanding these definitions is crucial for correctly applying the law, as misclassification can lead to incorrect payment of khums or treating treasure as luqata.

Guided Path
1
Define Rikaz and its subdivisions
Rikaz is property deposited in the earth. It comprises two types: Ma'din (created by Allah) and Kanz (placed by humans). Identify each term and its scope.
The definition is general; the commentary expands the meaning of 'deposited' to include both Allah and humans.
2
Categorize Ma'din into three types
Ma'din includes solid metals (gold, silver, iron), liquids (water, oil), and miscellaneous (pearls, turquoise). Only solid metals that melt by fire are subject to khums.
This categorization is from Jami' al-Rumuz and Durr al-Mukhtar.
3
Distinguish Kanz by owner type
Kanz from pre-Islamic disbelievers or non-Muslims without treaty is subject to khums. Kanz from Muslims or dhimmis is treated as luqata (lost property).
The finder must attempt to return luqata; khums is not due.
4
Verify conditions for khums on Ma'din
The condition for khums on Ma'din is that the mineral must be solid and meltable by fire. Liquids and non-meltable solids are excluded.
This is a distinct Hanafi condition; other schools may rule differently.
5
Confirm that finder's status is irrelevant
Khums is due regardless of the finder being Muslim, dhimmi, child, woman, or slave. The obligation attaches to the property itself.
As stated in al-Binaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الرِّكازُ : هو المالُ المركوز في الأرض مخلوقاً كان أو موضوعاً.
والمعدنُ : ما كان مخلوقاً.
والكَنْزُ : ما كان موضوعاً.
[1]قوله : المركوز ؛ أعم من أن يكون راكزه الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أو عبداً من عباده ؛ فلذا عمم وقال : مخلوقاً كان أو موضوعاً، والمراد بالمخلوق ما خلقه الله في الأرض، وهو الذي يقال له : المعدن ؛ بفتح الميم ، وكسر الدال المهملة وفتحها، بينهما عين مهملة ساكنة ، من عَدَنَ بمعنى : أقام ، وهو في المكان الذي يستقر فيه شيء، ثم غلبَ استعماله في نفس الأشياء المستقرة. والمراد بالموضوع ؛ الذي دفنَهُ واحدٌ من النَّاس، وهو الذي يقال له : الكنز، يقال : كَنَزَ كنزاً ؛ بفتح الكاف، جَمَعَه.
[٢] قوله : ما كان مخلوقاً ؛ هو على ثلاثة أقسام :
منطبع : كالذهب والفضة، والرصاص، والحديد.
ومائع : كالماء، والملح، والنفط، والقير.
وما ليس شيئاً منهما كاللؤلؤ والفيروزج، والزاج، والكحل، وغير ذلك، والذي يُخَمسُ إنما هو ما كان جامداً ينطبع بالنار لا غيره. كذا في «جامع الرموز»)، و«الدر المختار»، وغيرهما

Chapter of Rikaz. Rikaz: property deposited in the earth, whether created or placed. Metal (ma'din): what is created. Treasure (kanz): what is placed. [1] 'Deposited' is more general, whether its depositor is Allah or one of His servants; thus he generalized and said: created or placed. Intended by 'created' is what Allah created in the earth, and it is called 'ma'din'... Intended by 'placed' is what a person buried, and it is called 'kanz'. [2] 'What is created' is of three types: solid metals like gold, silver, lead, iron; liquid like water, salt, oil, tar; and neither like pearl, turquoise, vitriol, antimony, etc. That which is subject to khums is only what is solid and meltable by fire, not others. As stated in Jami' al-Rumuz, Durr al-Mukhtar, and others. [3] 'What is placed' is whether from disbelievers or Muslims, but only the former is subject to khums; the latter is treated as luqata (found property), as will come. [4] 'Found' is whether the finder is Muslim or dhimmi, child, woman, or slave. As in al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Rikaz (buried treasures and minerals). It provides definitions: Rikaz is wealth deposited in the earth, either created naturally (ma'din) or placed by humans (kanz). The commentary elaborates: ma'din is further divided into three categories: solid metals (like gold, silver), liquids (like water, oil), and neither (like pearls). The ruling on khums (one-fifth tax) applies only to solid metals that can be melted by fire, not to liquids or items like pearls. For placed wealth (kanz), the ruling depends on the owner: if from pre-Islamic disbelievers or non-Muslims with no treaty, it is subject to khums; if from Muslims or dhimmis, it is treated as luqata (lost property) to be returned or held as trust. The finder's status (Muslim, dhimmi, child, woman, slave) does not affect the obligation of khums; it is due on the found property regardless. This passage sets the foundational definitions and the scope of khums on rikaz.

Key Terms
الركاز (al-rikaz): Wealth deposited in the earth, whether created naturally or placed by humans.
المعدن (al-ma'din): What is created in the earth by Allah, i.e., natural minerals.
الكنز (al-kanz): What is placed in the earth by humans, i.e., buried treasure.

Key Takeaway
Khums on rikaz applies only to solid meltable metals (ma'din) and pre-Islamic buried treasure (kanz); all other finds are either luqata or tax-free.

Rikaz, Ma'din, and Kanz: Definitions and Hanafi Ruling on Khums
Defines rikaz, ma'din, and kanz with the Hanafi ruling on khums

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rikaz, ma'din, and kanz, and state which types are subject to khums according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Khums is wajib only on solid malleable minerals and on kanz of disbelievers.

The term 'rikaz' appears in the hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums,' but its exact scope is debated. In this lesson, we unpack the Hanafi classification of rikaz into ma'din (created minerals) and kanz (buried treasure), and learn which subtypes carry the obligation of khums. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying zakat law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general definition of rikaz
Rikaz is any wealth deposited in the earth, whether created by Allah or placed by humans. The commentator explains that 'makhluqan' refers to ma'din and 'mawdu'an' refers to kanz.
The word 'rikaz' comes from 'rakaza' meaning to fix or deposit.
2
Distinguish ma'din and kanz
Ma'din is what Allah created in the earth (minerals), while kanz is what humans buried (treasure). This distinction affects the ruling on khums.
Ma'din is further divided into three types: malleable, liquid, and other solids.
3
Classify ma'din and identify the khums rule
Ma'din is of three types: malleable (e.g., gold, silver, lead, iron), liquid (e.g., water, oil, salt), and other solids (e.g., pearl, turquoise). Khums is only due on solid minerals that are malleable by fire.
Liquid minerals and non-malleable solids are not subject to khums in the Hanafi school.
4
Note the ruling for kanz based on the owner
Kanz from disbelievers is subject to khums. Kanz from Muslims is treated as luqatah (lost property) and not as rikaz subject to khums.
The finder's status (Muslim, dhimmi, child, etc.) does not affect the obligation; only the type and origin matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الركاز

[1]
الرِّكازُ : هو المالُ المركوز في الأرض مخلوقاً كان أو موضوعاً.
والمعدنُ : ما كان مخلوقاً.
والكَنْزُ : ما كان موضوعاً.
هو مَعْدِنُ ذهب ونحوه وُجِدَ
[٤]
[1]قوله : المركوز ؛ أعم من أن يكون راكزه الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أو عبداً من عباده ؛ فلذا عمم وقال : مخلوقاً كان أو موضوعاً، والمراد بالمخلوق ما خلقه الله لا في الأرض، وهو الذي يقال له : المعدن ؛ بفتح الميم ، وكسر الدال المهملة وفتحها، بينهما عين مهملة ساكنة ، من عَدَنَ بمعنى : أقام ، وهو في المكان الذي يستقر فيه شيء، ثم غلبَ استعماله في نفس الأشياء المستقرة.
والمراد بالموضوع ؛ الذي دفنَهُ واحدٌ من النَّاس، وهو الذي يقال له : الكنز، يقال : كَنَزَ كنزاً ؛ بفتح الكاف، جَمَعَه.
[٢] قوله : ما كان مخلوقاً ؛ هو على ثلاثة أقسام :
منطبع : كالذهب والفضة، والرصاص، والحديد.
ومائع : كالماء، والملح، والنفط، والقير.
وما ليس شيئاً منهما كاللؤلؤ والفيروزج، والزاج، والكحل، وغير ذلك، والذي يُخَمسُ إنما هو ما كان جامداً ينطبع بالنار لا غيره. كذا في «جامع الرموز»، و«الدر المختار»، وغيرهما
(۳) قوله : ما كان موضوعاً ؛ سواء كان من الكفّار أو أهل الإسلام، لكن الذي يخمس هو الأول، والثاني في حكم اللقطة، كما سيأتي ذكره. 
[٤]قوله : وجد ؛ سواء كان الواجد مسلماً أو ذميّاً، صبياً أو امرأة أو عبداً. كذا في «البناية».
(۱) «جامع الرموز»(۱ : ۱۹۷).
(۲) «الدر المختار» (٢ : ٤٤).
(۳) (البناية» (۳ : ۱۳۸).
Chapter of Rikaz. It is a mineral of gold and the like that is found. [1] Rikaz is wealth deposited in the earth, whether created or placed. Ma'din is what is created. Kanz is what is placed. It is a mineral of gold and the like that is found. [4] [1] His statement: 'the deposited' is more general than its depositor being Allah or one of His servants; therefore he generalized and said: whether created or placed. By 'created' is meant what Allah created in the earth, and this is called ma'din; with fath of the mim, kasr of the dal and fath of it, with a silent 'ayn between them, from 'adana meaning he resided, and it is in the place where something settles, then its usage became dominant for the things themselves that settle. By 'placed' is meant what one of the people buried, and this is called kanz; it is said kanaza kanzan with fath of the kaf, meaning he collected it. [2] His statement: 'what is created' is of three types: malleable like gold, silver, lead, iron; liquid like water, salt, oil, tar; and what is neither like pearl, turquoise, vitriol, kohl, and other than that. That which is subject to khums is only what is solid and malleable by fire, not other than that. As in Jami' al-Rumuz and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. (3) His statement: 'what is placed' applies equally whether from disbelievers or Muslims, but that which is subject to khums is the first (disbelievers'), and the second (Muslims') is treated like luqatah, as will come. [4] His statement: 'found' applies whether the finder is Muslim or dhimmi, child, woman, or slave. As in al-Binaya. (1) Jami' al-Rumuz (1:197). (2) al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:44). (3) al-Binaya (3:138).
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of Rikaz from the matn. It begins by defining the term rikaz linguistically and legally. Rikaz is any wealth deposited in the earth, and it is of two types: ma'din (mineral deposits created by Allah) and kanz (buried treasure placed by humans). The commentator explains that the author of the matn used the word 'makhluqan' to mean what Allah created, i.e., ma'din, and 'mawdu'an' to mean what humans buried, i.e., kanz. The commentator then divides ma'din into three categories: metals that can be smelted (like gold, silver, lead, iron), liquids (like water, salt, oil, tar), and other solids (like pearls, turquoise). He states that khums (one-fifth) is only obligatory on solid minerals that can be smelted by fire, not on liquids or other non-malleable substances. For kanz (buried treasure), if it is from the wealth of disbelievers, it is subject to khums; if from Muslims, it is treated as luqatah (lost property). Finally, the finder of rikaz can be anyone: Muslim, dhimmi, child, woman, or slave; this is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الرِّكازُ (al-rikāz): Wealth deposited in the earth, whether created (ma'din) or placed (kanz).
المعدن (al-ma‘din): Mineral deposits created by Allah in the earth, e.g., gold, silver.
الكَنْزُ (al-kanz): Buried treasure placed by humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of ma'din is subject to khums?
A) All minerals, regardless of type
B) Only solid minerals that are malleable by fire
C) Only liquid minerals like oil
D) Only precious metals like gold and silver

Key Takeaway
Khums on rikaz is not universal; in the Hanafi school it is restricted to solid malleable minerals and treasure from disbelievers.
